Rational Judgments
beginner

The Three Rational Judgments: Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and defines each with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment that underpin all discussions in kalam.

Core Idea
Every theological question hinges on whether something is necessary, impossible, or possible.

Sanusi begins by establishing the foundational categories of rational judgment. Before we can reason about God or creation, we must know the three possible ways the mind can assess any proposition: necessary, impossible, or possible. This short passage lays the groundwork for every argument that follows in the study of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the concise style and the three terms: الوجوب, الاستحالة, الجواز.
The phrase 'الثلاثة مدار' may be a textual variant; focus on the definitions.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find how Sanusi defines each judgment: wajib as non-existence inconceivable, mustahil as existence inconceivable, ja'iz as both existence and non-existence conceivable.
Each definition is paired with an example: substance and space, substance without motion/rest, death of a person.
3
Reflect on the pivot
Consider why Sanusi says these three are the pivot (مدار) of all kalam discussions. Every theological question will involve deciding whether something is necessary, impossible, or possible regarding God or creation.
This connects the lesson to the broader purpose of studying kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ أقسام الحكم العقلي ]
وذلك أن تعلم أولاً : أنَّ الحكم العقلي منحصر في ثلاثة أقسام الثلاثة مدار مباحث علم الكلام كلها
: الوجوب ، والاستحالة ، والجواز ، وعلى هذهِ
فالواجبُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ؛ كالتحيز مثلاً للجوهر .
والمستحيلُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ؛ كعُرُو الجوهر عن الحركة والسكون .
والجائزُ : ما يصحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه ؛ كموتِ الواحد منا اليوم أو غداً
[Divisions of rational judgment] And that is that you should know first: that rational judgment is confined to three divisions, the three are the pivot of all topics of the science of kalam: necessity (wujub), impossibility (istihala), and possibility (jawaz). And upon these: the necessary (wajib) is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind, like occupation of space (tahayyuz) for example for a substance (jawhar). The impossible (mustahil) is that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind, like a substance being devoid of motion and rest. The possible (ja'iz) is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind, like the death of one of us today or tomorrow.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the three rational judgments. Sanusi states that rational judgments are confined to three types: necessity (wujub), impossibility (istihala), and possibility (jawaz). He then defines each. The necessary (wajib) is that whose non-existence is inconceivable to the mind; for example, a substance (jawhar) necessarily occupies space (tahayyuz). In kalam, substances always have location, so their occupation of space is necessary. The impossible (mustahil) is that whose existence is inconceivable; for example, a substance being devoid of both motion and rest is impossible because a substance must have either motion or rest. The possible (ja'iz) is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable; for example, the death of one of us today or tomorrow – it is neither necessary nor impossible, but possible. Sanusi emphasizes that these three categories are the pivot (madar) of all kalam discussions, meaning every theological question will involve determining whether something is necessary, impossible, or possible with respect to God or creation.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
وجوب
استحالة
جواز
تحيز
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three judgments apply to all propositions indiscriminately.
Correction: In kalam, these judgments specifically apply to rational judgments about existence and attributes, not to all propositions. For example, mathematical truths are necessary but not part of kalam's subject matter.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the definition of the possible (ja'iz)?
A) That whose non-existence is inconceivable
B) That whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable
C) That whose existence is inconceivable
D) That which is necessary for substances

Key Takeaway
Every rational proposition in kalam is either necessary, impossible, or possible, and these three categories are the foundation of all theological argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God that you have never questioned. Is it rationally necessary, impossible, or possible according to these categories? If it is only possible, are you willing to examine it more deeply, or are you treating it as necessary?
?
Your own death today is merely possible. Does that possibility change how you will spend the next hour? What would you do differently if you knew it were necessary?
?
Sanusi says these three judgments are the pivot of all kalam. Have you ever consciously sorted your theological questions into these three categories? What prevents you from doing so? Will you start now?

Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment in kalam and how they form the foundation of theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are three: necessary, impossible, and contingent.

Before diving into theological arguments, a kalam student must first grasp the basic categories of rational judgment. Imam Sanusi opens his discussion with a clear classification: every rational claim falls into one of three types. These three categories are the building blocks of all subsequent proofs about God and the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the three categories in sequence.
The passage is short but foundational.
2
Identify each category
Find the three terms: الواجب, المستحيل, الجائز. Note the definition and example for each.
The example for الواجب is about a substance occupying space.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: why does kalam begin with these three? How do they relate to discussions about Allah and creation?
These categories apply to God (necessary), His attributes (necessary or contingent), and the world (contingent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم العقلي
وذلك أن تعلم أولاً : أنَّ الحكم العقلي منحصر في ثلاثة أقسام هي مدار مباحث علم الكلام كلها : الوجوب ، والاستحالة ، والجواز ، وعلى هذه فالواجبُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ؛ كالتحيز مثلاً للجوهر . والمستحيلُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ؛ كعرو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون . والجائزُ : ما يصحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه ؛ كموتِ الواحد منا اليوم أو غداً
Sections of the rational judgment. That is, know first: the rational judgment is confined to three sections that are the orbit of all subjects of the science of kalam: al-wujub (obligatory), al-istihalah (impossibility), and al-jawaz (possibility). Upon these [divisions], al-wajib (the necessary) is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect; like occupying space, for example, for a substance. And al-mustahil (the impossible) is that whose existence is not conceivable in the intellect; like a body being devoid of movement and rest. And al-ja'iz (the contingent) is that whose existence and non-existence are both sound in the intellect; like the death of one of us today or tomorrow.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage classifies the rational judgments into three fundamental categories that underlie all discussions in 'ilm al-kalam. The author begins by stating that the rational judgment is limited to three types: obligation, impossibility, and possibility. These three are the central pivot of kalam topics.

1. The necessary (al-wajib): Defined as that whose non-existence is inconceivable by the intellect. An example given is the occupation of space (al-tahayyuz) for a substance. That is, it is rationally necessary that any substance occupies space; we cannot conceive a substance without spatial extension.

2. The impossible (al-mustahil): Defined as that whose existence is inconceivable by the intellect. The example is a body being devoid of both movement and rest. It is rationally impossible for a body to have neither motion nor rest; it must be either moving or at rest per se.

3. The contingent (al-ja'iz): Defined as that whose existence and non-existence are both rationally sound. The example is the death of a particular person today or tomorrow; both possibilities are rationally acceptable.

These three judgments are the basic categories used in kalam to describe God (wajib al-wujud), attributes of God (wajib), and the world (ja'iz, sometimes mustahil).

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'wajib' means legally obligatory (as in Islamic law), but here it means rationally necessary.
Correction: In kalam, 'wajib' refers to that which the intellect cannot conceive as non-existent, not to a legal obligation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which rational judgment applies to a body being devoid of both movement and rest?
A) al-wajib
B) al-mustahil
C) al-ja'iz
D) none of the above

Key Takeaway
Every rational claim in kalam fits into one of three categories: necessary, impossible, or contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that everything in your life—your health, your wealth, your very existence—is contingent, equally possible to be or not be. How does that change the way you hold onto your plans for tomorrow? Will you, here and now, surrender the illusion of control and admit you are nothing but a possibility sustained moment by moment?
?
The necessary is that which cannot not exist. You have spent your life clinging to things that can vanish. What would it look like for you to transfer your heart's 'necessity'—your non-negotiable dependence—from created things to the One who alone is wajib al-wujud?
?
You believe certain truths of faith, like the resurrection, are divinely revealed. But have you ever dismissed something as rationally impossible simply because you couldn't conceive it? What does it mean for you to accept that your intellect is bounded, and that what is 'impossible' is only what contradicts necessary rational truths—not what surpasses your imagination?

Definition and Proof of the Permissible in God's Acts
Sanusi defines the permissible regarding God and proves non-obligation of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the permissible (al-ja'iz) regarding God's actions and the proof that God is not obliged to observe benefit or what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
God is not obliged to create the best possible world; His actions are free and gracious.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces the third rational category—the permissible (al-ja'iz)—as it applies to God's own acts. He clarifies that nothing is obligatory upon God, and then provides a concise argument: if God were bound to create what is most beneficial, then trials, commands, and prohibitions would not exist. This sets the stage for understanding divine freedom and grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style: Sanusi packs both definition and proof into a single sentence.
2
Identify the definition of al-ja'iz
Find the phrase starting with 'وأما الجائز'. The definition is: every act of God such that nothing is obligatory upon Him, nor is He required to observe benefit or the most beneficial.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is a conditional: 'otherwise (if He were obliged) there would have occurred no trial... nor any command or prohibition.' Since trials and commands do exist, the obligation must be false.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhan al-tamanu').
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: it upholds God's transcendence and freedom. He is not compelled by any external consideration; His actions are pure grace.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must create the best (al-aslah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يجوز في حقه تعالى وبيان الدليل على عدم وجوب مراعاته تعالى الصلاح والأصلح وأن ما وقع من ذلك بمحض اختياره تعالى تفضلاً منه جل وعز وأما الجائز: فهو كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى، لا يجب عليه منه شيء، ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح، وإلا لما وقعت محنة دنيا ولا أخرى، ولا تكليف بأمر ولا نهي.
Chapter on what is permissible regarding Him, may He be exalted, and an exposition of the proof for the non-obligation of His observing benefit and what is most beneficial, and that what occurs of that is by the pure choice of Him, may He be exalted, as a grace from Him, may He be glorified and exalted. As for the permissible: it is every act from His acts, may He be exalted, nothing of which is obligatory upon Him, nor the observance of benefit nor what is most beneficial; otherwise there would have occurred neither trial in this world nor the next, nor any command or prohibition.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'al-ja'iz' (the permissible) with regards to God's actions. Sanusi begins by stating that he is explaining the proof that God is not obliged to observe what is beneficial (salah) or most beneficial (aslah) for creatures. He then defines the permissible: every act of God such that nothing is obligatory upon Him, and He is not required to observe benefit. He then gives a proof: if God were obliged to create the best or most beneficial, then trials (mihan) in this world and the next would not occur, nor would there be commands or prohibitions, because such trials and commands clearly involve hardship, which would contradict the obligation to create only what is best. Hence the obligation is impossible, and God's actions are purely by His choice and grace.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
الصلاح والأصلح (al-salah wa al-aslah)
المحنة (al-mihnah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is obliged to create the best possible world (al-aslah).
Correction: Sanusi argues that if God were obliged, trials and commands (which involve hardship) would not exist. Since they do exist, God cannot be under such obligation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would be the consequence if God were obliged to observe benefit (salah) or what is most beneficial (aslah)?
A) There would be no trials in this world or the next.
B) All people would enter Paradise.
C) God would be compelled to create only good.
D) There would be no need for revelation.

Key Takeaway
The existence of trials and obligations proves that God is not compelled to benefit His creatures; His actions are purely gracious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though God owes you a comfortable life? Examine your reaction when trials come. Do you complain as if God has wronged you? This lesson teaches that God is not obliged to give you benefit. How will you change your response to hardship starting today?
?
Think of a command or prohibition you find difficult. This lesson says such commands exist precisely because God is not obliged to smooth your path. Will you obey out of humility, recognizing that God's grace is pure gift, not obligation? Or will you resent the difficulty?
?
If you have ever thought, 'God should make this easier for me because I am trying to be good,' you have assumed an obligation on God. What false entitlement will you repent of today, and how will you instead receive trials as part of His perfect wisdom?

The Syllogistic Proof of Shukr
Sanusi answers an objection by producing a meaningful inference from the three modes of gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a syllogism in the first figure to prove that using the heart, tongue, and limbs for gratitude is linguistically called shukr.

Core Idea
Shukr is not just a word; it's a logical conclusion from evidence.

Sanusi has established that shukr (gratitude) encompasses three modes: heart, tongue, and limbs. Now he answers an objection by demonstrating that calling these three actions 'shukr' is not arbitrary but follows from a valid syllogism. This passage presents a subtle logical inference that every student of kalam should memorize, as it grounds a linguistic claim in rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice that the author is giving an answer (يجاب) to an objection, and that he calls the inference 'meaningful' (معنوي) and 'subtle' (ظريف).
The passage is short but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the syllogism's parts
Break the argument into its premises and conclusion. The author mentions two premises: (1) every customary recompense for a blessing is linguistically shukr, and (2) using the three (heart, tongue, limbs) is a customary recompense. The conclusion is: using the three is linguistically shukr.
This structure is known as the 'first figure' of a syllogism.
3
Reflect on why it is 'subtle'
The author calls this inference 'subtle' (ظريف) and urges memorization. Reflect on why it is clever: it transforms a linguistic observation into a logical necessity. Understanding this helps appreciate how kalam uses logic to defend doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يُجابُ : بأنَّ فيهِ استدلالاً معنوياً ؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ استعمل الثلاثة جزاءً للنعمة ، وكلُّ جزاء للنعمةِ عُرْفاً فهو شكر لغةٌ ، فينتجُ مِنَ الشكل الأوَّلِ : استعمالُ الثلاثة شكر لغةً ، وهو ظريف ، فاحفظه
And it may be answered: that in it there is a meaningful inference, in that he used the three (heart, tongue, limbs) as recompense for the blessing, and every recompense for a blessing customarily is linguistically shukr, so it results from the first figure: the use of the three is linguistically shukr, and it is subtle, so memorize it.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents an answer to an objection, and is thus a refutation. The author shows that the claim that using the heart, tongue, and limbs (the three) for gratitude is linguistically called shukr, is not arbitrary but based on a meaningful inference. The reasoning is syllogistic: every customary recompense for a blessing is linguistically shukr; using the three is a customary recompense; therefore, using the three is linguistically shukr. This is a valid syllogism in the first figure. The author calls it subtle and urges the reader to memorize it.

Key Terms
استدلال معنوي
جزاء النعمة
شكر
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shukr is defined by these three actions (heart, tongue, limbs) and that's just a matter of linguistic convention.
Correction: The passage shows that shukr being applied to these three actions is not arbitrary but follows from a logical syllogism: because they are a customary recompense for blessings, and such recompense is linguistically called shukr.

Checkpoint
What type of inference does the author use to prove that using the heart, tongue, and limbs for gratitude is linguistically shukr?
A) A categorical definition
B) A syllogism in the first figure
C) An appeal to authority
D) A direct linguistic analysis

Key Takeaway
The threefold gratitude is linguistically shukr because of a valid syllogistic argument, not by arbitrary definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing, do you consciously engage your heart, tongue, and limbs as a recompense, or do you merely say 'alhamdulillah' without deeper reflection? What would change if you treated gratitude as a logical duty derived from evidence?
?
The author calls this proof 'subtle' and commands you to memorize it. Have you ever considered that your gratitude must be a comprehensive act involving all three aspects? Where is your own practice lacking, and what concrete step will you take today to make your shukr complete?
?
If shukr is linguistically defined as recompense for blessings, does your daily life reflect that you truly believe blessings require a response? Or do you take them for granted? Identify one blessing you received today and plan how you will use heart, tongue, and limbs in gratitude for it.

Disbelief Lacks Proof, Faith Has Insight
Argues that kufr lacks proof while iman is based on knowledge and insight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why disbelief lacks rational proof and how faith is grounded in evidence and insight.

Core Idea
Disbelief is imitation without proof; faith is knowledge with insight.

In this passage, the author makes a powerful argument that disbelief (kufr) has no rational basis—it is mere imitation. He contrasts this with faith (iman), which is founded on knowledge and clear evidence. This distinction is crucial for understanding why Islam demands intellectual conviction, not blind acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the strong claims and Quranic citations.
2
Understand the core claim
Identify the statement: 'disbelief has no proof; it comes from imitation.' Ask yourself why this matters for the author's argument.
3
Examine the logical reductio
Reflect on the conditional: if faith were also without proof, then faith and disbelief would be equal. This shows the necessity of proof for faith.
The author uses this to highlight the difference.
4
Study the Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses: 23:117 shows that shirk lacks proof; 12:108 shows that the Prophet's call is based on insight.
The second verse defines insight as knowledge and evidence.
5
Conclude with the believer's state
The passage ends by affirming that a believer has knowledge and insight about his religion. This contrasts with the blind imitation of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي كلمة الكفر ، لا ثبوت لها في القلب ؛ أي : لا علم لصاحبها بحقيقتها ؛ إذ الكفرُ لا دليل عليهِ وإنَّما يحصل عن تقليد مِنْ غيرِ برهان ، ﴿ وَمَن يَدْعُ مَعَ اللَّهِ إِلَنهَا آخَرَ لَا بُرْهَانَ لَم بِهِ (۳) [المؤمنون : ۱۱۷] ، فالكفر لا برهان عليه ، فلو كانَ الإيمانُ أيضاً كذلك لكانا في حكم التسوية في عدم البرهان واحداً ! والقرآن العظيم شاهدٌ بأنَّ دينَ اللهِ أهلُه على علم وبصيرةٍ مِنْ أمرِه ، قال تعالى : ﴿ قُلْ هَذِهِ سَبِيلِي أَدْعُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ عَلَى بَصِيرَةٍ أَنَا وَمَنِ اتَّبَعَنِي ) يوسف : ۱۰۸] ، فقوله تعالى : ﴿ عَلَى بَصِيرَة ) أي : على علم ودلالة دونَ أخذ بالظنون في طُرُقِ التقليدِ ؛ فالمؤمنُ على علم بدينه ، وبصيرة
And it is the word of disbelief; it has no firmness in the heart, meaning: its possessor has no knowledge of its reality, because disbelief has no proof; rather it occurs from imitation (taqlid) without proof. 'And whoever invokes besides Allah another god for which he has no proof' [Al-Mu'minun 23:117]. So disbelief has no proof upon it. If faith also were likewise, then they would be equal in the judgment of lack of proof! And the great Quran is a witness that the people of Allah's religion are upon knowledge and insight (basira) of their affair. The Most High said: 'Say: This is my way; I invite to Allah with insight, I and those who follow me' [Yusuf 12:108]. So His saying 'upon insight' means upon knowledge and evidence, without taking conjectures in the ways of imitation. So the believer is upon knowledge of his religion and insight.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument for the necessity of proof in faith. The author asserts that disbelief (kufr) has no proof (burhan) and is based solely on blind imitation (taqlid). He quotes Quran 23:117 to support that associating partners with Allah lacks proof. Then he makes a logical point: if faith were also without proof, then faith and disbelief would be equivalent in lacking proof, which is absurd. The Quran itself testifies that the people of Allah's religion act upon knowledge and insight (basira). Quran 12:108 is cited to show that the Prophet's call is based on insight, meaning knowledge and clear evidence, not conjecture or imitation. Therefore, a true believer possesses knowledge and insight concerning his religion.

Key Terms
كفر
برهان
تقليد
بصيرة
علم
دين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is a blind leap without evidence
Correction: This passage shows that disbelief lacks proof while faith is based on knowledge and insight.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the basis of disbelief (kufr)?
A) It is based on rational arguments.
B) It is based on imitation (taqlid) without proof.
C) It is based on cultural tradition.
D) It is based on personal experience.

Key Takeaway
Faith is built on knowledge and insight, not blind imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have clear proof for your faith, or are you simply following what you were raised with? If your faith were challenged today, could you defend it with insight (basira) or would you fall back on imitation? What is one proof you will memorize and internalize this week?
?
The disbeliever has no proof; the believer has insight. When you face doubt, do you actively seek knowledge and evidence, or do you rely on vague feelings? What step will you take today to move from imitation to certainty?
?
If faith were without proof, it would be equal to disbelief—that is rationally unacceptable. Do you live with the confidence that your faith is rationally superior? Or secretly doubt it? What will you do to strengthen your conviction so that your heart is firm, unlike the word of disbelief?

Sound vs. Unsound Rational Investigation
Sanusi defines sound and unsound nazar with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between sound and unsound rational investigation (nazar) and how the rational connection between evidence and conclusion determines validity.

Core Idea
Sound nazar reveals the rational bond; unsound nazar misses it.

Sanusi distinguishes between two types of rational investigation: one that leads to knowledge and one that does not. He provides a concrete example involving reflection on the world to establish its createdness. This lesson will clarify the key concept of the 'rational bond' (rabṭ 'aqlī) that makes an investigation sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's own phrasing.
Notice the compact style; key terms are defined concisely.
2
Identify the definitions
Find the definitions of sound and unsound nazar. Note that sound nazar requires revealing the aspect of rational connection between evidence and conclusion.
The rational bond is what makes the investigation productive.
3
Analyze the example
The example contrasts two outcomes of reflecting on the world. See why the first is unsound (no necessary connection) and the second is sound (recognizes necessary connection to temporal attributes).
The key is the concept of 'mulāzama' (necessary connection).
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this distinction matters: without the right connection, investigation leads nowhere; with it, knowledge is attained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظرُ الصحيح الموصل للمعرفة والرابط بينهما ]
قوله : ( إلى النظرِ الصحيح ) يعني بالنظر الصحيح : التأمل الذي يُطْلعُ صاحبَهُ على الوجه الذي بينه وبين المدلول ربط عقلي (٢)
والنظرُ الفاسد : هو التأمُّلُ الذي لا يُطْلعُ صاحبَهُ على ذلكَ
مثال ذلكَ (۳) : التأملُ في العالَمِ بقصْدِ التوصل إلى معرفة حدوثه :
فتارةً : يكونُ غاية ما يصل إليهِ الناظرُ بتأمُّلِهِ : أنَّ العالَمَ أجرام موجودة تتصف بصفاتٍ ، ويعمى عما وراءَ ذلكَ ، فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ نظر هذا فاسد لا يُوصِّلُهُ إلى شيءٍ ؛ إذ لا ملازمة بين كون الشيء موجوداً يتّصفُ بصفاتٍ وبين كونه قديماً أو حادثاً
وتارةً : ينشرح صدره لأن يعرف أنَّهُ أجرام ملازمة لصفات حادثة

الهوامش:
(۲) يعني : بأن يكون النظر في الدليل من الجهة التي من شأنها أن ينتقل الذهن بها إلى ذلك المطلوب ، وتسمَّى جهة الدليل ، ووجهه ، وجهة الدلالة ، ووجهها . « صفاقسي » ( ١/ ٥٨ ) .
(۳) يعني : النظرين الصحيح والفاسد ، وسيبدأ بالتمثيل للفاسد
Sound investigation that leads to knowledge and the connection between them ]
His saying: 'to sound investigation' means by sound investigation: reflection that acquaints its possessor with the aspect through which there is a rational bond between it and the indicated [conclusion] (2).
And unsound investigation: is reflection that does not acquaint its possessor with that.
An example of that (3): reflection on the world with the intention of arriving at knowledge of its temporality:
Sometimes: the utmost that the investigator reaches by his reflection is that the world is existing bodies qualified with attributes, and he is blind to what is beyond that; so it is no secret that the investigation of this [person] is unsound, not leading him to anything; for there is no necessary connection between something being existing qualified with attributes and its being eternal or temporal.
And sometimes: his breast expands that he knows that it is bodies that are necessarily connected to temporal attributes.

Footnotes:
(2) Means: that the investigation is of the evidence from the perspective that the mind is accustomed to transfer from to that sought object; it is called the aspect of the evidence, its face, the aspect of indication, and its face. 'Safaqsi' (1/58).
(3) Means: the two investigations, sound and unsound, and he begins with the example of the unsound.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between sound and unsound rational investigation (nazar). Sound nazar is defined as reflection that reveals to the investigator the specific aspect or 'face' (wajh) through which a rational connection (rabṭ 'aqlī) exists between the evidence and the conclusion. Unsound nazar fails to reveal that connection. The author then provides an example: reflecting on the world to know that it is originated (ḥādith). In the unsound case, the investigator only sees the world as existing bodies with attributes, without recognizing the necessary connection (mulāzama) between those attributes and temporality; thus the conclusion is not reached. In the sound case, the investigator recognizes that the world consists of bodies that are necessarily connected to temporal attributes (such as motion and rest), leading to the knowledge of its origin. The footnotes clarify that the 'aspect' is the direction from which the mind moves from evidence to conclusion, and that the example begins with the unsound type.

Key Terms
نظر صحيح
نظر فاسد
ربط عقلي
جهة الدليل
ملازمة
تأمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any reflection on the world qualifies as sound nazar, because it involves thought.
Correction: Sound nazar requires identifying the specific rational connection between the evidence and the intended conclusion; mere observation without discovering that bond is insufficient.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes sound investigation from unsound investigation according to Sanusi?
A) Sound investigation leads to pleasure, unsound leads to confusion
B) Sound investigation reveals a rational connection between evidence and conclusion, unsound does not
C) Sound investigation is done by scholars, unsound by laymen
D) Sound investigation uses clear language, unsound uses ambiguous terms

Key Takeaway
Sound rational investigation depends on identifying the specific rational connection between the evidence and the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the world around you, do you merely observe it casually, or do you actively seek the rational connection that points to its Creator? What habit can you adopt to train your mind to see the mulāzama?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious claim based on mere association (e.g., 'the world is complex, so it must have a creator') without discerning the necessary logical link? How would you re-examine that belief in light of Sanusi's distinction between sound and unsound nazar?
?
If your investigation of the world fails to lead you to certainty about its temporality, what does that reveal about the quality of your nazar? What step of the proof are you neglecting, and how will you correct it starting today?

Mu'tazila Doctrine of Tawallud (Generation) of Knowledge from Reasoning
Mu'tazila explain how knowledge is generated from reasoning by human power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili concept of tawallud and their claim that knowledge is generated from reasoning through human power.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued that reasoning generates knowledge through human power, not divine direct action.

The Mu'tazila school defended human agency in producing knowledge from reasoning. They introduced the concept of tawallud (generation) to explain how the servant's power, created by Allah, produces both the reasoning and the resulting knowledge. This passage presents their core argument and definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and the English translation. Notice the key terms and how they build the argument.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the contrast between direct and indirect generation.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'tawallud', 'qudra haditha', and 'ikhtira'. Understand their definitions within the passage.
These terms are central to the Mu'tazili position.
3
Understand the mechanism of generation
Note that reasoning is invented directly by the servant, while knowledge is generated indirectly through the instrument of reasoning.
This distinction is crucial to their doctrine.
4
Reflect on the divine and human roles
According to the passage, Allah only creates the power to reason. The servant then invents both reasoning and knowledge using that power.
This contrasts with Sunni views that attribute all acts to Allah's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فقالوا : إنَّ العلمَ مُتولّد عنِ النظرِ ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّ قدرة الناظرِ أثَرَتْ في العلم بواسطة تأثيرها في النظر الموصل إليه ، وحقيقة التولُّدِ عندَهم : وجود حادث عن مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة ، فغاية ما خلق الله تعالى للعبدِ عندَهم القدرة على النظرِ ، أمَّا نفس النظرِ والعلم الحاصل بعدَهُ . . فالعبد عندَهم هو الذي اخترعهما بتلك القدرةِ التي خلق الله تعالى له ، إلا أنَّهُ اخترعَ النظر بلا واسطة ، والعلم بواسطة آلة النظر .
And the Mu'tazila said: Indeed knowledge is generated (mutawallid) from reasoning; meaning that the power of the thinker affects knowledge by means of its effect on the reasoning that leads to it. And the reality of generation (tawallud) according to them is: the existence of a contingent (hadith) from something possible by means of the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha). So the utmost that Allah created for the servant according to them is power over reasoning. As for reasoning itself and the knowledge that occurs after it – the servant according to them is the one who invents (ikhtara'a) them both by that power which Allah created for him, except that he invented reasoning without mediation, and knowledge by means of the instrument of reasoning.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazili position on how knowledge arises from reasoning. They hold that knowledge is 'generated' (mutawallid) from reasoning, meaning that the servant's acquired power (qudra haditha) brings about knowledge indirectly by first acting upon reasoning. The term 'tawallud' is defined: the occurrence of a contingent effect from something that is within one's power by means of the originated power. According to them, Allah only creates the power to reason; then the servant himself invents (ikhtara'a) the act of reasoning directly and the resulting knowledge indirectly using that power. Thus, they affirm human agency in producing both the reasoning and the knowledge, while limiting divine creation to the initial capacity.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
قدرة حادثة (originated power)
اختراع (invention)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawallud means knowledge automatically follows reasoning without human agency.
Correction: The Mu'tazila require the servant's active invention (ikhtira') using created power; it is not automatic.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, who invents (ikhtara'a) the reasoning?
A) Allah directly
B) The servant using created power
C) The philosophers
D) No one

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila affirm that knowledge is generated from reasoning by the servant's invented act, not directly by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in deep thought or study to arrive at a truth, do you credit your own intellect as the source, or do you recognize that even your ability to reason is a gift from Allah? How can this awareness change your humility?
?
The Mu'tazila say you 'invent' knowledge by your power. Is there any area of knowledge you are proud of? Does that pride stem from forgetting that the power itself was created by Allah? What would it mean to surrender that pride?
?
Imagine a moment when you felt you discovered a profound truth through your own reasoning. Now consider: that reasoning power was given by Allah, and the resulting knowledge was generated by your act. How does this reshape your gratitude? Will you thank Allah not just for the knowledge, but for the ability to reason itself?

Why Nazar is a Primary Obligation
Refutes the claim that nazar is only a subsidiary obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational inquiry (nazar) is a primary obligation (wajib asli) and not merely subsidiary, based on Sanusi's refutation of al-Sharif.

Core Idea
Nazar is primary because faith cannot be attained without it.

In this brief but powerful passage, Sanusi takes on a claim from al-Sharif al-Jurjani that neglecting nazar only results in sin, implying it is a subsidiary obligation like prayer. He dismantles this view by showing it lacks evidence and is circular reasoning. The truth, he asserts, is that nazar is the very means to faith, and what is indispensable for a primary duty (faith) is itself primary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a refutation, and a positive assertion.
2
Identify al-Sharif's position and his evidence
Al-Sharif claimed that for someone capable of nazar, neglecting it only results in sin (i.e., it's not a primary obligation). The evidence offered is that nazar is like prayer—a subsidiary obligation.
The passage calls this evidence a 'petitio principii' (مصادرة).
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi rejects both the claim (no evidence) and the evidence (circular). Then he gives the correct view: nazar is a means to faith (knowledge or inner assent), and without it faith cannot be attained, so it is a primary obligation.
This establishes nazar as wajib asli, not far'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما اقتصر عليه الشريف من المعصية فقط في حق المتمكن من النظر.. فدعوى منه لا دليل عليها، وما يمكن أن يستدل له بأن النظر واجب فرعي كالصلاة ونحوها.. فمصادرة لا تصح، بل الحق: أنه وسيلة إلى الإيمان الذي هو المعرفة، أو حديث النفس التابع لها، وما لا يحصل الإيمان إلا به فهو واجب أصلي كالإيمان.
And as for what al-Sharif restricted—that [only] sin [occurs] for the one capable of inquiry... that is a claim from him with no evidence for it. And what can be adduced in support of him—that inquiry is a subsidiary obligation like prayer and so on—is a petitio principii that is invalid. Rather, the truth is that it is a means to faith, which is knowledge (al-ma‘rifah), or the inner speech that follows it, and that without which faith is not attained is a primary obligation like faith itself.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a position held by 'al-Sharif' (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani or similar) who argued that for someone capable of rational inquiry (nazar), neglecting it only results in sin, implying it is a subsidiary (far‘i) obligation. The author refutes: first, that claim has no evidence. Second, the argument that nazar is subsidiary like prayer is question-begging (musadara), because the premise itself is disputed. The correct view: nazar is the means to faith, which is knowledge (ma‘rifah) or the inner assent that accompanies it. Since faith cannot be attained without it, it is a primary (asli) obligation, just as faith itself is primary. This establishes nazar as a fundamental duty.

Key Terms
النظر
الإيمان
المعرفة
واجب أصلي
واجب فرعي
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar is optional or merely recommended; neglecting it is only a minor sin.
Correction: Nazar is a primary obligation (wajib asli) for those capable, because faith cannot be attained without it, as stated in the passage.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that nazar is a subsidiary obligation?
A) Because it is impossible.
B) Because it is a means to faith, which is primary.
C) Because the claim contradicts consensus.
D) Because al-Sharif was a heretic.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is a primary obligation because it is the essential means to faith, and what is necessary for a primary duty is itself primary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have faith, but have you ever engaged in nazar—deliberate, personal inquiry into the proofs for God's existence—or do you rely solely on what others say? If nazar is the only path to true faith, what does your neglect of it say about the reality of your own iman?
?
Consider the statement: 'Whatever without which faith is not attained is a primary obligation.' If you have not actively pursued nazar, is your faith truly yours or just inherited belief? What specific step will you take today to begin that inquiry?
?
Al-Sharif's position may seem convenient—it reduces your responsibility to think deeply. But the truth is that nazar is not optional; it is as essential as faith itself. Are you willing to abandon the comfort of blind acceptance and take on the duty of investigation, even if it challenges your current beliefs? What cost are you willing to pay?

The Circularity Argument Against Taqlid
Sanusi refutes the claim that taqlid is valid from the hadith of faith by demonstrating a circular dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid (imitative belief) cannot be a valid basis for the fundamental articles of faith, because it leads to a logical circularity that prevents knowledge from being established.

Core Idea
Taqlid in faith creates a vicious circle: you need to know God before you can trust the Prophet.

Some argue that the hadith defining faith by certain traits supports the validity of taqlid. However, upon closer examination, this argument collapses into a logical fallacy. Imam Sanusi shows that if we rely on taqlid for the truths of faith, we would have to imitate the Prophet himself—but imitating the Prophet presupposes that we have already proven his prophethood, which itself depends on knowing God and His attributes. This creates a circular dependency, proving that taqlid is impossible for fundamental beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the hadith of faith validates taqlid. Learn what that claim is.
Objection: The Prophet defined faith by certain traits, so one can simply accept them by imitation.
2
See the first problem
Sanusi calls the understanding extremely weak. He then provides a logical demonstration of its weakness.
3
Trace the chain of dependencies
Follow Sanusi's argument: if taqlid were valid, the highest authority would be the Prophet. Imitating him requires proving his prophethood.
4
Identify the circularity
Prophethood depends on miracles, which depend on knowing God and His attributes. But these are part of the very traits of faith we are trying to know. Thus, circularity arises.
This shows the thing depending on itself.
5
Conclude the invalidity of taqlid
Therefore, taqlid in these matters is impossible. Only correct reflection (nazar) can break the circle.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا فهم صحَةِ التقليدِ مِنْ هذا الحديثِ فلا يخفى أَنَّهُ فهم ركيك في غاية ، ويدلُّكَ على غاية ركاكتِهِ زيادةً على ما سبق : أنَّ التقليد في هذه الخصال التي فُسِّرَ بها الإيمانُ باعتبار ثبوتِ مدلولها وصحته أعلى مَنْ يُقلِّدُ في ثبوتها - لو صح التقليد - النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وسائر الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام ، ولا شك أنَّ تقليدهم فيها فرعُ ثبوت رسالتهم ، وهي لا تثبتُ إلا بالمعجزة المتوقفِ معرفةُ دلالتِها على معرفة وجودِهِ تعالى ومعرفة صفاته التي يدخل فيها معرفةُ القَدَرِ فإذاً ؛ لو توقَّفَتْ معرفةُ خصالِ الإيمانِ ـ مِنْ حيثُ ثبوتُها ، لا مِنْ حيثُ إنَّها تفسير للإيمانِ الشرعي - على قول الرسولِ .. لَلزِمَ الدورُ وتوقف الشيء على نفسه .
As for the understanding of the validity of taqlid (imitative belief) from this hadith, it is not hidden that it is an extremely weak understanding. And it indicates to you the extreme weakness, in addition to what preceded, that taqlid in these traits by which faith is interpreted, in terms of the proof and validity of their referents – the highest one that one would imitate in their proof, if taqlid were valid – is the Prophet ﷺ and all the messengers, peace be upon them. And there is no doubt that taqlid of them in these traits is a branch of the proof of their message, and their message is not proven except by the miracle, the knowledge of its indication being dependent upon knowledge of His existence, Most High, and knowledge of His attributes, which includes knowledge of al-qadar. Therefore, if knowledge of the traits of faith – in terms of their proof, not in terms of them being a legal interpretation of faith – were dependent upon the statement of the messenger, then circularity would be necessary and the thing would be dependent upon itself.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection: someone might claim that the hadith about faith being defined by certain traits supports the validity of taqlid (imitative belief) in the fundamental articles of faith. The author calls this understanding extremely weak. He provides a logical proof: even if taqlid were valid, the most authoritative person to imitate would be the Prophet and the messengers. But imitating them depends on first proving their prophethood. Prophethood, in turn, is proven only by a miracle, and recognizing the miracle as evidence requires prior knowledge of the existence of God and His attributes – which includes the attribute of qadar (divine decree). Thus, if knowledge of the traits of faith – considered as proven facts, not merely as a legal definition – were to rely on the Prophet's statement, we would need to first know his prophethood, which requires knowledge of God and His attributes, which themselves are part of the very traits of faith. This creates a vicious circle (al-dawr): the thing would depend on itself. Therefore, taqlid in these matters is impossible; only correct reflection (nazar) can break the circle.

Key Terms
التقليد
الدور
الرسالة
المعجزة
صفات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that taqlid (imitative belief) is valid for fundamental articles of faith because the Prophet commanded obedience to his teachings.
Correction: This argument is circular: accepting the Prophet's authority already requires prior knowledge of God and His attributes, which are part of the very articles of faith one seeks to establish. Therefore, taqlid cannot be a sound basis for belief.

Checkpoint
What follows if knowledge of the traits of faith depends on the Prophet's statement?
A) It becomes easier for believers.
B) Circularity would occur.
C) It proves the validity of taqlid.
D) It makes faith impossible.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in the fundamentals of faith is logically impossible because it leads to a circular dependency that cannot be resolved without independent rational reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because you were raised in a Muslim home, without ever independently verifying their rational proof? How does Sanusi's argument challenge the comfort of that position, and what step will you take today to examine at least one of those beliefs critically?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you instinctively search for a hadith or a scholar's opinion to resolve it, or do you first establish the foundational proofs that make those sources trustworthy? If the former, how can you avoid the circularity Sanusi exposes, and what will you change starting this week?
?
Sanusi proves that even the Prophet's own statements cannot be the starting point for knowing the basic facts of faith. Does your heart truly accept that you cannot rely on blind imitation for your certainty? Will you commit today to learning one rational proof for God's existence — not for debate, but for your own soul's anchor?

The Three Rational Judgments: Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and their centrality to kalām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) and why they are the foundational principles of kalam.

Core Idea
All kalam arguments hinge on three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible.

Before diving into proofs about Allah and creation, Sanusi insists we master the basic conceptual tools. Here he lays out the three rational judgments that underpin every discussion in kalam. Understanding these definitions is the first step to sound theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Sanusi first announces the three categories, then defines each with an example, then explains their importance.
The examples are crucial—they ground abstract definitions in concrete physical reality.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three terms: wājib, mustaḥīl, jāʾiz. Note their definitions: wājib is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived; mustaḥīl is that whose existence cannot be conceived; jāʾiz is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable.
Contrast 'cannot be conceived' with 'cannot happen physically'—this is a rational, not empirical, judgment.
3
Study the examples
Sanusi gives examples: for wājib, a substance occupying space; for mustaḥīl, a substance devoid of motion and rest; for jāʾiz, a person's death today or tomorrow. Explain why each example fits its category.
These examples use the physical world to clarify abstract concepts.
4
Understand why these are mabādiʾ
Sanusi states that these three judgments are principles of kalam because the mutakallim's goal is to affirm or negate one of them, or affirm something branching from them. Reflect on how every theological claim involves one of these judgments.
For example, 'Allah exists' is wājib; 'Allah is unjust' is mustaḥīl; 'I will be saved' is jāʾiz.
5
Avoid the common misconception
The common error is to confuse 'necessary' (wājib) with 'always physically inevitable.' But Sanusi defines it intellectually: what cannot be rationally denied. Note the difference.
For example, the laws of physics are not wājib in this sense—they could be otherwise rationally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم العقلي، وبيان حدودها وذلك أن تعلم أولاً : أنَّ الحكم العقليَّ منحصر في ثلاثة أقسام : الوجوب ، والاستحالة ، والجواز ، وعلى هذه الثلاثةِ مدار مباحث علم الكلام كلها فالواجبُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه ؛ كالتحيز مثلاً للجوهر . والمستحيل : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ؛ كعرو الجوهر عن الحركة والسكون . والجائزُ : ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه ؛ كموتِ الواحدِ منَّا اليوم أو غداً لا شك أنَّ تصوُّرَ هذهِ الأحكام الثلاثة ومعرفة حقائقها مِنْ مبادئ علمِ الكلامِ ؛ إذْ غرضُ المُتكلّم منحصرٌ في هذهِ الثلاثةِ ؛ بأَنْ يثبت شيئاً منها ، أو ينفيه ، أو يثبت ما يتفرَّعُ عن ذلكَ ، فمَنْ لم يعرفُ حقائق هذه الأحكام الثلاثة لم يفهم ما ثبت منها في هذا العلم وما نُفِيَ .
Divisions of the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) and explanation of its limits. That is, you should know first: that the rational judgment is confined to three divisions: obligation (al-wujūb), impossibility (al-istiḥāla), and possibility (al-jawāz). And upon these three revolve all the investigations of the science of kalām. So the necessary (al-wājib) is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived in the intellect; like occupying space (al-taḥayyuz) for example for the atom (al-jawhar). And the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) is that whose existence cannot be conceived in the intellect; like the atom being devoid of motion and rest. And the possible (al-jāʾiz) is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the intellect; like the death of one of us today or tomorrow. There is no doubt that forming a concept of these three judgments and knowing their realities are among the principles (mabādiʾ) of the science of kalām; for the goal of the mutakallim is confined to these three: by affirming something of them, or negating it, or affirming what branches off from that. So whoever does not know the realities of these three judgments does not understand what has been affirmed in this science and what has been negated.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a clear classification and definition of the three rational judgments that form the foundation of kalām. Sanusi begins by stating that the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) is confined to three divisions: wujūb (necessity or obligation), istiḥāla (impossibility), and jawāz (possibility). He asserts that all discussions in kalām revolve around these three. He then defines each one with an example from the physical world: 1) al-wājib (the necessary) is that whose non-existence cannot be conceived; example: the attribute of occupying space (taḥayyuz) for a substance (jawhar) – i.e., every substance must occupy space, it cannot be conceived otherwise. 2) al-mustaḥīl (the impossible) is that whose existence cannot be conceived; example: a substance being completely devoid of both motion and rest – since a substance must always be in one of these states, it is impossible to conceive it without either. 3) al-jāʾiz (the possible) is that whose existence and non-existence are both intellectually valid; example: the death of a particular person today or tomorrow – it is neither necessary nor impossible. Sanusi then emphasizes that understanding these three judgments is a fundamental principle (mabdaʾ) of kalām because the mutakallim's entire objective is either to affirm or negate one of these judgments, or to affirm something that derives from them. Thus, anyone ignorant of these realities cannot grasp what is affirmed or negated in kalām.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
وجوب
استحالة
جواز
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
مبادئ علم الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing 'necessary' (wājib) with 'always physically inevitable'.
Correction: Sanusi defines wājib as 'that whose non-existence cannot be conceived in the intellect'. It is a rational necessity, not physical inevitability. For example, physical laws could be otherwise; rational necessities cannot.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the definition of al-wājib (the necessary)?
A) That whose existence always occurs
B) That whose non-existence cannot be conceived in the intellect
C) That which is obligatory by religious law
D) That which cannot exist

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, possible—are the foundational categories through which all kalām arguments operate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something bad happen, do you immediately assume it was 'possible' because it occurred, or do you reflect on whether it was necessary or impossible in Allah's plan? How does your reaction reveal your understanding of qadr?
?
If you truly believed that your own existence is merely possible (ja'iz) and that you could as easily not have existed, would your gratitude to Allah for creating you deepen? What would change in your daily life if you lived with that awareness?
?
The mutakallim's goal is to affirm or negate one of these three judgments regarding theological claims. Consider a time you made a statement of faith: did you treat it as necessary, impossible, or possible? How might you have been confused between logical impossibility and emotional difficulty?

The Three Rational Judgments and Their Role in Kalam
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and explains their necessity for kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments and why they are foundational for all reasoning in kalam.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the basic grammar of all theological reasoning.

Sanusi begins by establishing the conceptual foundation of kalam: the three rational judgments. Before any theological argument can be made, the mind must have a clear classification of what is necessary, impossible, and contingent. This passage defines these categories and shows their indispensability for any valid speculation about Allah and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the concise and precise style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the three categories
Locate the three terms: wujub, istihala, jawaz. Then read their definitions and examples.
Each category is defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive.
3
Understand their role in kalam
Sanusi states that all topics of kalam revolve around these three. Ask yourself: why must every mutakallim know these?
The purpose of kalam is to affirm or negate something from these categories.
4
Connect to the intellect
Note the link between perceiving these three and being rational ('aql). See how Sanusi relates this to both Imam al-Haramayn and al-Muhasibi.
Even if 'aql is defined as an instinct, perception of these three is inseparable from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم العقلي، وبيان حدوده
وذلك أن تعلم أولاً : أنَّ الحكم العقليَّ مُنحصر في ثلاثة أقسام : الوجوب ، والاستحالة، والجواز ، وعلى هذه الثلاثةِ مدار مباحث علم الكلام كلها
فالواجبُ : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه ؛ كالتحيز مثلاً للجوهر .
والمستحيل : ما لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده ؛ كعُرُو الجوهر عن الحركة والسكون .
والجائزُ : ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه ؛ كموتِ الواحدِ منَّا اليوم أو غداً
لا شك أنَّ تصوُّرَ هذهِ الأحكام الثلاثة ومعرفة حقائقها مِنْ مبادئ علمِ الكلامِ ؛ إذْ غرضُ المُتكلّم مُنحصِرٌ في هذهِ الثلاثةِ ؛ بأَنْ يثبت شيئاً منها ، أو ينفيه ، أو يثبت ما يتفرَّعُ عن ذلكَ ، فمَنْ لم يعرفُ حقائق هذه الأحكام الثلاثة.. لم يفهم ما ثبت منها في هذا العلم وما نُفِيَ .
وإدراك هذه الثلاثة : هو العقلُ الذي هو مبدأ النظر عند إمام الحرمين (١) ، فمَنْ لم يدركها فليس بعاقل ، ولا يتأتى منه نظر ولا استدلال صحيح أصلاً ، ومَنْ فسَّرَ العقل كالمحاسبي بأنَّهُ غريزة يتأتى بها إدراك المعقولات .. فإدراك تلك الثلاثة ملازم لهذهِ الغريزة (٢) ، وإلا لم يتأت بها إدراك المعقول
The divisions of rational judgment and the exposition of its limits. That is, know first: that rational judgment is confined to three divisions: al-wujub (necessity), al-istihala (impossibility), and al-jawaz (contingency). Upon these three revolve all the topics of the science of kalam. Al-wajib: that whose non-existence cannot be conceived in the mind; like occupation of space for a substance, for example. Al-mustahil: that whose existence cannot be conceived in the mind; like a substance being devoid of motion and rest. Al-ja'iz: that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind; like the death of one of us today or tomorrow. There is no doubt that the conception of these three judgments and knowledge of their realities are among the principles of the science of kalam; for the purpose of the mutakallim is confined to these three: to affirm something of them, or negate it, or affirm what branches from that. So whoever does not know the realities of these three judgments will not understand what is affirmed in this science and what is negated. And the perception of these three is the 'aql (intellect) which is the principle of speculation according to Imam al-Haramayn (1); so whoever does not perceive them is not rational, and no valid speculation or correct inference can come from him at all. And whoever interprets 'aql like al-Muhasibi as an instinct (ghariza) by which the perception of intelligibles is attained, then the perception of those three is inseparable from this instinct (2); otherwise, the perception of intelligibles would not be attained by it.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the three rational judgments and explains their foundational role in kalam. Sanusi begins by stating that rational judgment is confined to three categories: wujub (necessity), istihala (impossibility), and jawaz (contingency). He then defines each with examples: wajib is that whose non-existence is inconceivable (e.g., a substance's occupation of space); mustahil is that whose existence is inconceivable (e.g., a substance devoid of motion and rest); ja'iz is that whose existence and non-existence are both conceivable (e.g., one's death today or tomorrow). Sanusi then emphasizes that understanding these three is a principle of kalam because the mutakallim's entire purpose is to affirm or negate something from these categories or to affirm what follows from them; hence, ignorance of them prevents understanding of the science. Finally, he connects perception of these three to the intellect ('aql) as defined by Imam al-Haramayn — one who lacks this perception is not rational and cannot engage in valid speculation. He also addresses the alternative definition of 'aql by al-Muhasibi as an instinct for grasping intelligibles, noting that perception of the three judgments is inseparable from that instinct; otherwise, the instinct would not yield knowledge of intelligibles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these three rational judgments are merely abstract logical categories without direct relevance to theology.
Correction: Sanusi shows they are the very basis of kalam: the mutakallim's entire purpose is to affirm or negate something from these categories or their branches.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessity, impossibility, and contingency—are the foundation of all theological reasoning in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says that without perceiving these three judgments, no valid speculation or correct inference is possible. Is your own thinking about theological matters consistently grounded in these categories, or do you sometimes slip into confusion? What will you do to correct that?
?

Intellect and the Three Rational Judgments
Sanusi defines intellect and ties it to the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the intellect in kalam is defined by its capacity to perceive the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mustahil), and that this capacity is foundational for correct reasoning.

Core Idea
Intellect is the perception of the three rational judgments.

This passage establishes the very definition of intellect in the kalam tradition. Before we can discuss Allah's attributes, we must agree on what it means to be rational. Sanusi, following Imam al-Haramayn, argues that true intellect is nothing other than the ability to perceive the three fundamental rational categories. Without this, no valid reflection is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full English translation. Let the author's argument structure become clear.
Notice the reference to Imam al-Haramayn and al-Muhasibi.
2
Identify the three rational judgments
The passage refers to 'these three' — which in context are the three rational judgments: wajib (necessary), ja'iz (possible), and mustahil (impossible). Understand that these form the core of rational perception.
3
Understand the argument about intellect
The passage equates perception of these three with intellect itself. It then argues that without them, no correct reflection or inference is possible. Finally, it reconciles an alternative definition of intellect as instinct.
Ask yourself: Why are these three judgments so essential?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإدراك هذه الثلاثة : هو العقلُ الذي هو مبدأ النظر عند إمام الحرمين (١) ، فمَنْ لم يدركها فليس بعاقل ، ولا يتأتى منه نظر ولا استدلال صحيح أصلاً ، ومَنْ  َفسَّرَ العقل كالمحاسبي بأنَّهُ غريزة يتأتى بها إدراك المعقولات .. فإدراك تلك الثلاثة ملازم لهذهِ الغريزة (٢) ، وإلا لم يتأت بها إدراك المعقول
And the perception of these three is the intellect which is the basis of reflection according to Imam al-Haramayn (1). So whoever does not perceive them is not rational, and no correct reflection or inference can occur from him at all. And whoever interprets the intellect like al-Muhasibi as an instinct by which the perception of intelligibles is attained... then the perception of those three is concomitant with this instinct (2), otherwise the perception of intelligibles would not be attained by it.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This is a definition passage. The text begins by stating that the perception of the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mustahil) is the intellect itself, which is the starting point of reflection according to Imam al-Haramayn. It then asserts that one who does not perceive these three is not truly rational and cannot engage in valid reasoning. The text then addresses an alternative definition of intellect by al-Muhasibi, who defines it as an instinct that enables the perception of intelligibles. The text argues that the perception of the three judgments is necessarily concomitant with this instinct, otherwise the instinct would not produce perception of intelligibles. Thus, the three rational judgments are fundamental to the definition of intellect.

الغريزة

Correction: The passage defines intellect as the capacity to perceive the three rational judgments, not as acquired knowledge.

B) They are the very definition of intellect.
C) They are irrelevant to intellect.

Key Takeaway
Intellect is defined as the capacity to perceive the three rational judgments, without which no sound reasoning is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the perception of the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mustahil) is the very definition of intellect, then how often do you consciously apply these categories before forming a belief or making a decision? Starting today, pick one belief you hold and test it against these three judgments. What do you discover?
?
The passage warns that without perceiving these three, no correct reflection or inference can occur. Reflect on a time when your reasoning led you to a wrong conclusion. Could it be that you were missing a clear grasp of what is necessary, possible, or impossible in that situation? How would you approach it differently now?
?
Al-Muhasibi described intellect as an instinct, but the text insists that the perception of the three judgments is inseparable from it. Are there areas in your life where you rely on 'instinct' without checking it against the fundamental categories of reason? Will you commit to examining your instincts through the lens of these three judgments before acting?

Defining Wajib: Necessity and Reflection
Sanusi defines al-wajib and distinguishes its two modes of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-wajib in kalam and how it is divided into two types based on how its necessity is perceived.

Core Idea
Wajib is what cannot be thought non-existent—perceived either innately or through reflection.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces the foundational concept of al-wajib (the necessary). He not only defines it but also shows that necessity can be known in two distinct ways: some truths are so basic they require no thought, while others demand careful reasoning. Understanding this distinction is essential before tackling more complex theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the heading signals that examples will follow for wajib, ja'iz, and mustahil, but the passage focuses on defining wajib.
2
Identify the two types
Locate the phrases 'إمَّا بالضرورة' and 'إمَّا بالنظر'. These mark the two ways the intellect perceives the necessity of a wajib: by necessity (innate) or by reflection (reasoned).
The examples—occupying space for necessity, God's eternity for reflection—clarify the distinction.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why both types matter: some necessary truths (like a substance taking space) are self-evident, while others (like God's existence) require rational proof. This shows that 'wajib' is broader than just God.
This prevents the misunderstanding that only God is wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التمثيل للواجب والجائز والمستحيل ] وقوله في حد الواجب : ( ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ) أي : ما لا يُدرك في العقل عدمه . وهو يعني بعدم إدراكِ عدمِهِ : إمَّا بالضرورة ؛ كتحيز الجوهر مثلاً ؛ أخذُهُ قدراً مِنَ الفراغ ، فإِنَّ وجوبَ هذا المعنى له ضروري للعقل ، لا يفتقر إلى تأمُّل ، وإِمَّا بالنظرِ ؛ كقِدَم مولانا جل وعزَّ مثلاً ؛ فإنَّ وجوبَ هذا المعنى إنَّما يدركه العقلُ بالتأمل ؛ بأن يقول :
[Exemplification of the wajib, ja'iz, and mustahil] And his statement in the definition of al-wajib: (that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect) i.e., that whose non-existence is not perceived in the intellect. And by non-perception of its non-existence he means: either by necessity (bi al-darura), like the occupying of space (tahayyuz) of a substance, for example; its taking a measure of space; for the necessity of this meaning is innate for the intellect, not requiring reflection; or by reflection (bi al-nazar), like the eternity (qidam) of our Lord, exalted and majestic, for example; for the necessity of this meaning is only perceived by the intellect through reflection, by saying: ...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading indicating that it will give examples for the wajib, ja'iz, and mustahil. The text then quotes an earlier definition of al-wajib: 'that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect' and explains it. The author then clarifies that the 'non-perception of its non-existence' can be of two types: (1) by necessity (bi al-darura): e.g., the occupying of space (tahayyuz) of a substance; its taking a measure of space is perceived as necessarily existent, requiring no reflection. (2) by reflection (bi al-nazar): e.g., the eternity (qidam) of God; its necessity is perceived only through rational reflection. The text provides the first part of the reflective argument: 'by saying...' but does not complete it here.

Correction: Wajib also includes necessary properties of substances, such as occupying space, which is known by necessity.

The Division of Wajib: Necessary and Theoretical Judgment
Sanusi divides the necessary judgment into immediate and inferential types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational judgment 'necessary' is divided into two kinds: necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī), and you will be able to distinguish them using the examples of a substance occupying space and God's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Not all necessary truths are self-evident; some require proof.

This lesson explores the rational judgment 'necessary' (wājib) and its two categories. Sanusi defines it as that whose non-existence is inconceivable to the intellect. He then shows that this inconceivability can be immediate (ḍarūrī) or reached through reasoning (naẓarī), using clear examples to illustrate each type.

Guided Path
1
Define the necessary judgment
Read the opening definition: 'That whose non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect.' Understand that this applies to both types.
2
Examine the first type: necessary (ḍarūrī)
Look at the example of a substance occupying space. Notice that the intellect grasps this necessity without any reflection.
This type does not require reasoning.
3
Examine the second type: theoretical (naẓarī)
Study the example of God's pre-eternity. Follow the logical reasoning provided: if originated, then need for originator leads to regress or circle, impossible, so pre-eternity is necessary.
This type requires a proof to be known.
4
Reflect on the division's scope
Sanusi notes that the same division applies to impossible and contingent judgments. Consider why this matters for systematic theology.
It shows a consistent method for all rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في حد الواجب : ( ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ) أي : ما لا يُدرك في العقل عدمه . ويعني بعدم إدراكِ عدمِهِ : إمَّا بالضرورة ؛ كتحيز الجوهر مثلاً ؛ أخذُهُ قدراً مِنَ الفراغ ، فإِنَّ وجوبَ هذا المعنى له ضروري للعقل ، لا يفتقر إلى تأمُّل ، وإِمَّا بالنظرِ ؛ كقِدَم مولانا جل وعزَّ مثلاً ؛ فإنَّ وجوبَ هذا المعنى إنَّما يدركه العقلُ بالتأمل ؛ بأن يقول : لو كان تعالى حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، ويتسلسل أو يدور ، وكلاهما مستحيل ، فحدوثه تعالى إذا مستحيل ، فالقِدَمُ لَهُ جَلَّ وعزَّ إذاً واجبٌ ؛ إذ لا واسطة بين القدم والحدوثِ . فقد عرفت بهذا أنَّ الواجب على قسمين : ضروري ونظري . ومثل هذا التقسيم ينقسم كلٌّ مِنَ المستحيل والجائز.
And His statement in the definition of the necessary (al-wājib): 'That whose non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect' – meaning: that whose non-existence is not perceived in the intellect. And by 'non-perception of its non-existence' He means: either necessarily (bi-ḍ-ḍarūra), like the occupancy of space by a substance, for example; its taking an amount of void, for indeed the necessity of this meaning is necessary for the intellect, not needing reflection; or by theoretical reasoning (bi-n-naẓar), like the pre-eternity of our Master, Glorious and Exalted, for example; for indeed the necessity of this meaning is only perceived by the intellect through reflection, by saying: 'If the Exalted were originated, He would need an originator, and there would be an infinite regress or a circle, both of which are impossible; so the origination of the Exalted is therefore impossible, therefore pre-eternity for Him, Glorious and Exalted, is necessary, since there is no intermediary between pre-eternity and origination.' So you have known from this that the necessary is of two kinds: necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). And like this division, each of the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ) and the contingent (al-jāʾiz) is divided.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a definition and division of the rational judgment 'necessary' (wājib). It takes as its starting point a definition of wājib: 'that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect'. The author then clarifies that 'inconceivable' means the intellect does not perceive its non-existence. This non-perception can occur in two ways. First, necessarily (ḍarūrī): the intellect immediately sees that the judgment is necessary without any reflection. The example given is a substance's occupation of space (taḥayyuz al-jawhar), meaning it takes up an amount of void. The necessity of this attribute for a substance is instinctively known. Second, through theoretical reasoning (naẓarī): the intellect needs to reflect to reach the judgment. The example is the pre-eternity (qidam) of God. One reasons: if God were originated (ḥādith), He would need an originator, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a circle (dawr), both impossible; therefore origination is impossible for Him, so pre-eternity is necessary, as there is no intermediate state. Thus wājib is divided into two kinds: necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The author then states that the same division applies to the other two rational judgments: impossible (mumtaniʿ) and contingent (jāʾiz). This sets up the next lesson.

تحيز الجوهر
قدم الله

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary truths are self-evident.
Correction: Sanusi divides necessary judgments into those known immediately (ḍarūrī) and those requiring proof (naẓarī), like God's pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the example given for a necessary (ḍarūrī) wajib?
A) The pre-eternity of God
B) The impossibility of a body being neither moving nor still
C) A substance occupying space
D) God not being a body

Key Takeaway
The rational judgment 'necessary' is divided into two kinds: one known immediately by the intellect (ḍarūrī) and one known through reasoning (naẓarī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief about your faith that you have always assumed was self-evident—like the existence of God or the obligation of prayer. After reading this lesson, do you now see that it might require reasoning? Are you willing to examine that belief rationally, even if it makes you uncomfortable? What is one step you can take this week to strengthen that belief with sound reasoning?
?
This lesson shows a clear method for proving God's pre-eternity. But do you personally rely more on intellectual proofs or on inward certainty (such as from the Quran or spiritual experience)? Which one shapes your daily actions more? Be honest: if your certainty were tested, would your reasoning hold up? What would you say to someone who asked you, 'Why do you believe God is eternal?' Could you give the proof from this passage?
?

Consider one such area. What would it cost you to investigate it properly using theoretical reasoning? Are you willing to pay that price to move from blind acceptance to true conviction?
