Examples of Necessary and Theoretical Impossible
Sanusi illustrates necessary and theoretical impossible with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a necessary impossible (mumtani' dharuri) and a theoretical impossible (mumtani' nazari) through concrete examples from Sanusi's text.

Core Idea
Some impossibilities are self-evident; others require proof.

Sanusi now gives two concrete examples to clarify the division of the impossible (mumtani') into necessary and theoretical. The first example, a body being neither moving nor still, is immediately recognized as impossible by the intellect. The second example, God being a body, requires rational proof to establish its impossibility. This lesson will walk through both examples and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the English translation. Notice how Sanusi first gives a general example (existence and non-existence) and then the specific example from the original text.
Focus on the logical structure: definition of body leads to necessary impossibility.
2
Understand the necessary impossible example
Examine why the abstraction of a body from both motion and stillness is necessarily impossible. Sanusi defines body as that which occupies space, so it must either stay (still) or move.
The impossibility is immediate, without need for reasoning.
3
Understand the theoretical impossible example
See why God being a body is theoretical impossible. It requires two prior proofs: that every body is originated, and that God is pre-eternal.
Note the dependence on prior knowledge—this is the mark of a theoretical judgment.
4
Contrast the two examples
Compare the two: one is self-evident, the other requires reasoning. This illustrates the core distinction between dharuri and nazari in rational judgments.
This distinction is foundational in kalam epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمثالُ المستحيل الضروري : الجمعُ بين وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ ، ونحوه ما مثَّلَ بهِ في الأصلِ ؛ وهو عُرُدُّ الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون معاً ؛ أي : تجرُّدُهُ عنهما بحيثُ لا يتَصفُ بواحد منهما ؛ فإنَّه لا يخفى الحكم باستحالة هذا العُرُوِّ ضروري للعقل ؛ إذ الجِرْمُ معناه : أنَّ ما لهُ حيّز ؛ أي : قَدْرٌ مِنَ الفراغ ، فهو إما أنْ يثبت فيهِ فيكون ساكناً أو ينتقل عنه فيكونَ مُتحرِّكاً ، وكونه لا يثبتُ في حيَّزِهِ ولا ينتقل عنه مستحيل ضرورةً . ومثال المستحيل النظري : كونُ مولانا جل وعزَّ جِرْماً مثلاً ؛ فإِنَّ الحكم باستحالة هذا المعنى عليهِ جلَّ وعزَّ إِنَّما يدركه العقل بواسطة معرفة شيئين : أحدهما : برهان وجوب حدوثِ الأجرام . والثاني : برهان وجوبِ القِدَمِ لمولانا جل وعزَّ . فإذا حقق هذين المطلبين صح له حينئذٍ أنْ يقول : لو كان مولانا جل وعزَّ جِرْماً لوجبَ حدوثه ؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوبِ حدوثِ الأجرامِ ،
So an example of the necessary impossible (al-mumtaniʿ aḍ-ḍarūrī): the conjunction of existence and non-existence of a thing, and similar to it what he gave as an example in the original: and it is the abstraction of a body from motion and stillness together; i.e., its divestment from both so that it is not qualified by either of them; for indeed the judgment of the impossibility of this abstraction is not hidden—it is necessary for the intellect; for the meaning of body (jirm) is: that which has a space—i.e., an amount of void—so it either settles in it and is still, or moves from it and is moving; and its not settling in its space nor moving from it is impossible necessarily. And an example of the theoretical impossible (al-mumtaniʿ an-naẓarī): our Master, Glorious and Exalted, being a body, for example; for indeed the judgment of the impossibility of this meaning for Him, Glorious and Exalted, is only perceived by the intellect through knowledge of two things: one of them: the proof of the necessity of the origination of bodies; and the second: the proof of the necessity of pre-eternity for our Master, Glorious and Exalted. So if he investigates these two demands, it becomes correct for him then to say: 'If our Master, Glorious and Exalted, were a body, His origination would be necessary, because it is established that bodies are necessarily originated'.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples for the division of the impossible (mumtaniʿ) into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The first example of a necessary impossibility is the conjunction of existence and non-existence of the same thing—this is self-evidently impossible. A related example, given in the original source, is the abstraction (ʿurūḏ) of a body from both motion and stillness simultaneously. The author explains why this is immediately known as impossible: a body is defined as something that occupies a space (ḥayyiz), meaning an amount of void. It either stays fixed in that space and is still, or it moves away and is in motion. It cannot do neither—that would violate its definition. Hence the impossibility is necessary (ḍarūrī) for the intellect without any need for argument. The second example is a theoretical impossibility: God being a body. This judgment is not self-evident; it requires reasoning (naẓar). The intellect perceives this impossibility through knowledge of two proofs: (1) the proof that every body is necessarily originated (wujūb ḥudūth al-ajrām), and (2) the proof that God is necessarily pre-eternal (wujūb al-qidam). Once these two proofs are established, one can conclude: if God were a body, He would be originated (since all bodies are originated), but He is pre-eternal, so the supposition is impossible. This is a theoretical (naẓarī) impossibility because it depends on prior rational arguments.

برهان وجوب حدوث الأجرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the impossibility of God being a body is self-evident like the first example.
Correction: Sanusi shows it requires rational proof, hence it is theoretical (naẓarī), not necessary (ḍarūrī).

Checkpoint
Why is the impossibility of a body being neither moving nor still considered necessary (ḍarūrī)?
A) Because it requires a complex proof
B) Because the definition of a body directly entails that it must be either moving or still
C) Because it is mentioned in revelation
D) Because it is agreed upon by all schools

Key Takeaway
A necessary impossible is self-evident, while a theoretical impossible requires rational proof to be known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If a necessary impossible like the conjunction of existence and non-existence is self-evident, what does that tell you about the clarity of your own mind? Are there contradictory beliefs you hold that you have not yet confronted?
?
Sanusi uses rational arguments to defend God's transcendence. When you next face doubt about an attribute of Allah, will you seek the rational proofs, or rely only on emotion? What step will you take today to strengthen your intellectual foundation?

Rational Judgments

Two Kinds of Impossible: Self-Evident and Theoretical
Sanusi enumerates and illustrates two kinds of impossibility with detailed examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between self-evident and theoretical impossibility and how rational proofs establish theological truths.

Core Idea
Some impossibilities are obvious; others require proof.

Sanusi continues his discussion of rational judgments by applying the division to the category of the impossible. He illustrates each kind with a concrete example: a body cannot be simultaneously free from both motion and rest (self-evident), and God cannot be a body (theoretical). This lesson unpacks both examples and shows how theoretical impossibility demands a two-part demonstration. You'll learn how the mind moves from premises to conclusion using conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi begins by stating the division applies to both impossible and possible, but here focuses on the impossible.
2
Identify the two examples
Locate the self-evident impossible example (body free from motion and rest) and the theoretical impossible example (God being a body). Observe how the author describes each.
The self-evident example is explained in one sentence; the theoretical example unfolds over several lines.
3
Follow the theoretical argument
Trace the two proofs Sanusi uses: (1) all bodies are temporally originated, (2) God is eternal. See how they combine in a conditional syllogism (if body then originated; God is not originated; therefore God is not a body).
The author explicitly names the two parts: the conditional premise (ملازمة شرطية) and the exceptive premise (استثنائية).
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Sanusi provides these examples. They show that not all impossibilities are immediately obvious; some require rational demonstration. This affirms that theology can use rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل هذا التقسيم ينقسم كلٌّ مِنَ المستحيل والجائز : فمثالُ المستحيل الضروري : الجمعُ بين وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ ، ونحوه ما مثَّلَ بهِ في الأصلِ ؛ وهو عُرُدُّ الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون معاً ؛ أي : تجرُّدُهُ عنهما بحيثُ لا يتَصفُ بواحد منهما ؛ فإنَّه لا يخفى الحكم باستحالة هذا العُرُوِّ ضروري للعقل ؛ إذ الجِرْمُ معناه : أنَّ ما لهُ حيّز ؛ أي : قَدْرٌ مِنَ الفراغ ، فهو إما أنْ يثبت فيهِ فيكون ساكناً أو ينتقل عنه فيكونَ مُتحرِّكاً ، وكونه لا يثبتُ في حيَّزِهِ ولا ينتقل عنه مستحيل ضرورةً ومثال المستحيل النظري : كونُ مولانا جل وعزَّ جِرْماً مثلاً ؛ فإِنَّ الحكم باستحالة هذا المعنى عليهِ جلَّ وعزَّ إِنَّما يدركه العقل بواسطة معرفة شيئين : أحدهما : برهان وجوب حدوثِ الأجرام والثاني : برهان وجوبِ القِدَمِ لمولانا جل وعزَّ . فإذا حقق هذين المطلبين صح له حينئذٍ أنْ يقول : لو كان مولانا جل وعزَّ جِرْماً لوجبَ حدوثه ؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوبِ حدوثِ الأجرامِ ، لكنَّه جلَّ وعزّ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوبِ قِدَمِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ ; فحينئذٍ ينتج لهُ : أنَّ مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ جِرْماً ; فالمطلب الأوَّلُ الذي عرفناه بالبرهان - وهو وجوب الحدوثِ لجميع الأجرام - هو الذي قرَّرَ لنا الجزء الأَوَّلَ مِنْ هذا الدليل ; وهو ملازمه الشرطية، والمطلب الثاني - وهو وجوبُ القِدَمِ له جلَّ وعلا ـ هو الذي قرَّرَ لنا الجزء الثاني مِنَ الدليل ; وهو الاستثنائية، وقس على هذا، فمثله كثير، والله الموفق .
And similarly, each of the impossible and the possible is divided into two. An example of the self‑evident impossible is the combination of existence and non‑existence of a thing, and similar to what was exemplified in the original text, which is the abstraction of a body from motion and rest together; that is, its being devoid of both such that it is not characterized by either. For the judgment of the impossibility of this abstraction is self‑evident to the intellect. Indeed, a body means: that which occupies space, i.e., a portion of emptiness, so it either settles in it and is at rest, or moves away from it and is in motion. Its neither settling in its space nor moving away from it is impossible by necessity. An example of the theoretical impossible is, for instance, that our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted, be a body. For the intellect perceives the impossibility of this meaning for Him only through knowledge of two things: first, the proof of the necessity of the temporal origination of bodies, and second, the proof of the necessity of pre‑eternity for our Lord. So when he has verified these two requirements, he can then say: 'If our Lord were a body, His temporal origination would be necessary, based on what has been established regarding the necessity of temporal origination of bodies. But it is impossible for Him to be temporally originated, based on what has been established regarding the necessity of His pre‑eternity. Thus, it follows for him that it is impossible for our Lord to be a body.' So the first requirement, which we have known through demonstration, namely the necessity of temporal origination for all bodies, is what established for us the first part of this proof, which is its conditional premise. The second requirement, namely the necessity of pre‑eternity for Him, is what established for us the second part of the proof, which is the exception [i.e., the categorical premise]. And analogize from this, for its like is many. God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage applies the dual division (self‑evident and theoretical) to the category of the impossible (al‑mustaḥīl). The author first gives an example of self‑evident impossibility: the combination of existence and non‑existence, and the abstraction of a body from both motion and rest simultaneously. He explains that a body, by definition, occupies space; it must either be at rest (settled in its space) or in motion (moving away from it). The impossibility of neither state is obvious to the intellect without any need for reasoning. Then he provides an example of theoretical impossibility: that God be a body. This impossibility is not immediately apparent; it requires two proofs: (1) the proof that all bodies are temporally originated (wujūb ḥudūth al‑ajrām), and (2) the proof that God is eternal (wujūb al‑qidam). Using a conditional syllogism (sharṭiyya) and an exceptive syllogism (istithnā'iyya), one concludes: if God were a body, He would be originated (since all bodies are originated), but He is eternal (not originated), therefore God cannot be a body. This illustrates how theoretical judgments rely on demonstration (burhān).

Key Terms

الضروري
النظري
الجسم
الحركة
السكون
البرهان
الملازمة الشرطية
الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of God being a body is self-evident like the abstraction of body from motion and rest.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that this impossibility is theoretical, requiring two proofs. It is not immediately obvious to the intellect without demonstration.

Checkpoint
Why is the impossibility of God being a body considered theoretical rather than self‑evident?

B) Because it requires two proofs to establish.
C) Because it is not actually impossible.
D) Because it is based on revelation only.

Key Takeaway
Theoretical impossibility relies on demonstration, showing that faith and reason work together to establish theological truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's transcendence, do you rely on rational proofs or just accept it because scholars say so? This lesson shows that some truths require effort to know—are you making that effort personally?
?
If someone challenged you tomorrow, 'Why can't God be a body?', could you clearly explain the two premises from this lesson? If not, what will you do to deepen your understanding?
?
The proof in this lesson used a logical chain: if A then B, not B, therefore not A. Do you trust that human reason can lead you to certainty about God? Or do you harbor doubts that hold you back from fully embracing these proofs?

Division of the Possible into Self-Evident and Theoretical
Sanusi divides the possible into self-evident and theoretical, with refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possible (al-mumkin) is divided into self-evident and theoretical types, and why theological debates arise when rational proofs are neglected.

Core Idea
The possible is either self-evident or theoretical; theological errors often stem from ignoring rational proof.

In this passage, Sanusi applies the earlier division of rational judgments to the category of the possible. He shows that within the possible, some matters are known immediately (self-evident) while others require rational proof (theoretical). He illustrates this with two key examples: God's rewarding the obedient and bodily resurrection, and explains how deviant groups erred by failing to grasp the proof of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow from definition to examples to refutation.
The passage is a direct continuation of the previous lesson on rational judgments.
2
Identify the two categories of the possible
Look for how Sanusi distinguishes between self-evident (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī) types of the possible. The self-evident type is exemplified from the original creed.
The theoretical type requires proof; its examples are given next.
3
Examine the two theoretical examples
First example: God's rewarding the obedient is possible, not obligatory. Second example: bodily resurrection is possible, not impossible. Note which groups erred on each.
The Mu'tazila made reward obligatory by reason; disbelievers and philosophers denied resurrection.
4
Reflect on the incomplete conclusion
The passage ends mid-sentence, likely stating that each of the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) is divided into two types. Consider what Sanusi intended.
This completes the framework for the entire discussion of rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الجائز - ويُقالُ لهُ : الممكنُ أيضاً - : فمثالُ الضروري منهُ : ما مثل بهِ في أصل العقيدة ومثالُ النظري منهُ : كونُ إثابةِ اللهِ تعالى للمطيع جائزة لا واجبة ، وكونُ البعثِ جائزاً لا مستحيلاً ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ الجائزاتِ التي تفتقرُ إلى البرهان ، ولافتقارها إلى البرهانِ عَمِيَ أَمرُها على كثيرٍ ممَّنْ ضَلَّ ; إذ لم يُوفقوا للاطلاع على برهان إمكانها ، ولهذا خالف في المثالِ الأول المعتزلة ، فجعلوا إثابة الله تعالى للمطيع واجبةً عقلاً ، وخالف في المثال الثاني كثيرٌ مِنَ الكفَرةِ ، فقالوا : وما نحن بمبعوثين(۱) وخالف فيه الفلاسفة أيضاً، فمنعوا البعث البدني، والله سبحانَهُ الهادي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إِلى صراط مستقيم فقدِ اتَّضِحَ لكَ : أنَّ هذه الأقسام الثلاثةَ ينقسم كلُّ واحدٍ منها إلى
As for the permissible—also called the possible—an example of its self‑evident type is what was exemplified in the original creed. An example of its theoretical type is that God's rewarding the obedient is permissible, not obligatory, and that resurrection is permissible, not impossible, and similar things from the permissibilities that require proof. Due to their need for proof, their matter was obscure to many of those who went astray, for they were not granted success in obtaining the proof of their possibility. For this reason, the Muʿtazila disagreed in the first example, making God's rewarding the obedient obligatory by reason. In the second example, many of the disbelievers disagreed, saying: 'We will not be resurrected' (Qur'an 6:29), and the philosophers also disagreed, denying bodily resurrection. And God, glorified be He, is the Guide whom He wills to the straight path. It has thus become clear to you that these three divisions are each divided into [two].
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage covers the division of the possible (al‑jā'iz, also called al‑mumkin) into self‑evident (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The author first notes the self‑evident examples from the original creed. Then he gives two theoretical examples: (1) God's rewarding the obedient is possible, not obligatory by reason—contra the Muʿtazila who held it obligatory; (2) bodily resurrection is possible, not impossible—contra disbelievers and philosophers who denied it. The author explains that these matters require rational proof; many went astray because they failed to grasp the proof of their possibility. The passage concludes by stating that it is now clear that each of the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) is divided into two types (self‑evident and theoretical), though the sentence is incomplete in the source.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that God's rewarding the obedient is rationally obligatory.
Correction: Sanusi teaches that it is permissible (jā'iz) and not obligatory by reason; it is a grace from God.

Checkpoint

A) They believed reward is impossible.
B) They believed reward is necessary by reason.
C) They denied resurrection.
D) They believed God is a body.

Key Takeaway
The possible includes matters that require rational proof; theological errors often stem from neglecting this proof.

?

?
Disbelievers and philosophers denied resurrection because they could not conceive its possibility. What truth about the afterlife—such as bodily resurrection, judgment, or eternal life—do you struggle to truly internalize because it seems remote or beyond your current experience? The Possible: Examples and Deviant Views
Sanusi defines the possible and gives two theoretical examples with deviations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the possible in kalam, its two types of examples, and how deviant sects erred by misapplying rational judgments.

Core Idea
The possible is what can be or not be — neither necessary nor impossible.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin defines the third rational judgment: the possible. After explaining its two types of examples — self-evident and theoretical — Sanusi warns that many went astray by failing to grasp the proof of possibility. The Mu'tazila wrongly made reward obligatory, and philosophers denied bodily resurrection. Understanding these errors helps clarify correct Sunni doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Sanusi's definition and examples.
Notice the two categories: self-evident (daruri) and theoretical (nazari).
2
Identify the two types of possible
The self-evident example is from the original creed; the theoretical examples are God's rewarding the obedient and resurrection.
Theoretical examples require demonstration (burhan).
3
Understand the warning
The passage states that the need for proof caused many to go astray because they failed to attain the proof of possibility.
4
Analyze the deviations
The Mu'tazila made reward obligatory (confusing possible with necessary); disbelievers and philosophers denied resurrection (confusing possible with impossible).
The philosophers specifically denied bodily resurrection.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Why does correctly classifying actions as possible matter for theology? It preserves divine freedom and the contingency of creation.

This passage defines the possible (al-ja'iz/al-mumkin) and gives examples. The possible is what can either occur or not occur. Its self-evident examples were given earlier in the creed. Its theoretical examples require proof: (1) God's rewarding the obedient is possible, not obligatory; (2) resurrection is possible, not impossible. The author notes that because these require demonstration, many deviants err. The Mu'tazila wrongly make reward obligatory. Disbelievers deny resurrection entirely (citing Quran 6:29). Philosophers deny bodily resurrection (affirming only spiritual). God guides whom He wills.

Six Divisions of Rational Judgment
Sanusi explains the twofold subdivision of each rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the three rational judgments is divided into self-evident and theoretical types, resulting in six total categories.

Core Idea
Each rational judgment has two modes: self-evident and theoretical, giving six in all.

Sanusi has just established the three rational judgments. Now he shows that each can be known in two ways: immediately or through reflection. This creates a complete set of six divisions. The author also explains why he chose only self-evident examples in his creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the mathematical language: 'multiplying three by two'.
2
Identify the six divisions
Understand that each of the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) has a self-evident and a theoretical variant, making six.
Self-evident means known without reflection; theoretical means requiring thought.
3
Reflect on the author's method
Sanusi explains why he only gave self-evident examples in his creed: to avoid complexity and ensure clarity.
Theoretical examples require deeper reasoning, which he chose to omit.
4
Consider the broader implication
This division shows that theological knowledge can be either immediate or acquired, and both are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقدِ اتَّضِحَ لكَ: أنَّ هذه الأقسام الثلاثةَ ينقسم كلُّ واحدٍ منها إلى قسمين: ضروري ونظري، وبحسَب ذلك كان مجموع أقسامها ستة؛ مِنْ ضرب ثلاثة في اثنينِ، وقدِ اقتصرَ في أصل العقيدة على أنْ مثَلَ لكلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأقسامِ الثلاثةِ بالضروري مِنْ قسميه؛ طلباً للإيضاح؛ لئلا يتشعب في التمثيل بما لا يُدرِكُ العقلُ حكمَهُ إلا بعد تأمل، وبالله التوفيق.
Thus it has become clear to you that these three divisions each divide into two parts: self-evident (daruri) and theoretical (nazari). Accordingly, their total number is six — from multiplying three by two. And the author confined himself in the original creed to giving for each of the three divisions only the self-evident of its two parts, seeking clarity, so that he would not branch out in exemplification to what the mind cannot perceive its ruling except after reflection. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of the rational judgments. It explains that each of the three categories (necessary, impossible, possible) can be either self-evident (daruri) or theoretical (nazari), making six total (3 × 2 = 6). The author notes that in the original creed he only gave self-evident examples for each category to avoid complexity, since theoretical examples require deeper reflection. He ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The self-evident/theoretical distinction depends on the individual's knowledge; what is self-evident for one may be theoretical for another. Also, self-evident judgments may still require some reflection to be articulated, but they are known without inference.

Key Takeaway
Each rational judgment can be known either directly (self-evident) or through reflection (theoretical), making six categories in total.

The Six Divisions of Rational Judgments
Sanusi explains the six divisions and the methodological choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six divisions of rational judgments and why the original creed used only daruri examples.

Core Idea
Three rational judgments multiply by two modes (daruri/nazari) make six.

Sanusi now explains a key methodological point: the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mustahil) each come in two varieties – known necessarily (daruri) or through reflection (nazari), making six in total. He then justifies why the original creed only gave examples from the daruri side: to keep things clear and avoid the need for deep reflection.

3

This passage explains the classification of rational judgments (al-ahkam al-‘aqliyya) and the reason the author used only necessary (daruri) examples in the original creed. The author of the original creed (likely a short text like Al-Aqida al-Sanusiyya) chose to give examples only from the necessary side of each judgment to avoid confusing the reader with examples that require reflection. The point is clarity and accessibility.

Proof of Contingency: Impossibility of Eternal Motion or Rest
Sanusi proves the impossibility of eternal motion and rest for bodies, concluding all bodies are created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that all bodies are originated by demonstrating that neither motion nor rest can be eternal.

Core Idea
If motion or rest were eternal, bodies could never change — but they do.

This passage presents a rigorous proof (burhān qaṭ‘ī) for the contingency of all material things. Sanusi systematically examines the three possible states of a body in pre-eternity and shows each leads to contradiction or impossibility. By the end, we see why nothing in the world can be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow of the proof.
Notice the structure: three divisions are introduced, then each is refuted.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage considers three possible states of a body in pre-eternity: (1) moving, (2) at rest, (3) neither moving nor at rest. Find where each is discussed.
The third division is dismissed as obviously impossible.
3
Analyze the argument against eternal rest
Understand why eternal rest leads to a contradiction: if rest were eternal, the body could never move, but we observe movement in bodies, so rest cannot be eternal.
Key point: the eternal cannot cease to exist.
4
Analyze the argument against eternal motion
The argument against eternal motion uses the same logic (if motion were eternal, rest would never occur) and adds that motion is inherently an accident that requires a prior state, which is incompatible with eternality.
Motion is defined as transition from one space to another, implying it is a temporary state.
5
Understand the conclusion
Having refuted all three possibilities, the author concludes that no body can be eternal. The entire world, from Throne to carpet, is contingent.
This is a certain proof (burhān qaṭ‘ī).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأزلِ ؛ فيكون وجود الجزم في الأزلِ مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّه لا يُعقل وجوده عارياً عن تلك الأقسام الثلاثةِ .
أمَّا بيانُ استحالةِ القسم الثالث : فظاهر ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ جرم في الأزلِ ولا فيما لا يزالُ .. ليس ثابتاً في الحيّز ولا منتقلاً عنه
وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الثاني - وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ ساكناً في الأزلِ - فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ كذلكَ لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرَّكَ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ سكونَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ قديم ، والقديم لا يقبلُ العدم ؛ إذ لو قبل العدم لاحتاج وجوده إلى مُخصص ؛ لجوازه حينئذ ، فيكونُ مُحْدَثاً ، وقد فُرِضَ قديماً ، فهذا تناقض لا يُعقَلُ ، ودليلُ قَبولِ السكونِ العدم : مشاهدتنا الحركة في بعض الأجرام ، وذلك يقضي بجواز الحركة على جميع الأجرام ؛ لتماثلها
·
وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الأوَّلِ ـ وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ مُتحرِّكاً - فالوجه فيه : ما عرفت الآنَ في القسم الثاني ، ويزيدُ هذا القسم بوجه آخرَ مِنَ الاستحالة ؛ أنَّ حقيقة الحركةِ لا تُعقَلُ قديمةً ؛ إذْ هى الانتقالُ مِنْ وهو حيز إلى حيز ، فهي إذاً لا تكون إلا طارئة على الجرْمِ ، ولا بدَّ أنْ يتقدَّمَ على وجودِها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والقديمُ لا يُتصوَّرُ أنْ يكونَ طارئاً ، ولا أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودِهِ غيره
فقد خرج لك بهذا البرهان القطعي : كونُ العالم كلِّه حادثاً ؛ مِنْ عرشِهِ إِلى فَرْشِهِ ، لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ شيء منه قديماً .
In pre-eternity; then the existence of a body in pre-eternity is impossible, because it is not conceivable that it exist devoid of those three divisions. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the third division: it is apparent, because a body is not conceivable in pre-eternity nor in what never ceases [to be] as neither established in space nor moving away from it. And as for the explanation of the impossibility of the second division – that is, the body being at rest in pre-eternity – its aspect is: that if it were so, it would never accept movement ever, because its rest on this assumption is eternal, and the eternal does not accept non-existence; for if it accepted non-existence, its existence would need a specific [cause], due to its permissibility then, so it would be originated (muḥdath), but it was assumed eternal – this is an inconceivable contradiction. And the proof of the acceptance of rest's non-existence is our observation of movement in some bodies, and that necessitates the permissibility of movement for all bodies, due to their similarity. And as for the explanation of the impossibility of the first division – that is, the body being in motion in pre-eternity – its aspect is what you already know from the second division, and this division is increased by another aspect of impossibility: that the reality of movement is not conceivable as eternal, because it is the transition from [؟] a space to a space, thus it is only an accident (ṭāri'a) upon the body, and it is necessary that before its existence there be the being in the space from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be imagined to be an accident, nor that something else precede its existence. Therefore, by this certain proof it has emerged for you: that the entire world is originated (ḥādith), from its Throne to its carpet; it is not conceivable in reason that anything from it be eternal.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage proves the contingency (ḥudūth) of all bodies by demonstrating that neither motion nor rest can be eternal. The author structures the proof by considering three possible states of a body in pre-eternity: (1) moving, (2) at rest, or (3) neither. He begins by stating that a body cannot be conceived without one of these three states. The third case (neither moving nor at rest) is dismissed as obviously impossible. He then argues that eternal rest is impossible because if rest were eternal, the body could never move (since the eternal cannot cease). He supports this with an empirical observation: we see bodies moving, so movement is possible for bodies, and due to their similarity, it must be possible for all. Similarly, eternal motion is impossible for two reasons: first, the same argument as for rest (if motion were eternal, rest would never occur), and second, because motion is inherently an accident (ṭāri'a) that entails a prior state, which contradicts eternality. The proof concludes decisively that every body is originated, and nothing in the world can be eternal.

Key Terms
جرم (jirm)
حركة (ḥaraka)
سكون (sukūn)
قديم (qadīm)
حادث (ḥādith)
برهان قطعي (burhān qaṭ‘ī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument only proves the impossibility of eternal rest or motion, but not that bodies themselves cannot be eternal.
Correction: The author clarifies that bodies are inseparable from these states; their impossibility entails the impossibility of eternal bodies.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider it impossible for a body to be at rest eternally?
A) Because rest is a state that cannot exist without motion.
B) Because if rest were eternal, the body could never move, contradicting empirical evidence of movement.
C) Because rest is an accident that requires a subject.
D) Because eternal rest would mean the body is not in space.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of eternal motion or rest proves that every body is created, and nothing in the universe is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own body—every cell, every breath—is originated and contingent, not eternal, then what are you holding onto that you treat as if it will last forever? What would it mean to genuinely accept that you are completely dependent on the Creator every moment? Will you today, right now, turn from relying on your own strength to relying on Him?
?
The proof shows that everything from the Throne to the carpet is created. You have been given this life, this body, as a trust from Allah. Are you using it as if it is your own possession or as a loan that will be returned? What would change if you truly believed that not a single atom of your being is self-sufficient?
?
Sanusi's argument leaves no room for any part of the universe to be eternal. Does your heart still seek permanence in people, possessions, or status? These are all changing, moving, resting—all created. Will you let go of the illusion of permanence and anchor your hope only in the Eternal, the Self-Sufficient?

The Four Pillars of the Proof of the World's Origination
Sanusi enumerates the four pillars and explains their logical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four foundational pillars on which the proof of the world's origination (ḥudūth al-‘ālam) is built and why each is necessary.

Core Idea
The proof of the world's origination rests on four logical pillars that ensure a sound inference.

Sanusi lays out the four principles that form the backbone of the classical argument for the world's origin. Without each of these pillars, the proof would collapse. In this lesson, we will examine each pillar and see how they fit together to establish that the world must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Notice the four numbered pillars and the explanation that follows.
2
Identify the four pillars
List the four pillars as Sanusi enumerates them. Each is a necessary condition for the proof.
3
Understand the logical connection
The explanation shows why these four are needed: because the proof infers origination from an inseparable accident. Grasp why an accident must be established, why it must be originated, why bodies cannot be free from it, and why an infinite chain of accidents is impossible.
4
Reflect on the fourth pillar
The fourth pillar (impossibility of infinite regress) is often misunderstood. Note that it blocks an infinite series of originated events, not any infinite chain.
This addresses a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البرهان عندهم ينبني على إثباتِ أربعة أركان : الأول : إثبات زائد تنصفُ بِهِ الأجرام الثاني : إثبات حدوثِ ذلكَ الزائدِ . الثالثُ : إثباتُ كونِ الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد الرابع : إثباتُ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها . ووجه ابتناء حدوث العالم على هذه الأربعة الأصول (۲) : أَنَّكَ قد عرفت أنَّ دليله راجع إلى الاستدلالِ بحدوث أحد المتلازمين على حدوث الآخر : فاحتاجَ إذا إلى إثباتِ زائد على الأجرام ليُحكم عليه بالملازمة ؛ إذ الشيء لا يلزم نفسَهُ
And this proof, according to them, is built upon establishing four pillars: The first: establishing an accident (zāʾid) by which bodies are characterized. The second: establishing the origination of that accident. The third: establishing that bodies are not free from that accident. The fourth: establishing the impossibility of events that have no beginning. And the manner of building the origination of the world upon these four principles (2) is that you have known that its evidence reduces to inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things to the origination of the other: so it needed, then, to establish an accident upon bodies so that inseparability could be ruled upon it, since a thing does not necessitate itself.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four foundational principles (arkān) upon which the conclusive proof for the world's origination rests. First, one must establish an accident (zāʾid) that characterizes bodies. Second, one must establish that this accident is originated (ḥādith). Third, one must establish that bodies are never free from that accident. Fourth, one must establish the impossibility of an infinite series of originated events (istiḥālat ḥawādith lā awwala lahā). The author then explains why these four pillars are necessary: because the proof works by inferring the origination of one of two inseparable things (al-mutalāzimayn) from the origination of the other. To apply that, we need an accident that is inseparable from the body; without that accident, there is no inseparability. And since a thing does not necessitate itself, the accident must be something additional. Thus the four pillars ensure that the logical structure of the proof is sound: bodies have an inseparable accident, that accident is originated, so bodies must be originated as well, provided that an infinite regress of originated accidents is impossible.

Key Terms
الأركان الأربعة
زائد
حدوث
لا تنفك
استحالة حوادث لا أول لها
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth pillar denies any infinite series whatsoever.
Correction: It denies specifically an infinite regress of originated events (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā). Other forms of infinite series (like causal chains in some other contexts) are not necessarily denied here.

Checkpoint
How many pillars does the proof of the world's origination rest upon according to Sanusi?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The four pillars logically ensure that the world must have a beginning because its inseparable accidents are originated and cannot go back infinitely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that you, and everything around you, are originated and dependent on something else. What would change if you lived today with the constant awareness that you are not self-sufficient? What specific action can you take to acknowledge your contingency before the Creator?
?
The fourth pillar says there cannot be an infinite regress of events. Consider the endless cycles of your daily routine—work, eat, sleep—do you ever reflect that this rhythm is itself a sign that you are not eternal? How can you break the cycle of heedlessness today?
?
The proof requires establishing an accident that characterizes bodies. What 'accidents' of your own character—like anger, pride, or love—reveal that you are constantly changing and therefore not self-subsisting? How does recognizing your own changeability move you to turn to the Unchanging?

The Four Pillars of the Proof for Temporal Origination
Sanusi enumerates the four pillars of the proof and explains the necessity of the first three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four principles (arks) that the proof for the temporality of the world is built upon, and the rationale for the first three pillars.

Core Idea
The proof for the world's temporality rests on four pillars, starting with establishing accidents in bodies.

Sanusi begins by outlining the foundational pillars of the famous argument for the temporal origination of the world. This lesson presents those pillars and explains why the first three are necessary to establish the concomitant relationship between bodies and their accidents. The fourth pillar will be addressed in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the four pillars
Read the Arabic passage that lists the four pillars. Notice how each pillar is introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc. This is the structural framework of the entire proof.
The pillars are: (1) establishing an accident, (2) its temporality, (3) bodies' inseparability from it, (4) impossibility of infinite regress.
2
Understand the need for the first pillar
The proof works by inferring from one concomitant to another. A thing cannot necessitate itself, so we need an accident (za'id) over and above the body to serve as the basis for the logical relationship.
The author explains that the proof reduces to 'inferring from the temporal origination of one of two concomitants to the temporal origination of the other.'
3
Comprehend the second and third pillars
The accident must be temporally originated (second pillar), and bodies must never be free from such accidents (third pillar). This establishes the concomitance: if the accident is temporally originated, the body must be as well.
Sanusi notes that 'world' here means substances (jawahir), a metonymy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البرهان عندهم ينبني على إثباتِ أربعة أركان : الأول : إثبات زائد تنصفُ بِهِ الأجرام الثاني : إثبات حدوثِ ذلكَ الزائدِ . الثالثُ : إثباتُ كونِ الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد الرابع : إثباتُ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها • ووجه ابتناء حدوث العالم على هذه الأربعة الأصول (۲) : أَنَّكَ قد عرفت أنَّ دليله راجع إلى الاستدلالِ بحدوث أحد المتلازمين على حدوث الآخر : فاحتاجَ إذا إلى إثباتِ زائد على الأجرام ليُحكم عليه بالملازمة ؛ إذ الشيء لا يلزم نفسَهُ (۱) وكذلك ثبوتهما معاً ، وحكمه كنفيهما ؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين ، ولذلك استغنى عن ذكرها (۲) المراد بالعالم هنا : جواهره ؛ مجاز مرسل ؛ من إطلاق الكل على الجزء واحتاج إلى إثباتِ حدوثِ ذلكَ الزائدِ ؛ إِذْ بحدوثِهِ يُستدَلُّ على حدوثِ العالم . عن ذلكَ الزائد ؛ ليُثبِتَ واحتاج إلى إثباتِ كون الأجرامِ لا تنفك التلازم بينهما ، حتى يلزمَ مِنْ حدوثِ ذلك الزائد حدوث الأجرام . واحتاج إلى إثباتِ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ؛ لأنَّهُ بعدما ثبَتَتْ لنا الأصول الثلاثةُ ، وأردنا أن نستدلَّ بحدوث ذلكَ الزائد على حدوثِ الأجرام الملازمة له .. اعترض علينا الخصم ؛ بأَنَّهُ لا يلزم ذلك إلا لو كانَتْ أفراد ذلكَ الزائدِ الحادثة لها مبدأ ، قال : ونحن نوافق على حدوثها ، لكن نقولُ : لا أَوَّلَ لها ؛ فالفَلَكُ مثلاً - وإنْ لازمته حركات حادثة - لا يلزم حدوثه إلا لو كانَ لجملة تلك الحركاتِ مبدأ ؛ ليلزمَ مِنْ قِدَمِهِ وجودُ المحالِ ؛ وهو كون الجزم عارياً عن الحركة والسكون ، أما إذا كانَتِ الحركاتُ لا أَوَّلَ لها فلا يلزمُ ذلكَ ! فهذا وجه احتياج البرهان إلى هذه الأربعة الأصول .
And this proof, according to them, is built upon establishing four pillars: First: Establishing an accident (za'id) that qualifies bodies. Second: Establishing the temporal origination of that accident. Third: Establishing that bodies are never free from that accident. Fourth: Establishing the impossibility of events without a beginning. • And the reason for the construction of the proof of the world's temporal origination upon these four principles (2) is that you have known that its proof reduces to inferring from the temporal origination of one of two concomitants to the temporal origination of the other. So it needed, therefore, the establishment of an accident over and above the bodies to rule upon it with concomitance; because a thing does not necessitate itself (1). And likewise their simultaneous existence, and its ruling is like their non-existence, due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories, and therefore he dispensed with mentioning them. (2) What is meant by 'world' here is its substances; a metonymy; from applying the whole to the part. And he needed to establish the temporal origination of that accident, since by its temporal origination the temporal origination of the world is inferred. And he needed to establish that bodies are never free from that accident, to prove the concomitance between them, so that from the temporal origination of that accident the temporal origination of the bodies follows. And he needed to establish the impossibility of events without a beginning; because after the three principles are established for us, and we want to infer from the temporal origination of that accident to the temporal origination of the bodies that are concomitant with it... the opponent objects to us, saying: That does not follow unless the individual events of that accident have a beginning. He says: And we agree with their temporal origination, but we say: They have no beginning. So the celestial sphere, for example—even if it is accompanied by temporally originated motions—its temporal origination does not follow unless the sum of those motions has a beginning; so that from its pre-eternity there would follow the existence of an impossibility, namely the body being devoid of motion and rest. But if the motions have no beginning, that does not follow! So this is the reason the proof needs these four principles.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of the proof for the temporal origination of the world. The author states that the proof is built on four pillars (arkan). First, establishing an accident (za'id) that qualifies bodies, i.e., that bodies have accidents. Second, establishing that those accidents are temporally originated. Third, establishing that bodies are inseparable from accidents. Fourth, establishing that an infinite regress of events is impossible. The author then explains why each pillar is necessary. The proof works by inferring the temporal origination of bodies from the temporal origination of their accidents because of the concomitant relationship. A thing cannot necessitate itself, so we need an accident to be the cause. The opponent might object that even if accidents are temporally originated, they might have no beginning (infinite regress), in which case the body could still be eternal. To block this, the fourth principle is needed. The author uses the example of the celestial sphere (falak) and its motions: if the motions have no beginning, the sphere could be eternal while always having motions. Thus, to prove the sphere's temporality, one must show that the motions have a beginning.

الملازمة

Key Takeaway
The first three pillars establish the concomitant link between bodies and their accidents, showing that if accidents are temporal, bodies must be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the proof shows that every body you see is temporally originated because it is never free from temporal accidents, then why are you so attached to your body and this world as if they will last forever? How can you realign your priorities today in light of this certain temporality?
?
The third pillar establishes that bodies are never free from accidents—meaning you are always changing, always dependent. Does your daily life reflect a deep awareness of your constant need for the Creator every moment, or do you behave as if you are self-sufficient? What one habit can you start to remind yourself of this dependency?
?
The proof rests on the impossibility of an infinite regress, forcing the mind to accept a First Cause. Have you truly let this truth settle in your heart, or do you still live as if the chain of causes can explain everything without needing Allah? What would change in your life if you fully internalized that Allah is the only necessary Being?

The Fourth Pillar: Refuting Infinite Regress
Sanusi explains the necessity of the fourth pillar and refutes the objection of an infinite regress of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth pillar—the impossibility of an infinite regress of events—is necessary to complete the proof for the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
Even if accidents are temporal, an infinite regress of them would allow a body to be eternal—so the fourth pillar blocks this.

After establishing the first three pillars, Sanusi introduces the opponent's objection: accidents might be temporally originated but have no beginning. This would allow the world to be eternal. The fourth pillar, the impossibility of an infinite regress of events, is needed to refute this. This lesson explains that objection and the reasoning behind the fourth pillar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the fourth pillar
Read the passage starting from 'واحتاج إلى إثباتِ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها'. Notice how Sanusi presents the objection and his response.
The passage includes the example of the celestial sphere (falak) and its motions.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent agrees that accidents are temporally originated but argues they may have no beginning—i.e., an infinite series. If so, the body could be eternal while always having accidents.
This objection threatens the entire proof, because the third pillar alone doesn't guarantee that the series of accidents has a starting point.
3
See how the fourth pillar resolves it
Sanusi uses the example of the celestial sphere: if its motions have no beginning, its temporal origination does not follow. Only by establishing that an infinite regress is impossible can we conclude that the series must have a first event, thus forcing the body to be temporal.
The fourth pillar is the principle 'impossibility of events without a beginning' (استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And this proof, according to them, is built upon establishing four pillars: First: Establishing an accident (za'id) that qualifies bodies. Second: Establishing the temporal origination of that accident. Third: Establishing that bodies are never free from that accident. Fourth: Establishing the impossibility of events without a beginning. • And the reason for the construction of the proof of the world's temporal origination upon these four principles (2) is that you have known that its proof reduces to inferring from the temporal origination of one of two concomitants to the temporal origination of the other. Misconception & Correction

?

?

When you reflect on your own existence, do you truly live with the conviction that you were created ex nihilo by a Creator, or do your actions sometimes betray a sense that the world has always been as it is? Four Sub-Principles for the Second Principle
Sanusi enumerates four sub-principles for the second principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that proving accidents are temporally originated requires establishing four specific sub-principles.

Core Idea
Four refutations are needed to prove accidents are created.

Sanusi explains that the second principle—the temporal origination of accidents—is not self-evident; it depends on four other principles. These principles refute alternative theories about accidents and establish a key attribute of the Eternal. This passage shows the careful framework needed for the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four sub-principles
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the four numbered sub-principles that Sanusi lists as necessary for proving the temporal origination of accidents.
The four sub-principles are: refuting accident's self-subsistence, transference, latency/manifestation, and establishing impossibility of annihilation of the Eternal.
2
Recognize the pattern: three refutations and one positive assertion
Notice that the first three sub-principles begin with 'Ibtal' (refuting) while the fourth begins with 'Ithbat' (establishing). Consider why a positive proof is needed alongside the refutations.
The fourth sub-principle is not a refutation but a positive statement about the Eternal.
3
Consider why each sub-principle is necessary
Reflect on how each sub-principle removes a possible objection to the temporal origination of accidents. For example, if accidents could subsist by themselves, they might not need bodies. If they could transfer, they might not arise anew. If they could be latent, their appearance might not be temporal origination. And if the Eternal could cease, then the existence of the world might not require a creator.
These sub-principles together ensure that accidents are dependent, arise in bodies, and that the Eternal is permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الثاني منها ـ وهو حدوث الزائدِ ـ يتوقَّفُ أيضاً على معرفة أربعة أصول : الأَوَّلُ : إبطال قيام ذلكَ الزائدِ بنفسِهِ الثاني : إبطال انتقاله الثالثُ : إبطالُ كُمونِهِ وظُهورِه . الرابع : إثبات استحالةِ عُدْمِ القديمِ
Then the second principle from them—namely the temporal origination of the accident—also depends on knowing four principles: First: Refuting the accident's subsistence by itself. Second: Refuting its transference. Third: Refuting its being latent and manifest. Fourth: Establishing the impossibility of the annihilation of the eternal.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage explains that the second pillar of the overall proof—the temporal origination of accidents—itself depends on four sub-principles. The author lists them: 1) Refuting that accidents can subsist by themselves (i.e., they are not self-subsistent entities but inhere in bodies). 2) Refuting that accidents can transfer from one body to another. 3) Refuting that accidents can be latent (kumun) and then manifest, meaning they don't pre-exist in a hidden state. 4) Establishing that it is impossible for the eternal (God) to cease to exist. These sub-principles together support the conclusion that accidents are temporally originated and dependent on bodies.

Key Terms
الأصل الثاني
قيامه بنفسه
انتقاله
كمونه وظهوره
عدم القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That proving the temporal origination of accidents is straightforward and does not require refuting alternative views.
Correction: In reality, one must first refute the possibilities that accidents could be self-subsistent, transferable, or latent/manifest, and also establish the impossibility of the Eternal's annihilation.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth sub-principle needed to prove the temporal origination of accidents?
A) Refuting that accidents can be latent and manifest.
B) Refuting that accidents can transfer.
C) Refuting that accidents subsist by themselves.
D) Establishing the impossibility of the annihilation of the eternal.

Key Takeaway
The temporal origination of accidents cannot be proven without first refuting that accidents are self-subsistent, transferable, or latent/manifest, and establishing that the Eternal cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a change in your mood from sadness to happiness, do you take a moment to recognize that this new accident is created by God, not simply transferred from elsewhere? What false assumption about that change are you prone to making, and how does this lesson call you to replace it with tawḥīd?
?
The fourth sub-principle affirms that God cannot cease to exist. Are there moments in your life when you live as if God's presence or favor could be lost? How can you reshape your daily trust in Him based on this eternal permanence?
?
Which of the three refutations (self-subsistence, transference, or latency) challenges a belief you might unconsciously hold about how God's power operates? Write down one area where you can actively refute that wrong thought starting today.

The Need for a Fourth Principle in the Proof of the World's Createdness
Sanusi explains why the proof requires four principles after an objection about infinite accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof for the world's createdness requires a fourth principle to counter the objection of an infinite series of accidents.

Core Idea
Proving the world's createdness needs four principles, not three, to block the infinite regress objection.

After establishing three principles to prove the world's createdness, an opponent raises a subtle objection: each accident is created, but the series of accidents could be beginningless. To meet this challenge, a fourth principle is needed. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's reasoning and shows how the proof is completed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three initial principles
Recall the three principles established earlier: the createdness of the additional thing, the inseparability between bodies and that thing, and the impossibility of events without a beginning. These three seem sufficient, but the opponent objects.
The objection focuses on the third principle: the opponent accepts individual createdness but denies a beginning for the series.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the opponent's objection using the celestial sphere example.
Pay attention to the logical flow: from established principles to the objection to the need for a fourth principle.
3
Analyze the celestial sphere example
The opponent says the celestial sphere has ever-occurring motions. If those motions have no beginning, the sphere could be eternal despite perpetual motion, because the impossibility only arises if the series has a beginning.
This example shows why the third principle alone is insufficient—it must be shown that an infinite series is impossible.
4
Identify the fourth principle
The fourth principle is the impossibility of an infinite series of events (accidents). This blocks the opponent's objection and completes the proof for the world's createdness.
Sanusi concludes that the proof requires four principles, not three, to be sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتاج إلى إثباتِ حدوثِ ذلكَ الزائدِ ؛ إِذْ بحدوثِهِ يُستدَلُّ على حدوثِ العالم . عن ذلكَ الزائدِ ؛ ليُثبِتَ و〔؟〕 واختاج إلى إثباتِ كون الأجرامِ لا تنفك التلازم بينهما ، حتى يلزمَ مِنْ حدوثِ ذلك الزائد حدوث الأجرام . واحتاج إلى إثباتِ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ؛ لأنَّهُ بعدما ثبَتَتْ لنا الأصول الثلاثةُ ، وأردنا أن نستدلَّ بحدوث ذلكَ الزائد على حدوثِ الأجرام الملازمة له .. اعترض علينا الخصم ؛ بأَنَّهُ لا يلزم ذلك إلا لو كانَتْ أفراد ذلكَ الزائدِ الحادثة لها مبدأ ، قال : ونحن نوافق على حدوثها ، لكن نقولُ : لا أَوَّلَ لها ؛ فالفَلَكُ مثلاً - وإنْ لازمته حركات حادثة - لا يلزم حدوثه إلا لو كانَ لجملة تلك الحركاتِ مبدأ ؛ ليلزمَ مِنْ قِدَمِهِ وجودُ المحالِ ؛ وهو كون الجزم عارياً عن الحركة والسكون ، أما إذا كانَتِ الحركاتُ لا أَوَّلَ لها فلا يلزمُ ذلكَ ! فهذا وجه احتياج البرهان إلى هذه الأربعة الأصول .
And he needed to prove the createdness of that additional thing, for by its createdness one infers the createdness of the world. [؟] And he needed to establish that bodies are inseparable from it, so that from the createdness of that additional thing the createdness of bodies follows necessarily. And he needed to establish the impossibility of events that have no beginning; because after the three principles were established for us, and we wanted to infer by the createdness of that additional thing the createdness of the bodies concomitant with it, the opponent objected to us: that does not follow unless the individuals of that additional thing that occur have a beginning. He said: We agree with you on their createdness, but we say: They have no beginning. For example, the celestial sphere - even if it is inseparably accompanied by occurring motions - its createdness does not follow unless the totality of those motions has a beginning, so that from its eternity the existence of the impossible would follow, which is that the body be devoid of motion and rest. But if the motions have no beginning, that does not follow! So this is the aspect of the proof's need for these four principles.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage demonstrates why the proof for the world's createdness requires four principles, specifically after an objection regarding an infinite series of accidents. The author states that one needs to prove three things initially (the three principles): (1) the createdness of the additional thing (al-za'id), (2) the inseparability (talazum) between bodies and that additional thing, and (3) the impossibility of events without a beginning. However, when using the createdness of the additional thing to infer the createdness of bodies, the opponent objects: the inference only holds if the series of accidents has a beginning. The opponent concedes the createdness of each individual accident but denies the series has a first, thus avoiding the conclusion. The author uses the example of the celestial sphere (falak) and its motions: if motions are infinite and without a beginning, then the sphere could be eternal despite perpetual motion, because the impossibility of a body being devoid of motion and rest only arises if the series has a beginning. Therefore, a fourth principle is needed: establishing that an infinite series of accidents is impossible. This shows why the proof must include four principles, not just three.

الأصول الثلاثة
استحالة حوادث لا أول لها
التلازم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving each accident's createdness suffices to prove the world's createdness without addressing the possibility of an infinite series.
Correction: The opponent's objection shows that even if each accident is created, an infinite series could be eternal, so a fourth principle is needed to rule out a beginningless series.

Checkpoint

A) Because the opponent denied the createdness of accidents.
B) Because the opponent argued the series of accidents could be infinite, so the createdness of individual accidents does not entail a beginning for the body.
C) Because the three initial principles were insufficient to prove the existence of God.
D) Because the proof required establishing the eternity of the world.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the world's createdness requires a fourth principle—the impossibility of an infinite series of accidents—to block the objection that the series could be beginningless.

The Four Principles for Proving an Accident is Created
Sanusi enumerates and explains the four principles needed to prove the createdness of an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational principles that must be established to prove that an accident (al-za'id) is created, and how these principles are applied in two distinct inference strategies.

Core Idea
Proving an accident is created requires eliminating alternative explanations and ruling out eternity.

Sanusi now delves into the second principle of createdness—the createdness of an accident. He explains that this depends on four foundational principles. These principles systematically rule out any possibility that the accident could be eternal or self-sustaining. Understanding these principles is essential before grasping the actual proof of an accident's temporal origin.

Guided Path
1
Meet the four principles
Read the list of four principles carefully. Each one denies a specific alternative that would prevent us from concluding that the accident is created.
The first three concern the accident itself; the fourth concerns the eternal.
2
Two inference methods
Notice that the createdness of an accident can be inferred in two ways: by the occurrence of existence after non-existence, or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence. The first three principles are needed for both.
3
The crucial fourth principle
Understand why the fourth principle is necessary only for the second inference method. Without it, one could argue that the accident is eternal and simply ceased to exist, which does not entail a beginning.
This guards against a subtle objection.
4
Reflect on the logic
Put all four principles together and see how they collaboratively establish that the accident cannot be eternal, self-subsistent, transmitted, or latent, forcing the conclusion that it is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصلُ الثاني منها ـ وهو حدوث الزائدِ ـ يتوقَّفُ أيضاً على معرفة أربعة أصول :
الأَوَّلُ : إبطال قيام ذلكَ الزائدِ بنفسِهِ
الثاني : إبطال انتقاله
الثالثُ : إبطالُ كُمونِهِ وظُهورِه .
الرابع : إثبات استحالةِ عُدْمِ القديمِ
ووجه توقُفِ حدوثِ العَرَضِ الزائدِ على هذه الأصولِ : أَنَّ جهة الاستدلال على حدوثِهِ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ بطروء الوجودِ بعد العدم ، أو بطروء العدم بعد الوجودِ
•
وتحقيق الاستدلال بطروء الوجود يستدعي ثلاثة أمورٍ ؛ وهي ما عدا إثبات استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ (1) [في هامش ( هـ ) ( التي هي : قيام العرض بنفسه ، وانتقاله ، والكمون والظهور له ؛ إذ لا يعرف أن الوجود طرأ - أي : حدث ـ بعد أن لم يكن إلا بعد استحالة أنه كان قائماً بنفسه ، أو أنه انتقل ، أو أنه كان كامناً مثلاً ، ومثله يقال في طرفي العدم )]، وحينئذ يلزم الحدوث ، وتحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم يستدعي تلك الأمور الثلاثةَ ، وحينئذ يتحقق العدم . ثم لما لم يكن العدمُ اللاحق للوجودِ هو نفس الحدوثِ .. احتيجَ إلى بيانِ استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ (2) [في هامش ( هـ ) : أي : أنَّ العدم الطارىء على العرض ليس هو نفس الحدوث ؛ إذ الحدوث : وجود بعد عدم ، والمذكور هنا إنما هو العدم بعد الوجود ، فلو أقمنا دليلاً على حدوث العرض وقلنا : لأنه عدم بعد أن كان موجوداً . . فربَّما قيل : إنَّ العدم المذكور غير الحدوث ، فلا ينافي القدم واحتيج إلى بيان أنَّ العدم المذكور لا يطرأ على القديم .]؛ ليلزمَ مِنْ طروء العدمِ على الوجودِ سبقُ العدم عليه الذي هو معنى الحدوثِ
وبيانُ هذا الكلام : أن نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء الوجودِ للعرض كالحركة والسكون مثلاً : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن طارئاً لكان موجوداً
Then the second principle among them - which is the createdness of the additional thing - depends also on knowing four principles:
The first: invalidating that this additional thing subsists by itself.
The second: invalidating its transmission.
The third: invalidating its latency and manifestation.
The fourth: establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal.
And the aspect of the dependence of the createdness of the additional accident on these principles is: the direction of inference regarding its createdness is either by the occurrence of existence after non-existence, or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence.
•
And the verification of inference by the occurrence of existence requires three matters; they are other than establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal (1) [In margin (H): which are: the subsistence of the accident by itself, its transmission, and its latency and manifestation; for it is not known that existence occurred - i.e., happened - after it was not, except after the impossibility that it subsisted by itself, or that it transmitted, or that it was latent, etc., and similar is said regarding the two sides of non-existence.], and then createdness is entailed. And the verification of inference by the occurrence of non-existence requires those three matters, and then non-existence is realized. Then, since the non-existence that follows existence is not itself createdness, it was needed to establish the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal (2) [In margin (H): meaning that the non-existence that occurs to the accident is not itself createdness; for createdness is existence after non-existence, while what is mentioned here is only non-existence after existence; so if we mounted a proof for the createdness of the accident and said: because it non-exists after having existed, it might be said: that non-existence is not createdness, so it does not contradict eternity, and it was needed to show that that non-existence does not occur to the eternal.]; so that from the occurrence of non-existence after existence, the precedence of non-existence over it, which is the meaning of createdness, follows. And the explanation of this statement: that we say in verifying the inference by the occurrence of existence for the accident, like motion and rest for example: if it were not occurring, it would be existent.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage enumerates and explains the four principles required to prove the createdness of an accident (al-za'id). The author states that the second principle from earlier (i.e., the createdness of the accident) depends on knowing four principles: (1) invalidating that the accident subsists by itself (i.e., it is not a substance), (2) invalidating its transmission (i.e., it cannot move from one locus to another), (3) invalidating its latency and manifestation (i.e., it is not a potentiality that becomes actual), and (4) establishing the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal (al-qadim). The reason for this dependence is that the inference for createdness can proceed in two ways: either by the occurrence of existence after non-existence, or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence. For the first way (existence after non-existence), we need only the first three principles (excluding the fourth) because if we can show the accident was not self-subsistent, not transmitted, and not latent, then its existence must be a new occurrence, implying createdness. For the second way (non-existence after existence), we also need the first three to establish that non-existence occurs to it, but additionally we need the fourth principle. This is because non-existence after existence is not the same as createdness (which is existence after non-existence). If we merely say the accident non-exists after existing, one could object that this non-existence does not entail a beginning; the accident could be eternal and then cease to exist. To avoid that, we must prove that non-existence cannot occur to the eternal being (God's attribute?), i.e., that the eternal is not subject to non-existence, so if non-existence occurs to the accident, it must be preceded by existence, and that existence must have begun, giving the true meaning of createdness. The marginal notes clarify the three matters (first three principles) and explain the need for the fourth. This is a precise logical analysis of the epistemological requirements for proving that an accident is created.

Key Terms

إبطال قيامه بنفسه
إبطال انتقاله
إبطال كمونه وظهوره
استحالة عدم القديم
طروء الوجود
طروء العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving an accident's non-existence after existence is enough to prove its createdness, because ceasing to exist implies a beginning.
Correction: Non-existence after existence does not entail a beginning; an eternal thing could cease to exist. To prove createdness from non-existence after existence, we must also prove that non-existence cannot occur to the eternal (principle 4), ensuring the accident is not eternal.

Checkpoint
Why is the fourth principle (establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal) needed when inferring createdness through the occurrence of non-existence after existence?
A) Because non-existence after existence is itself the definition of createdness.
B) Because without it, one could argue the accident is eternal and then ceases to exist, which does not entail a beginning.
C) Because the first three principles only apply to substances.
D) Because the eternal cannot have accidents.

Key Takeaway
The four principles systematically eliminate all alternative possibilities for an accident (self-subsistence, transmission, latency) and rule out eternity, leaving createdness as the only coherent explanation.

?
