Proof via Occurrence of Existence
Sanusi presents the first method: elimination of three possibilities to prove occurrence is new.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the occurrence of existence after non-existence proves an accident is temporal, by eliminating the possibilities of self-subsistence, transfer, and latency.

Core Idea
An accident's existence must be new; it cannot be latent, transferred, or self-subsisting.

Sanusi begins the first of two parallel arguments for the temporality of accidents. He focuses on the inference from the appearance (turu') of existence. To show that this appearance is truly a renewal after non-existence, he eliminates three alternatives: that the accident was self-subsisting, that it transferred from another locus, or that it was latent in the same locus. This lesson walks through that elimination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic first, then the translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi presents three alternatives and then shows why each is impossible for an accident.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three ways an accident could have existed earlier: (1) self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihī), (2) transferred from another locus (intiqāl), (3) latent in the same locus (kumūn). Find each in the text.
3
Understand why each is impossible
An accident cannot be self-subsisting because it needs a substrate. It cannot transfer between substrates because that would make it independent of a locus. It cannot be latent because that implies prior hidden existence, which is not true generation.
The commentary explains the reasoning behind the impossibility.
4
Connect to the conclusion
With the three possibilities invalidated, the only remaining explanation is that the accident's existence is a true renewal after non-existence – this is temporality (huduth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه توقُفِ حدوثِ العَرَضِ الزائدِ على هذه الأصولِ : أَنَّ جهة الاستدلال على حدوثِهِ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ بطروء الوجودِ بعد العدم ، أو بطروء العدم بعد الوجودِ . وتحقِيقُ الاستدلال بطروء الوجود يستدعي ثلاثة أمورٍ ؛ وهي ما عدا إثبات استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ ، وحينئذ يلزم الحدوث ، وتحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم يستدعي تلك الأمور الثلاثةَ ، وحينئذ يتحقق العدم . ثم لما لم يكن العدمُ اللاحق للوجودِ هو نفس الحدوثِ .. احتيجَ إلى بيانِ استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ ؛ ليلزمَ مِنْ طروء العدمِ على الوجودِ سبقُ العدم عليه الذي هو معنى الحدوثِ . وبيانُ هذا الكلام : أن نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء الوجودِ للعرض كالحركة والسكون مثلاً : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن طارئاً لكان موجوداً قبلَ هذه الحال التي شهدنا طروءَهُ فيها ، ولو كان موجوداً قبلها لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ في محلّ فهو إمَّا هذا المشاهَدُ طريانُهُ فيهِ أو غيرُه ؛ فإن كانَ هذا فقد كانَ كامناً فيه ، وإنْ كانَ غيرَهُ فلا يصل إليهِ إلا بانتقال مِنْ غيرِهِ إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غير محلّ قبل أن يطراً على هذا .. فهو إذا قد قامَ بنفسِهِ ، فتوقَّفَتِ الدلالة على حدوثِ العرض بطروئِهِ على إبطال هذه الأقسام الثلاثةِ ؛ فحينئذ يستبينُ : أَنَّ الطروءَ المشاهَدَ هو تجدد بعد عدمِ ، وهو معنى الحدوث . وكذا نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم للعَرَضِ بعد وجودِهِ : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن قد عُدِمَ لكانَ باقياً ، وهو إما أن يبقى في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإن بقي في محلّ فهو إما في هذا المحل أو غيره ؛ فإنْ كانَ في هذا المحل فهو كامنُ ، وإنْ كانَ في غيره فلا يصل إليه إلا بانتقال مِنْ هذا إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غيرِ محلّ فقد قامَ بنفسِهِ ؛ فإذا بطلَتْ هذه الأقسام الثلاثةُ تُحقِّقَ حِينَئِذٍ طروءُ العدمِ . لكن يُقالُ : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ طروءَ العدم بعد الوجود يستلزم الحدوث الذي هو سبقُ العدم على الوجودِ ؟ ولِمَ لا يُقالُ : إِنَّ هذا العَرَضَ قديم ثم طرأ عليهِ العدمُ ؟ فنجيب حينئذٍ : بأنَّهُ لو كان قديماً لم يجز عدمه ، ونبرهن على ذلك ، وإذا تُحقِّقَ بطروء العدم أنَّه لم يكن قديماً لزِمَ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ؛ وهو المطلوب
The reason for the dependence of the temporality (huduth) of the accident (al-'arad) upon these principles is that the direction of inference upon its temporality is either by the occurrence (turu') of existence (wujud) after non-existence ('adam), or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence. The verification of the inference by the occurrence of existence requires three matters, besides proving the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal (qadim), and then temporality follows. The verification of the inference by the occurrence of non-existence requires those three matters, and then non-existence is realized. Then, since the non-existence subsequent to existence is not itself temporality, it was needed to state the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal, so that from the occurrence of non-existence upon existence follows the precedence of non-existence over it, which is the meaning of temporality. The explanation of this statement is that we say in the verification of the inference by the occurrence of existence for an accident, like motion and rest for example: If it were not occurring, it would have existed before this state in which we witnessed its occurrence. And if it existed before it, it would not be free from being either in a locus (mahall) or not. If it is in a locus, it is either this locus in which its occurrence is witnessed or another. If it is this, then it was latent (kāmin) in it; if it is another, then it would not reach it except by transfer (intiqāl) from the other to it. And if it were not in a locus before it occurred upon this, then it would be self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihī). So the indication of the accident's temporality by its occurrence depends on invalidating these three divisions; then it becomes clear that the witnessed occurrence is a renewal after non-existence, which is the meaning of temporality. Similarly, we say in the verification of the inference by the occurrence of non-existence for an accident after its existence: If it had not ceased to exist, it would have remained. And it is either that it remains in a locus or not. If it remains in a locus, it is either this locus or another. If it is in this locus, it is latent; if it is in another, it would not reach it except by transfer from this to it. And if it were not in a locus, it would be self-subsisting. So when these three divisions are invalidated, then the occurrence of non-existence is realized. But it is said: Why do you say that the occurrence of non-existence after existence entails temporality, which is the precedence of non-existence over existence? And why not say that this accident is eternal (qadīm) and then non-existence occurred upon it? So we answer then: Because if it were eternal, its non-existence would not be permissible, and we will prove that. And when it is realized by the occurrence of non-existence that it was not eternal, it necessarily follows that it is temporal (hādith); and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a proof: Sanusi explains why proving the temporality (huduth) of an accident (al-'arad) hinges on rejecting three possibilities. He presents two parallel methods of inference, each ending with the same structure. He then addresses an objection.

The passage begins by stating that there are two ways to infer the temporality of an accident: either by the occurrence of existence after non-existence (i.e., it comes into being), or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence (i.e., it perishes). Both methods require three preliminary matters, which are implicitly the elimination of self-subsistence, transfer, and latency. Additionally, the second method needs a separate proof that the eternal cannot cease to exist, because simple non-existence after existence does not automatically mean it was preceded by non-existence (i.e., it could be eternal and then vanish).

Sanusi then elaborates the first method: If an accident (like motion) were not occurring (i.e., newly appearing), it would have existed before the moment we observe it. If it existed earlier, it must either have been in a locus (substrate) or not. If in a locus, either the same locus (making it latent, kāmin) or another (requiring transfer, intiqāl). If not in a locus, it would be self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihī). All three possibilities are impossible for an accident (an accident cannot exist without a locus, cannot transfer between loci, and cannot be latent in the sense of being hidden and then appearing). Thus, the only option is that it is truly new—occurrence after non-existence, which is temporality.

The second method is analogous: If an accident had not ceased to exist, it would remain. To explain its ceasing, we again consider three possibilities: remaining in a locus (either the same or different) or being self-subsisting. All are impossible, so non-existence occurs. However, an objection arises: non-existence after existence does not directly prove temporality, because one could claim the accident is eternal but then ceases. Sanusi answers that an eternal being cannot cease to exist; if it could, it would not be eternal. Therefore, the occurrence of non-existence proves that it was not eternal, hence it must be temporal.

Thus, the proof for the temporality of accidents is complete, relying on elimination of impossible modes of existence.

عرض arad
طروء turu'
وجود wujud
محل mahall
قيام بالنفس qiyam bil-nafs
انتقال intiqal
كمون kumun

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's first method, which of the following is NOT one of the three possibilities that must be eliminated to prove temporality?
A) The accident was self-subsisting
B) The accident was transferred from another locus
C) The accident was latent in the same locus
D) The accident was created ex nihilo

Key Takeaway
To prove an accident is temporal via occurrence of existence, you must show it was not self-subsisting, not transferred, and not latent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider one blessing you enjoy daily—like health, wealth, or a relationship. Do you treat it as if it has always been there, or as a fresh gift from God that could be taken away at any moment? How would your gratitude change if you truly saw it as a brand-new accident every moment?
?
If every accident that occurs in your life—every thought, every feeling, every circumstance—is genuinely new and not just a manifestation of something else, then you are constantly being created anew. How does that reality confront your sense of control over your own life?
?
Sanusi eliminates the possibility that an accident could be 'hidden' and then appear. Is there a sin or bad habit you have been treating as 'latent'—thinking it will just appear when you are not careful—rather than as a full, new action you are choosing in each moment? What would it mean to take full responsibility for that?

Proof via Occurrence of Non-Existence and Objection
Sanusi presents the second method and addresses an objection about the possibility of an eternal accident ceasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the occurrence of non-existence after existence also proves an accident's temporality, and why an objection that it could be an eternal that ceased is refuted.

Core Idea
Non-existence after existence proves temporality only because an eternal cannot cease to exist.

Sanusi now presents the second method to prove an accident is temporal: by observing that it ceases to exist. He argues that this ceasing can only be explained if the accident was temporal, not eternal. However, a potential objection arises: maybe the accident is eternal and then simply disappears. Sanusi answers by showing that an eternal being cannot undergo non-existence. This lesson unpacks that logical sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second method
Read the Arabic and translation for the second method. Notice it mirrors the first method but with non-existence.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states: Even if non-existence occurs after existence, why must the accident be temporal? Could it not be eternal and then cease? Identify the question in the text.
3
Learn the answer
Sanusi answers that if something is eternal, its non-existence is impossible. So if non-existence occurs, the thing cannot be eternal; it must be temporal.
This relies on the principle that the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot be subject to annihilation.
4
Connect both methods
Both methods converge on the same conclusion: accidents are temporal. The second method requires an extra premise about the impossibility of the eternal ceasing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه توقُفِ حدوثِ العَرَضِ الزائدِ على هذه الأصولِ : أَنَّ جهة الاستدلال على حدوثِهِ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ بطروء الوجودِ بعد العدم ، أو بطروء العدم بعد الوجودِ . وتحقِيقُ الاستدلال بطروء الوجود يستدعي ثلاثة أمورٍ ؛ وهي ما عدا إثبات استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ ، وحينئذ يلزم الحدوث ، وتحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم يستدعي تلك الأمور الثلاثةَ ، وحينئذ يتحقق العدم . ثم لما لم يكن العدمُ اللاحق للوجودِ هو نفس الحدوثِ .. احتيجَ إلى بيانِ استحالةِ عُدم القديمِ ؛ ليلزمَ مِنْ طروء العدمِ على الوجودِ سبقُ العدم عليه الذي هو معنى الحدوثِ . وبيانُ هذا الكلام : أن نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء الوجودِ للعرض كالحركة والسكون مثلاً : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن طارئاً لكان موجوداً قبلَ هذه الحال التي شهدنا طروءَهُ فيها ، ولو كان موجوداً قبلها لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ في محلّ فهو إمَّا هذا المشاهَدُ طريانُهُ فيهِ أو غيرُه ؛ فإن كانَ هذا فقد كانَ كامناً فيه ، وإنْ كانَ غيرَهُ فلا يصل إليهِ إلا بانتقال مِنْ غيرِهِ إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غير محلّ قبل أن يطراً على هذا .. فهو إذا قد قامَ بنفسِهِ ، فتوقَّفَتِ الدلالة على حدوثِ العرض بطروئِهِ على إبطال هذه الأقسام الثلاثةِ ؛ فحينئذ يستبينُ : أَنَّ الطروءَ المشاهَدَ هو تجدد بعد عدمِ ، وهو معنى الحدوث . وكذا نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم للعَرَضِ بعد وجودِهِ : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن قد عُدِمَ لكانَ باقياً ، وهو إما أن يبقى في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإن بقي في محلّ فهو إما في هذا المحل أو غيره ؛ فإنْ كانَ في هذا المحل فهو كامنُ ، وإنْ كانَ في غيره فلا يصل إليه إلا بانتقال مِنْ هذا إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غيرِ محلّ فقد قامَ بنفسِهِ ؛ فإذا بطلَتْ هذه الأقسام الثلاثةُ تُحقِّقَ حِينَئِذٍ طروءُ العدمِ . لكن يُقالُ : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ طروءَ العدم بعد الوجود يستلزم الحدوث الذي هو سبقُ العدم على الوجودِ ؟ ولِمَ لا يُقالُ : إِنَّ هذا العَرَضَ قديم ثم طرأ عليهِ العدمُ ؟ فنجيب حينئذٍ : بأنَّهُ لو كان قديماً لم يجز عدمه ، ونبرهن على ذلك ، وإذا تُحقِّقَ بطروء العدم أنَّه لم يكن قديماً لزِمَ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ؛ وهو المطلوب
The reason for the dependence of the temporality (huduth) of the accident (al-'arad) upon these principles is that the direction of inference upon its temporality is either by the occurrence (turu') of existence (wujud) after non-existence ('adam), or by the occurrence of non-existence after existence. The verification of the inference by the occurrence of existence requires three matters, besides proving the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal (qadim), and then temporality follows. The verification of the inference by the occurrence of non-existence requires those three matters, and then non-existence is realized. Then, since the non-existence subsequent to existence is not itself temporality, it was needed to state the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal, so that from the occurrence of non-existence upon existence follows the precedence of non-existence over it, which is the meaning of temporality. The explanation of this statement is that we say in the verification of the inference by the occurrence of existence for an accident, like motion and rest for example: If it were not occurring, it would have existed before this state in which we witnessed its occurrence. And if it existed before it, it would not be free from being either in a locus (mahall) or not. If it is in a locus, it is either this locus in which its occurrence is witnessed or another. If it is this, then it was latent (kāmin) in it; if it is another, then it would not reach it except by transfer (intiqāl) from the other to it. And if it were not in a locus before it occurred upon this, then it would be self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihī). So the indication of the accident's temporality by its occurrence depends on invalidating these three divisions; then it becomes clear that the witnessed occurrence is a renewal after non-existence, which is the meaning of temporality. Similarly, we say in the verification of the inference by the occurrence of non-existence for an accident after its existence: If it had not ceased to exist, it would have remained. And it is either that it remains in a locus or not. If it remains in a locus, it is either this locus or another. If it is in this locus, it is latent; if it is in another, it would not reach it except by transfer from this to it. And if it were not in a locus, it would be self-subsisting. So when these three divisions are invalidated, then the occurrence of non-existence is realized. But it is said: Why do you say that the occurrence of non-existence after existence entails temporality, which is the precedence of non-existence over existence? And why not say that this accident is eternal (qadīm) and then non-existence occurred upon it? So we answer then: Because if it were eternal, its non-existence would not be permissible, and we will prove that. And when it is realized by the occurrence of non-existence that it was not eternal, it necessarily follows that it is temporal (hādith); and that is what is sought.

If not in a locus, it would be self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihī). ?

?

Proof of Accident's Temporality Through Emergence
Sanusi proves the temporality of an accident by analyzing its observed emergence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses logical elimination to prove that an observed accident must be temporal (ḥādith) and not pre-existent.

Core Idea
An accident's emergence cannot be a re-emergence; therefore it is a new creation.

Sanusi presents a rigorous logical proof for the temporality of accidents. He considers all possible ways an accident could have existed before its observed emergence and shows each is impossible. The only remaining conclusion is that it is genuinely new, a renewal after non-existence. This argument by elimination is a hallmark of kalām reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical style.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage enumerates possibilities for the accident's state before emergence. Find each one: being in a locus (same or different) or not in a locus.
These are the three 'aqsām (divisions).
3
Understand why each is invalid
Reflect on why each possibility is impossible: latency (kumūn) is rejected, transfer is not observed, self-subsistence is impossible for accidents.
Sanusi implies these have been refuted elsewhere.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since all alternatives are impossible, the only logical conclusion is that the emergence is a renewal after non-existence, i.e., temporality (ḥudūth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبلَ هذه الحال التي شهدنا طروءَهُ فيها ، ولو كان موجوداً قبلها لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ في محلّ فهو إمَّا هذا المشاهَدُ طريانُهُ فيهِ أو غيرُه ؛ فإن كانَ هذا فقد كانَ كامناً فيه ، وإنْ كانَ غيرَهُ فلا يصل إليهِ إلا بانتقال مِنْ غيرِهِ إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غير محلّ قبل أن يطراً على هذا .. فهو إذا قد قامَ بنفسِهِ ، فتوقَّفَتِ الدلالة على حدوثِ العرض بطروئِهِ على إبطال هذه الأقسام الثلاثةِ ؛ فحينئذ يستبينُ : أَنَّ الطروءَ المشاهَدَ هو تجدد بعد عدمِ ، وهو معنى الحدوث
Before this state in which we witnessed its emergence, if it had existed before it, it would not be free: either it would be in a locus or not; if it were in a locus, then either in this observed locus upon which it emerges or another; if it were this, then it had been latent (kāmin) in it, and if it were another, then it would not reach it except by transfer from that other to it; and if it were not in a locus before emerging upon this, then it would be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihī). Then the indication of the temporality (ḥudūth) of the accident by its emergence depends on invalidating these three divisions; then it becomes clear: that the observed emergence is a renewal after non-existence, and that is the meaning of temporality (al-ḥudūth).
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage provides a logical proof for the temporality of an accident (ʿaraḍ) based on its observed emergence. Sanusi begins by considering the state before the accident's appearance. If the accident existed before this observed state, it would have to be either in a locus or not. If in a locus, that locus would be either the same one in which it now emerges or a different one. If it were in the same locus, then it was latent (kāmin) within it—a doctrine attributed to some natural philosophers. If it were in a different locus, it would have to transfer from that locus to the new one. If it were not in any locus, it would be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihī), which is impossible for accidents. Since these three possibilities are all untenable (as Sanusi implies they have been refuted elsewhere), the only conclusion is that the observed emergence is not a manifestation of a pre-existing thing but a renewal after non-existence. This renewal after non-existence is precisely the meaning of temporality (ḥudūth). Thus, the accident must be temporal.

Key Terms
طروء (ṭurūʾ)
حدوث (ḥudūth)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
كمون (kumūn)
قيام بنفسه (qiyām bi-nafsih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof assumes the accident did not exist before, and merely asserts emergence implies newness.
Correction: The proof does not assume non-existence; it eliminates all possible ways the accident could have existed before (latency, transfer, self-subsistence) through logical analysis, leaving only the conclusion of newness.

Checkpoint
What does the observed emergence (ṭurūʾ) of an accident prove according to Sanusi?
A) That the accident could be eternal
B) That the accident is a renewal after non-existence (ḥudūth)
C) That the accident is latent in the body
D) That the accident is self-subsistent

Key Takeaway
The observed emergence of an accident is proof of its temporality because all possibilities of pre-existence are logically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on accidents like sight, sound, and movement every moment. This lesson proves they are created anew each instant. How does that change your awareness of Allah's constant creation? Will you pause today to recognize each color and sound as a direct act of His power?
?
The proof eliminates all possibilities of pre-existence. Is there a belief or habit you hold that you assume has 'always been there' without evidence? What would it cost you to submit that to logical scrutiny as Sanusi does with accidents?
?
Your own emotional states—anger, peace, love—are accidents, temporal and renewed by Allah. Are you living as if they are permanent or as if you own them? How would your reactions change if you saw each emotion as a fresh creation from Allah, demanding gratitude or repentance?

Proof Through Cessation of Accident and Objection
Sanusi gives a parallel proof through an accident's ceasing to exist and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof of an accident's temporality through its cessation and the objection that an eternal thing could cease, and why that objection fails.

Core Idea
The cessation of an accident proves it is temporal, because an eternal cannot cease to exist.

Sanusi now examines a second angle: not only do accidents begin to exist, but they also cease to exist. He presents a proof that this cessation itself demonstrates the accident is not eternal. However, an opponent might argue that an eternal being could theoretically cease. Sanusi counters with the principle that the eternal is necessary and thus cannot be subject to non-existence. Let's see how he develops this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the logical flow from the proof to the objection and response.
The passage is dense; you may need to re-read slowly.
2
Analyze the proof by exhaustion
Sanusi argues that if the accident had not ceased, it would have to remain in one of three ways: in the same locus (latent), in another locus (by transfer), or no locus (self-subsistent). All three are impossible, so cessation is real.
Focus on why each option is impossible: latent accidents can't exist, transfer is not possible for accidents, and accidents are not self-subsistent.
3
Identify the objection
An objector asks: why does cessation imply temporality? Could the accident be eternal and then cease? This challenges the connection between cessation and needing a beginning.
The objector is suggesting that an eternal thing could later cease to exist.
4
Understand Sanusi's answer
Sanusi responds that if the accident were eternal (qadīm), it would be impossible for it to cease, because the eternal is necessary in existence and cannot be subject to non-existence. Therefore, cessation proves it is temporal.
This answer relies on a principle that will be proven later: the eternal is necessary and cannot change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا نقول في تحقيق الاستدلال بطروء العدم للعَرَضِ بعد وجودِهِ : إنَّهُ لو لم يكن قد عُدِمَ لكانَ باقياً ، وهو إما أن يبقى في محلّ أو لا ؛ فإن بقي في محلّ فهو إما في هذا المحل أو غيره ؛ فإنْ كانَ في هذا المحل فهو كامنُ ، وإنْ كانَ في غيره فلا يصل إليه إلا بانتقال مِنْ هذا إليه ، وإنْ كانَ في غيرِ محلّ فقد قامَ بنفسِهِ ؛ فإذا بطلَتْ هذه الأقسام الثلاثةُ تُحقِّقَ حِينَئِذٍ طروءُ العدمِ . لكن يُقالُ : لِمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ طروءَ العدم بعد الوجود يستلزم الحدوث الذي هو سبقُ العدم على الوجودِ ؟ ولِمَ لا يُقالُ : إِنَّ هذا العَرَضَ قديم ثم طرأ عليهِ العدمُ ؟ فنجيب حينئذٍ : بأنَّهُ لو كان قديماً لم يجز عدمه ، ونبرهن على ذلك ، وإذا تُحقِّقَ بطروء العدم أنَّه لم يكن قديماً لزِمَ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً ؛ وهو المطلوب
And similarly we say in verifying the proof through the emergence of non-existence for the accident after its existence: that if it had not ceased to exist, it would have remained, and it would either remain in a locus or not; if it remains in a locus, then either in this locus or another; if it were in this locus, it would be latent (kāmin), and if it were in another, it would not reach it except by transfer from this to it; and if it were not in a locus, it would be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihī). So when these three divisions are invalidated, then the emergence of non-existence is realized. But it is said: Why do you say that the emergence of non-existence after existence necessarily entails temporality (ḥudūth), which is the precedence of non-existence over existence? And why is it not said that this accident is eternal (qadīm) and then non-existence emerged upon it? So we answer: that if it were eternal, its non-existence would not be permissible, and we will prove that; and when the emergence of non-existence verifies that it was not eternal, it follows that it is temporal (ḥādith); and that is what is required.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
In this passage, Sanusi presents another proof for an accident's temporality, this time focusing on its ceasing to exist (ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam). The same logical analysis is applied: after the accident ceases, if it had remained, it would have to do so in a locus or not, with similar subdivisions leading to impossibility. Thus, the only conclusion is that it truly ceased to exist. However, an objector raises a question: why does the fact that something ceases to exist after having existed imply that it is temporal (i.e., preceded by non-existence)? Could it not be that the accident is eternal and then ceasing to exist emerged upon it? Sanusi answers that if it were eternal (qadīm), it would be impossible for it to cease to exist, because an eternal being is necessary in existence and cannot be subject to non-existence. (The fuller proof of this is given in principle five later.) Since the accident has ceased, it cannot be eternal, so it must be temporal (ḥādith). This answers the objection and reinforces the argument.

Key Terms
طروء العدم (ṭurūʾ al-ʿadam)
قديم (qadīm)
حادث (ḥādith)
عدم (ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal being can cease to exist.
Correction: In Islamic theology, the eternal (qadīm) is necessary in existence and therefore cannot be subject to non-existence. If something ceases, it must be temporal.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the proof through an accident's cessation?
A) The accident might have been self-subsistent
B) The accident might be eternal and then non-existence emerged upon it
C) The accident might be latent in the substance
D) The accident might transfer to another locus

Key Takeaway
The cessation of an accident proves it is temporal because an eternal being cannot cease to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that anything that ceases to exist cannot be eternal. Is there any person, possession, or status in your life that you have treated as if it would never end? What would it cost you to reorder your heart so that only Allah is truly permanent?
?
Sanusi insists that if something were eternal, its non-existence would be impossible. Do you live with that same certainty about Allah's existence? When fear or despair creeps in, do you act as if Allah could leave or diminish? What practical step can you take today to anchor your hope in the only Necessary Being?
?
This lesson demolishes the idea that any created thing can last forever. Consider one attachment you have that you know will eventually end—perhaps your youth, a relationship, or a role you hold. How does this proof help you to love it less and fear Allah more? What specific change in attitude or action would show that you truly believe this truth?

The Seven Foundational Principles for Proving the World's Temporal Origin
Sanusi enumerates the seven principles for the proof of the world's temporal origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven rational principles that serve as premises for the cosmological argument (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) as enumerated by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Seven premises, not one, ground the proof that the world began.

Sanusi begins the argument for the world's temporal creation by listing the seven principles on which the proof depends. This lesson presents that list, giving you a bird's‑eye view of the entire argumentative structure before each premise is explained separately.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic list and its translation, noticing the pattern of 'إثبات' and 'إبطال'. Each principle is either affirming or invalidating a concept.
The first four principles concern accidents (aʿrāḍ), the next two about the eternal, and the seventh about infinite regress.
2
Categorize the principles
Group the seven principles into three categories: those about accidents (1-4), those about the eternal (5-6), and the final one about events without beginning (7).
3
Relate to the proof
Consider why each principle is necessary for the conclusion that the world is not eternal. For example, the first four establish the nature of change, while the last two prevent infinite regress.
This step prepares you for the detailed exposition of each principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمجموع الأصول التي ينبني عليها برهان حدوث العالم سبعة :
الأوَّلُ : إثبات زائد على الأجرام .
الثاني : إبطال قيامه بنفسِهِ
الثالث : إبطال انتقاله
الرابع : إبطالُ كُمونِهِ وظُهوره
الخامس : إثبات استحالة عُدْمِ القديم
السادس : إثبات كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد
السابع : إثبات استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها
So the total of the principles upon which the proof of the occurrence of the world is built are seven:
First: Affirming an accident (zā'id) upon bodies.
Second: Invalidating its self-subsistence.
Third: Invalidating its transference.
Fourth: Invalidating its latency (kumūn) and manifestation.
Fifth: Affirming the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal.
Sixth: Affirming that bodies are never free from that accident.
Seventh: Affirming the impossibility of events without beginning.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage enumerates the seven foundational principles required for the proof of the world's temporal creation (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). The first four principles concern the nature of accidents (aʿrāḍ): their existence, inability to subsist by themselves, inability to transfer, and impossibility of latency and manifestation. The fifth and sixth concern the eternal: the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist and the necessary connection between bodies and accidents. The seventh principle negates an infinite regress of events. These principles are not proven here; they are simply listed as the premises needed for the broader cosmological argument.

Key Takeaway
The proof of the world's temporal origin rests on seven distinct rational premises, each addressing a specific metaphysical possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If your conviction in the world's creation rests only on what you've been told, isn't that a fragile foundation? Will you commit to studying these seven principles until you can articulate each one yourself?
?
The seventh principle denies the possibility of an infinite series of events. Think about your daily life: do you ever live as if this world has no purpose or ultimate beginning? How does accepting that the world had a beginning change the way you view your own existence and accountability to the Creator?
?
Ibn Sanusi' enumeration of these principles was not just academic—it was meant to fortify your faith. So ask yourself: do you actually use rational proofs when your faith is challenged, or do you rely on emotional comfort? Which of these seven principles, if you truly grasped it, would most strengthen your certainty in Allah's existence? Will you make it your study this week?

The Self-Evident Existence of Accidents
Sanusi argues that the existence of accidents is self-evident and proves it via the opponent's own admission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of accidents (aʿrāḍ) is considered self-evident and how the opponent's own denial proves their existence.

Core Idea
Accidents are undeniable because every rational person experiences them.

Sanusi begins his proof of the seven principles with the most foundational one: accidents exist. He argues that this is not a matter of debate—it is directly perceived by every rational being. Then he strengthens his case with a clever argument: the very act of denying accidents is itself an accident, so the opponent cannot escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the two parts: the claim of self-evidence and the dialectical argument.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ḍarūrī' and 'zā'id'.
2
Understand the self-evident claim
Sanusi says the existence of accidents is 'ḍarūrī' – self-evident, needing no proof. Reflect on why: every rational person perceives additional meanings (like thoughts, feelings) in themselves beyond their essence.
This is a foundational principle in kalam; it is not deduced but directly known.
3
Examine the dialectical argument
Sanusi quotes a scholar's response to a denier: if you deny accidents, your denial itself is an accident. If you say it has no existence, you refute yourself; if you admit the denial occurred, you concede an accident.
This is a classic self-refutation argument.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why this argument is considered definitive: the opponent cannot coherently deny accidents without affirming one. This shows the principle is inescapable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه الاستدلال على هذه الأصول السبعة باختصار : أن نقول : أَمَّا الأَوَّلُ - وهو إثبات زائد على الأجرام تتَّصِفُ به كالحركة والسكون - : فهو ضروري ، لا يحتاج إلى دليل ؛ إذْ ما مِنْ عاقل إلا وهو يُحِسُّ أن في ذاتِهِ معاني زائدة عليها. ولهذا قال بعضُ أذكياء العلماء في جوابِ مَنْ منع وجودَ الأعراض : نزاعكم لنا في ثبوتِ الأعراض : أموجود هو أم معدوم ؟ فإن قلتُم : لا وجود له.. خرجتُم عن طور العقلاء ، وسقطَتْ مكالمتكم ؛ لإقراركم بأنَّهُ لم يقع منكم نزاع لنا ، وإن أقررتُم بأنَّ نزاعكم لنا وقعَ منكم .. فلا شكّ أنَّ ذلكَ النزاع أمرٌ زائد على الذاتِ ؛ وهو الذي نعني بالعَرَضِ ، فقد سلّمتُم وجود زائد على الأجرام .
And the explanation of the proof for these seven principles in brief: we say: As for the first—which is affirming an accident (zā'id) upon bodies that qualify them, like motion and rest—it is necessary (ḍarūrī), requiring no proof; for there is no rational person except that he perceives in his own self meanings additional to his essence. That is why some of the intelligent scholars said, in answer to one who denied the existence of accidents: 'Your dispute with us regarding the affirmation of accidents: is it existent or non-existent? If you say it has no existence, you exit from the realm of rational people, and your argument falls; because you acknowledge that no dispute occurred from you. But if you acknowledge that your dispute with us did occur, then there is no doubt that that dispute is a matter additional to the essence; that is what we mean by accident. So you have conceded the existence of something additional to bodies.'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the first foundational principle: the existence of accidents (aʿrāḍ) as real attributes inhering in bodies. The author first declares the principle self-evident (ḍarūrī), since everyone, including deniers of accidents, experiences internal states (like thoughts, desires) that are additional to their essence. He then strengthens this by quoting an argument attributed to al-Ghazālī: The denial of accidents is a self-refuting position. If an opponent says that accidents do not exist, then their own act of denying—a mental or verbal act—is itself an accident. Thus they either concede accidents exist or they refute their own argument. This argument shows that the existence of accidents is undeniable even by those who try to reject it.

Key Terms
ضروري
زائد
عَرَض
ذات
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are not real entities; they are merely mental constructs or changes in perception.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that accidents are real, self-evident attributes inhering in bodies, and even the opponent's denial proves their reality.

Checkpoint

B) It is self-evident and the opponent's own denial is an accident

D) It is deduced from the existence of God

Key Takeaway
The existence of accidents is self-evident and inescapable: even denying them affirms them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If these are real and additional to your body, what does that mean about the nature of your soul and its relationship to its Creator? Will you spend time today in silent reflection, acknowledging that every internal state is a gift and a sign of the One who creates them?
?
Sanusi's argument shows that even denying a truth can be self-refuting. Think of a belief you hold that you have never questioned deeply. Are you willing to apply the same scrutiny to your own convictions, knowing that rejecting truth may itself be an argument for truth? Commit to investigating one core belief this week with the same intellectual honesty.
?
The passage proves that accidents are undeniable because you experience them every moment. Yet how often do you live as if only the physical world is real, ignoring the spiritual realities that are just as present? Proof of the Second and Third Principles: Accidents Cannot Subsist by Themselves or Transfer
Sanusi proves accidents cannot be self-subsistent or transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments proving that accidents cannot be self-subsistent nor transfer between substances.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot stand alone or move; their very definition forbids it.

Sanusi now proves two key principles about accidents: they cannot subsist by themselves, and they cannot transfer from one substance to another. These proofs rely on the very definition of an accident and the absurdity of infinite regress. The arguments are concise but foundational for kalam metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style of Sanusi; each clause carries weight.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two distinct proofs: a definitional argument (reversal of essence) and a regress argument (tasalsul). Find where each begins.
The definitional argument applies to both self-subsistence and transference; the regress argument specifically targets transference.
3
Understand the definitional argument
Consider motion as an example: its essence is the transference of a substance. If motion itself subsisted or transferred, it would no longer be motion—its essence would be reversed.
This shows that accidents depend on substances for their very definition.
4
Understand the regress argument
If an accident like motion transferred, then that transference would itself be an accident requiring another transference, leading to an infinite chain. This is impossible.
Regress is avoided by denying the initial transference.
5
Connect to kalam principles
These proofs establish that accidents are ontologically dependent on substances and cannot be self-sufficient or mobile. This underpins the kalam cosmological argument.
Understanding this helps distinguish accidents from substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني - وهو إبطال قيام العَرَضِ بنفسِهِ - والثالث - وهو إبطال انتقاله - : فدليلُهما : أَنَّهُ لو قامَ العَرَضُ بنفسِهِ أَوِ انتقلَ .. لزمَ قلْبُ حقيقةِ العَرَضِ ؛ فإنَّ الحركة مثلاً حقيقتها : انتقال جوهرٍ مِنْ حيّز إلى حيّز ، فلو قامَتْ بنفسها أو انتقلَتْ لزم قلب هذهِ الحقيقة. وأيضاً : لو انتقلَتْ هي لزمَ قيامُ انتقال بها ، وذلك الانتقال ينتقل أيضاً ، فيقومُ بهِ انتقال آخرُ ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى .
And as for the second—which is invalidating the accident's self-subsistence—and the third—which is invalidating its transference—their proof is: that if the accident subsists by itself or transfers, then the reversal of the accident's essence would necessarily follow. For motion, for example, its essence is: the transference of a substance from one space to another. So if it subsisted by itself or transferred, the reversal of this essence would follow. Also: if it transferred, then a transference would necessarily subsist in it, and that transference would also transfer, so another transference would subsist in it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow, and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage provides a unified proof for both the second principle (accidents cannot subsist by themselves) and the third principle (accidents cannot transfer from one substance to another). The proof has two parts: 1) Definitional reversal: The essence of an accident like motion is precisely being a transference of a substance. If motion were self-subsistent or transferred, it would cease to be motion because its definition would change. 2) Infinite regress: If an accident like motion were to transfer, then that transference would itself need a 0 transference, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul). Moreover, this would result in one accident inhering in another accident (قيام المعنى بالمعنى), which is impossible. Thus both self-subsistence and transference are false.

Key Terms
عرض
قيام بنفسه
انتقال
قلب الحقيقة
تسلسل
قيام المعنى بالمعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents can move or exist independently like substances.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if accidents were self-subsistent or transferred, their very definition would be contradicted, and infinite regress would follow.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Sanusi say would follow if an accident like motion transferred from one substance to another?
A) It would become a substance
B) The definition of the accident would be reversed and infinite regress would occur
C) The accident would cease to exist
D) The substance would become an accident

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot be self-subsistent or transfer because doing so would violate their essential nature and lead to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that nothing in the physical world can exist independently—every accident relies on a substance that relies on Allah. In your life, what are the 'accidents' you treat as self-sufficient (like your health, wealth, or abilities)? How can you daily remind yourself that they are not self-subsisting but sustained by Allah?
?
The infinite regress argument demonstrates that chains of causes must eventually stop. Have you ever deeply pondered that you yourself are an accident subsisting by Allah's will at every moment? What would change in your prayer and reliance on Him if you truly felt that dependency?
?
If the very essence of something (like motion) would be destroyed if it stood alone, what about your own identity? Could it be that your sense of self and existence only has meaning when anchored in submission to Allah? What step can you take today to realign your 'self' as a accident dependent on its Creator?

Refuting Latency and Manifestation of Accidents
Sanusi presents two arguments against latency and manifestation of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key arguments Sanusi uses to refute the theory that accidents can be latent (kumūn) and manifest (ẓuhūr) in a substance.

Core Idea
Latency of accidents is impossible because it would force opposites to coexist and accidents to lose their nature.

Sanusi now tackles a theory that might seem plausible at first: that accidents can sometimes be 'hidden' within a substance and then become manifest. He shows why this is rationally impossible, using clear examples from motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Understand the theory under attack
The theory says accidents like motion can be latent (kāmin) in a substance and then become manifest. Grasp what this claim entails.
2
Follow the first argument: opposites
If rest is latent in a moving substance, then motion and rest (two opposites) would coexist in that substance at the same time, which is impossible.
3
Follow the second argument: accident's own attribute
If motion were latent, it would lack its defining attribute of causing the substance to transfer, so it would no longer be motion. Its essence would be reversed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع - وهو إبطالُ الكُمونِ والظُّهور - : فوجهُهُ : أَنَّ الكُمُونَ والظهور يُؤدِّي إلى اجتماع الضدَّينِ في المحل الواحدِ ؛ لأنَّ الجوهر إذا تحرك مثلاً والسكون كامن فيه زمان حركته.. لزم اجتماع الضدين فيه - وهما الحركة والسكونُ - ضرورةً ، ويُؤدِّي أيضاً إلى وجودِ العَرَضِ بدونِ صفةِ نفسِهِ ؛ فإنَّ الحركة مثلاً مِنْ صفةِ نفسها أنْ ينتقل بها الجوهر ؛ فلو كَمَنَتْ لانقلبتْ حقيقتُها ، وفارقتها صفةً نفسها. وأيضاً : فالكُمُونُ والظُّهورُ اللذان قاما بالعَرَض أو تعاقبا عليهِ على قولهم .. يلزم أن يكونا عَرَضينِ أيضاً في أنفسهما كالحركة والسكون
And as for the fourth—which is invalidating latency (kumūn) and manifestation (ẓuhūr)—its proof is: that latency and manifestation lead to the conjunction of two opposites in a single locus. For if a substance moves, for example, and the motion of the opposite (sukūn) is latent in it during the time of its motion, then the conjunction of two opposites—motion and rest—in it would necessarily follow. And it also leads to the existence of the accident without its own attribute. For motion, for example, has as part of its own attribute that the substance transfers by it. So if it were latent, its essence would be reversed, and its own attribute would depart from it. Also, latency and manifestation, which subsist in the accident or alternate upon it according to their view, would necessarily be accidents themselves, like motion and rest.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments against the theory that accidents can be latent (kumūn) or manifest (ẓuhūr) in a substance. First, if rest were latent in a moving body, then motion and rest (two opposites) would coexist in the same substance, which is impossible. Second, if motion were latent, it would lack its defining attribute—that the substance transfers by it—so it would no longer be motion; its essence would be reversed. Third, the latency and manifestation themselves, if they are accidental modifications of the accident, would themselves be accidents, leading to an infinite regress or a circularity. Thus the theory is refuted.

Key Terms
كُمون
ظهور
ضدان
اجتماع الضدين
صفة النفس
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think accidents can 'sleep' within a substance and later become active, like a potentiality.
Correction: Sanusi shows this is impossible because it would force opposites to coexist and accidents to lose their defining nature.

Checkpoint
What is one reason Sanusi gives against the view that an accident like motion can be latent (kāmin) in a substance?
A) It would require the substance to be divisible infinitely
B) It would lead to the conjunction of opposites (motion and rest) in the same locus
C) It would make the accident eternal
D) It would contradict the existence of the substance

Key Takeaway
Latency of accidents is rationally impossible because it would force opposites to coexist and accidents to lose their essential defining attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed a false belief to 'lie latent' in your heart while outwardly professing the truth, thinking it could coexist? This passage shows that truth and falsehood cannot both dwell in a sound soul. What falsehood have you been sheltering? Today, expose it and reject it.
?
Your essential nature as a believer is defined by your actions and convictions. If you 'hide' your faith or keep it latent when it should manifest, do you not risk losing your very identity? In what area of your life are you acting as if your faith can be latent without consequence? Commit now to letting your faith manifest fully.
?
The argument that an accident loses its essence when latent applies to you too. If you keep your good deeds hidden when they should be seen, or your bad deeds hidden when they should be repented, you are altering your own spiritual essence. Which of your deeds have you rendered 'latent' to avoid confrontation? Bring them into the light today.

Proof of Accidents from the Opponent's Dispute
Sanusi refutes those denying accidents by using their own dispute as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the opponent's own act of disputing as a logical proof for the existence of accidents.

Core Idea
Your own dispute proves the existence of accidents.

In the previous lesson, we learned what accidents (a‘rāḍ) are and why they matter. Now Sanusi presents a clever argument that forces an opponent to admit that accidents exist simply by the fact that they are arguing. He traps them in a dilemma: either their dispute never happened (which is absurd) or it did happen—and that dispute itself is an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Notice the rhetorical force of the opening question.
The passage is a single logical squeeze.
2
Identify the two options of the dilemma
Sanusi gives the opponent two choices: either the dispute has no existence, or it does. Trace how each choice leads to a contradiction or concession.
3
Understand the key term 'accident'
Notice that Sanusi defines an accident as 'something additional to the essence'. This is the definition used in the argument.
The opponent's dispute fits this definition exactly.
4
Reflect on the strength of this proof
Ask yourself why this argument is so powerful: it requires no external evidence, only the opponent's own behavior. How does this relate to the nature of rational debate in kalām?
This connects to the broader method of logical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معدوم ؟ فإن قلتُم : لا وجود له.. خرجتُم عن طور العقلاء ، وسقطَتْ مكالمتكم ؛ لإقراركم بأنَّهُ لم يقع منكم نزاع لنا ، وإن أقررتُم بأنَّ نزاعكم لنا وقعَ منكم .. فلا شكّ أنَّ ذلكَ النزاع أمرٌ زائد على الذاتِ ؛ وهو الذي نعني بالعَرَضِ ، فقد سلّمتُم وجود زائد على الأجرام .
Does it not exist? If you say: it has no existence, you have departed from the way of rational people, and your argument collapses; because you have admitted that no dispute from you occurred to us, and if you admit that your dispute with us occurred from you, then there is no doubt that that dispute is something additional to the essence; and it is what we mean by accident. So you have acknowledged an existence additional to bodies.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny that accidents (ʿaraḍ) have any real existence. Sanusi presents a dilemma: either the opponent admits that their dispute with the proponents of accidents never occurred, or they admit it did occur. If they admit it never occurred, they have abandoned reason and their argument is void. If they admit it did occur, then that dispute itself is something extra beyond the substance (jawhar) of the disputants, and this extra thing is precisely what is meant by 'accident'. Therefore, by admitting their own dispute, they have conceded the existence of an accident.

Key Terms
عَرَض
ذات
أجرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi is proving accidents from sense perception or empirical observation.
Correction: The proof is purely logical: it uses the opponent's own act of disputing as evidence, not any external observation.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi use to prove the existence of accidents?
A) Empirical observation of motion
B) The opponent's own act of disputing
C) A scriptural proof
D) The definition of substance

Key Takeaway
The opponent cannot deny the existence of accidents without undermining their own act of disputing, which is itself an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever denied something about your own behavior that was clearly true? How did that affect your self-honesty and credibility with others?
?
This argument shows that the very act of denial proves the reality of what is denied. Are there beliefs you hold that are contradicted by your own actions? What would it take for you to acknowledge that inconsistency and change?
?
If your own existence as a rational being testifies against your claims, are you willing to let truth prevail even if it means admitting you were wrong? What comfort might you need to let go of to do that?

Accident Cannot Subsist by Itself Nor Transfer
Sanusi proves that accidents cannot be self-subsistent or transfer, using the nature of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot subsist independently or transfer, based on the definitional and regress arguments using the example of motion.

Core Idea
Accidents are ontologically dependent; they cannot stand alone or move from substance to substance.

Sanusi presents two critical refutations regarding the nature of accidents. He argues that if an accident could subsist by itself or transfer, its very reality would be overturned. Using motion as an example, he demonstrates the logical impossibility, including an infinite regress. This lesson examines these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; the proof is packed into two main points.
2
Identify the two refutations
The passage refutes two claims: that an accident can subsist by itself, and that it can transfer. Identify where each is addressed.
The first refutation is introduced by 'إبطال قيام العَرض بنفسه' and the second by 'إبطال انتقاله'.
3
Understand the proof using motion
Motion's reality is defined as the transfer of a substance. If motion itself could subsist or transfer, it would contradict its own definition. This overturns the essence of mobility.
4
Recognize the infinite regress argument
If motion could transfer, the act of transferring would itself be an accident requiring another transfer, leading to an infinite chain. This is impossible in kalam.
This argument specifically targets the transfer claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال قيام العَرَضِ بنفسِهِ وأما الثاني - وهو إبطال انتقاله - : فدليلُهما : أَنَّهُ لو قامَ العَرَضُ بنفسِهِ أَوِ انتقلَ .. لزمَ قلْبُ حقيقةِ العَرَضِ ؛ فإنَّ الحركة مثلاً حقيقتها : انتقال جوهرٍ مِنْ حيّز إلى حيّز ، فلو قامَتْ بنفسها أو انتقلَتْ لزم قلب هذهِ الحقيقة وأيضاً : لو انتقلَتْ هي لزمَ قيامُ انتقال بها ، وذلك الانتقال ينتقل أيضاً ، فيقومُ بهِ انتقال آخرُ ، ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمَعْنى .
Refutation of the accident subsisting by itself. And as for the second—which is the refutation of its transfer—the proof for both is: that if the accident subsisted by itself or transferred, it would necessitate overturning the reality of the accident; for motion, for example, its reality is: the transfer of a substance from one place to another. So if it subsisted by itself or transferred, it would necessitate overturning this reality. Also: if it transferred, it would necessitate the subsistence of a transfer in it, and that transfer would also transfer, so another transfer would subsist in it, and infinite regress would follow, and the subsistence of an attribute in an attribute.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage combines two refutations: (1) that an accident cannot subsist by itself (qiyāmuhu bi-nafsihi), and (2) that an accident cannot transfer (intiqāl) from one substance to another. Sanusi gives a single proof for both: if an accident could subsist by itself or transfer, it would invert the very definition of accident. He takes motion as an example: the reality of motion is the transfer of a substance from one location to another. If motion itself could subsist by itself or transfer, then motion would no longer be a transfer of a substance; its essence would be overturned. Additionally, if motion could transfer, then it would require another transfer (since transfer itself is a change), leading to an infinite regress. This also implies an attribute (motion) subsisting in another attribute (transfer), which is impossible.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
انتقال
حقيقة
حركة
جوهر
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents are like substances, but Sanusi shows they are ontologically dependent.
Correction: Accidents cannot exist independently; they require a substance to inhere in and cannot transfer between substances.

Checkpoint
What would result if motion could subsist by itself or transfer?
A) Motion would become a substance
B) The reality of motion would be overturned
C) Bodies would cease to exist
D) Accidents would become eternal

Key Takeaway
Accidents are ontologically dependent on substances; they cannot subsist independently nor transfer, as proven by definitional and regress arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your ability to move, speak, or think are accidents that depend entirely on Allah's preservation. When you sin with your limbs, do you realize that you are using a loan that could be withdrawn at any moment? What would change in your life if you truly felt the fragility of every action?
?
The proof shows that accidents cannot stand alone. Your good deeds are accidents—they will not persist without Allah's acceptance. Are you trusting in your deeds as if they were substances? How can you shift your hope from your own actions to the One who sustains them?
?
Consider one attribute you pride yourself on—intelligence, patience, kindness. None of these can subsist by themselves. If Allah withdraws His support, you become the opposite in an instant. Will you thank Him today for every good quality you have, and beg Him to keep them for you?

Refutation of Latency and Emergence of Accidents
Sanusi refutes the theory of latency and emergence with three logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theory of latency and emergence of accidents is logically untenable through three key arguments.

Core Idea
Latency leads to absurdities: opposites coexisting, accidents losing identity, and infinite regress.

Having established the basic categories of accidents and substances, Sanusi now refutes a flawed theory that attempts to explain change through hidden accidents. This theory, known as kumūn and ẓuhūr, claims that accidents can be latent within a substance and then emerge. Sanusi shows that this idea leads to three logical contradictions, each more damaging than the last. Let's examine his penetrating critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. This will give you a sense of the flow of arguments.
2
Identify the first argument: conjunction of opposites
Notice how Sanusi argues that latency leads to two opposites (like motion and rest) coexisting in the same substance at the same time, which is impossible.
This is a direct application of the principle of non-contradiction.
3
Identify the second argument: accident without its own attribute
See how latency would strip an accident of its essential property; for example, if motion is latent, it cannot cause transfer, so it loses its defining attribute.
This shows that latency contradicts the very nature of accidents.
4
Identify the third argument: infinite regress
Understand that if latency and emergence themselves are accidents, they would require further latency and emergence, leading to an endless chain.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع - وهو إبطالُ الكُمونِ والظُّهور - : فوجهُهُ : أَنَّ الكُمُونَ والظهور يُؤدِّي إلى اجتماع الضدَّينِ في المحل الواحدِ ؛ لأنَّ الجوهر إذا تحرك مثلاً والسكون كامن فيه زمان حركته.. لزم اجتماع الضدين فيه - وهما الحركة والسكونُ - ضرورةً ، ويُؤدِّي أيضاً إلى وجودِ العَرَضِ بدونِ صفةِ نفسِهِ ؛ فإنَّ الحركة مثلاً مِنْ صفةِ نفسها أنْ ينتقل بها الجوهر ؛ فلو كَمَنَتْ لا نقلبَتْ حقيقتُها ، وفارقتها صفةً نفسها وأيضاً : فالحُمونُ والظُّهورُ اللذان قاما بالعَرَض أو تعاقبا عليهِ على قولهم .. يلزم أن يكونا عَرَضينِ أيضاً في أنفسهما كالحركة والسكون المتعاقبين على الجوهرِ ، فإنْ كانَ ينعدم أحدهما عند وجودِ الآخرِ فقد ناقضوا أصلهم في كُمون الأعراض ، ولزمهم ما فرُّوا منهُ مِنْ ملازمة الجواهر للحوادث ، وإن قالوا بكُمون وظُهورِ آخرين للكُمونِ والظهور .. لزم التسلسل .
And as for the fourth—which is the refutation of latency (kumūn) and emergence (ẓuhūr)—its aspect is: that latency and emergence lead to the conjunction of two opposites in a single locus; because if a substance moves, for example, and rest is latent in it during the time of its motion, then the conjunction of the two opposites—namely motion and rest—in it would be necessary. It also leads to the existence of an accident without its own attribute; for motion, for example, has as part of its own attribute that the substance transfers by it; so if it were latent, its reality would be overturned, and its own attribute would separate from it. Also: the latency and emergence that subsist in the accident or alternate upon it according to their claim would necessarily be accidents in themselves, like motion and rest alternating upon the substance. If one of them ceases at the existence of the other, then they have contradicted their own principle regarding the latency of accidents, and they incur what they fled from, namely the necessary accompaniment of substances by events. And if they say that there are other latency and emergence for the latency and emergence, then an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the theory that accidents can be latent (hidden) within a substance and then emerge. Sanusi raises several objections. First, latency and emergence would require the coexistence of opposites in the same subject: for example, if a body is moving while rest is latent in it, then motion and rest would exist together, which is impossible. Second, an accident would exist without its essential property; for motion, its essential property is to cause the substance to transfer; if motion is latent, it would not cause transfer, thereby losing its defining attribute. Third, the latency and emergence themselves, if they are accidents, would require further latency and emergence, leading to an infinite regress. Finally, the theory contradicts the principle of the dependence of accidents on substances, which the opponents tried to avoid.

Key Terms
كُمون
ظُهور
ضدَّين
صفة نفسها
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think latency is a plausible explanation for how accidents can change, but it is not merely a hidden presence.
Correction: Sanusi shows that latency leads to logical absurdities: opposites coexisting, accidents losing their identity, and infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence of the theory of latency and emergence?
A) Accidents become eternal
B) Opposites can coexist
C) Substances are annihilated
D) Motion and rest become identical

Key Takeaway
The theory of latency and emergence is invalid because it requires impossible conjunctions, undermines the essence of accidents, and leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that if an accident is latent, it loses its defining attribute. In your own life, are there 'latent' aspects—like faith, gratitude, or patience—that you claim to possess but never manifest? Are you, in effect, negating their true nature? What will you do today to bring one hidden virtue into action?
?
The theory of latency tries to avoid the necessary connection between substances and events. Reflect: Do you sometimes try to hide your true state from Allah, pretending to be something you are not? Consider the contradiction between your outward behavior and inner reality—how does Sanusi's argument against hidden contradictions expose the futility of such pretense? Commit to aligning your inner and outer self starting now.
?
Infinite regress is impossible; there must be a final point. Look at your goals and desires: is there an endless cycle of worldly pursuits without an ultimate purpose? How does recognizing the absurdity of regress push you to anchor your life in the ultimate reality—Allah—rather than in endless chains of ephemeral wants? Write one concrete step to break a pattern of infinite chasing and turn toward the Eternal.

Bodies Are Inseparable from Accidents
Sanusi states that bodies are necessarily connected to accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodies must always be accompanied by accidents according to Sanusi, and why this is a necessary rational judgment.

Core Idea
A body cannot exist without a single accident — it is rationally necessary.

Sanusi now presents a crucial principle for understanding the relationship between bodies and accidents. He argues that it is not merely possible but necessary for a body to have some accident. This principle is foundational for later arguments about the contingent nature of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Sanusi's concise statement and its translation. Note that he calls this proof 'necessary.'
The passage is very short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the concept of necessity
Consider what it means for something to be rationally necessary (ḍarūrī). Why can't a body be thought of without any accident?
This is a key kalam concept.
3
Apply to examples
Think of any body: it must be either moving or at rest. Could it be neither? Sanusi says no.
This illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا السادس - وهو إثباتُ كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد - فهو ضروري ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ كون الجَزم مُنفكّاً عن كونه متحركاً أو ساكناً مثلاً .
And as for the sixth—which is the proof that bodies (ajrām) are not free from that additional thing (accident)—it is necessary; because it is inconceivable for a body to be devoid of being in motion or at rest, for example.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents a simple but powerful argument: bodies are always characterized by an accident. Sanusi says this is necessary (ḍarūrī) because it is impossible to conceive of a body that is neither moving nor at rest, nor in any other accidental state. In other words, a body must always have some accident; it cannot exist without any accidental attribute. This is a foundational principle in kalam.

Key Terms
أجرام
زائد
ضروري
متحرك
ساكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body could exist without any accident or attribute.
Correction: Sanusi argues this is rationally impossible; a body must always have some accident like motion or rest.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say is the status of bodies regarding accidents?
A) They can exist without accidents
B) They are necessarily accompanied by accidents
C) Accidents exist independently
D) Only motion is an accident

Key Takeaway
According to Sanusi, it is a necessary truth that every body is always accompanied by some accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is always in some state—moving, resting, breathing. How often do you pause to recognize that this constant change is a sign that you are not self-sufficient? What would it take for you to live each day with that awareness?
?
If every accident in your body—every thought, every heartbeat—is contingent and depends on Allah, why do you still sometimes feel independent? What would change if you genuinely internalized this truth?
?
You cannot imagine a body without a single accident. Does this make you feel trapped in materiality, or does it drive you to seek the Necessary Being behind all change? How can you use this reality to strengthen your tawheed?

The Fifth Principle: Impossibility of the Eternal Ceasing to Exist
Sanusi proves the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the eternal (God) cannot cease to exist, establishing His necessary existence.

Core Idea
If the eternal could cease, it would be possible, but only originated things are possible.

Sanusi begins the fifth principle with a concise proof by contradiction. He argues that if the eternal (i.e., God) could cease to exist, then His existence would be merely possible, not necessary. But only originated things are possible, so the eternal would be originated—a contradiction. This lesson walks through that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Notice the key terms: القديم, الجائز, الواجب, المُحدث.
The passage is very short but dense.
2
Understand the proof structure
Identify the assumption (the eternal could cease), the consequence (its existence is possible), and the contradiction (the eternal would be originated).
This is a classic proof by contradiction.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect that this proof shows God's existence is necessary (wājib), not possible (jāʾiz), and thus He cannot cease.
This distinguishes God from all created things.
4
Test with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to see if you can apply the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخامس - وهو إثباتُ استحالةِ عُدْمِ القديم - : فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لوِ انعدمَ لكانَ وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ، والجائز لا يكون إلا مُحدثاً ، فيكون هذا القديم مُحدَثاً ! وهو تناقض

وأمَّا السادس - وهو إثباتُ كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد - فهو ضروري ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ كون الجِرْم [؟] مُنفكّاً عن كونه متحركاً أو ساكناً مثلاً .

وأما السابع - وهو إثباتُ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها - فله أدلة كثيرة ، ذكرناها في « عقيدتنا الكبرى » و « شرحها » (١) ، وأقربُ الأدلَّةِ أن نقول : إذا كانَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِ الحوادثِ حادثاً في نفسِهِ .. فعدمُ جميعها ثابت في الأزلِ ، ثم لا يخلو : إمَّا أَنْ يقارنَ ذلكَ العدمَ فردٌ مِنَ الأفراد الحادثة أو لا ؛ فإن قارنَهُ لزم اجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ ؛ إِذْ ذلكَ الفردُ مِنْ جملةِ الأفرادِ التي تقرَّرَ عدمُها في الأزلِ ، واجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ محال بضرورة العقل ، وإنْ لم يقارن ذلكَ العدمَ شيءٌ مِنْ تلكَ الأفرادِ الحادثة.. لزمَ أنَّ لها أَوَّلاً ؛ لخلو الأزلِ على هذا الفَرْضِ عن جميعها .
As for the fifth — which is the proof of the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal — its proof is that if it ceased to exist, its existence would be possible, not necessary, and what is possible is only originated (muḥdath), so this eternal would be originated! And that is a contradiction. As for the sixth — which is the proof that bodies are not free from that extra (attribute) — it is necessary; because it is not conceivable for a body to be free from being, for example, moving or at rest. As for the seventh — which is the proof of the impossibility of events without a beginning — it has many proofs, which we mentioned in our 'Great Creed (ʿAqīdatunā al-Kubrā)' and its commentary (1). The closest of the proofs is to say: If every individual of events is in itself originated, then the non-existence of all of them is established in pre-eternity (al-azal). Then it is inevitable: either an individual from the originating events accompanies that non-existence or not. If it accompanies it, then the simultaneous coexistence of existence and non-existence of a thing follows, since that individual is among the individuals whose non-existence was established in pre-eternity. And the simultaneous coexistence of existence and non-existence of a thing is impossible by necessity of reason. And if nothing from those originating events accompanies that non-existence, then it follows that they have a beginning, because pre-eternity on this assumption is empty of all of them.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs for the fifth, sixth, and seventh of the seven principles (al-uṣūl al-sabʿa) used to demonstrate the creation of the world. The argument begins with the fifth principle: the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist. The proof is that if the eternal (i.e., God) could cease to exist, then its existence would be mere possibility (jāʾiz) rather than necessity (wājib), and only originated things are possible; thus the eternal would be originated, which is a contradiction. The sixth principle asserts that bodies (al-ajrām) are inseparable from an added attribute (al-zāʾid), such as motion or rest. This is self-evident (ḍarūrī), as one cannot conceive of a body that is neither moving nor at rest. The seventh principle is the impossibility of an infinite series of events without a beginning. Sanusi presents a concise proof: If every individual event is originated in itself, then all events are non-existent in pre-eternity (al-azal). Now either one particular event coexists with that pre-eternal non-existence or not. If it does, then that event would both exist (since it is present) and not exist (since all events are non-existent in pre-eternity), which is impossible. If no event coexists, then there must be a first moment when events begin, i.e., a first event, because pre-eternity is empty of all events. Thus an infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
القديم
الجائز
الواجب
المُحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'the eternal' here refers to the world or matter.
Correction: In this context, 'the eternal' refers to God, whose existence is necessary. The proof shows He cannot cease, unlike all created things.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence if the eternal could cease to exist?
A) It would be necessary.
B) It would be possible.
C) It would be originated.
D) It would be self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
The eternal cannot cease to exist because that would imply its existence is possible, but only originated things are possible, leading to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's existence is necessary and cannot cease, how would your reliance on Him change in moments of fear or uncertainty? Are there areas of your life where you act as if God could 'disappear' or fail you, and how does this proof confront that?
?
This proof shows that God cannot cease to exist. Do you live with the same certainty about His presence and power? When has your doubt about God's constancy led you to sin or despair? What commitment will you make today to ground your trust in His necessary existence?
?
Sanusi's argument is logical, but does your heart truly rest in the fact that God's existence is necessary and eternal? Think of a time you felt abandoned—did you forget that God cannot cease to be with you? How will you remind yourself of this truth daily?
