The Sixth Principle: Bodies Inseparable from Attributes
Sanusi asserts that bodies are necessarily characterized by an attribute like motion or rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that bodies (al-ajrām) are necessarily characterized by an attribute like motion or rest, and that this is self-evident.

Core Idea
A body cannot exist without being either moving or at rest.

The sixth principle is brief but foundational. Sanusi declares it is self-evident (ḍarūrī) that bodies cannot be free from an added attribute (al-zāʾid), such as motion or rest. This lesson unpacks that claim and its role in the proof of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the single sentence. Notice the term الزائد (the extra) which refers to an attribute like motion.
The word الجِرم is uncertain in the OCR; it likely means 'body'.
2
Understand why it is necessary
Sanusi says it is 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) because it is inconceivable to think of a body without any attribute.
This is an intuitive truth accepted by reason.
3
Connect to the proof of creation
This principle is used later in the argument that bodies are originated because they are always accompanied by originated attributes.
This principle bridges bodies and their attributes.
4
Reflect on the implication
If a body necessarily has an attribute, and that attribute is originated, then the body itself must be originated.
This is developed in later principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخامس - وهو إثباتُ استحالةِ عُدْمِ القديم - : فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لوِ انعدمَ لكانَ وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ، والجائز لا يكون إلا مُحدثاً ، فيكون هذا القديم مُحدَثاً ! وهو تناقض

وأمَّا السادس - وهو إثباتُ كون الأجرام لا تنفك عن ذلك الزائد - فهو ضروري ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ كون الجِرْم [؟] مُنفكّاً عن كونه متحركاً أو ساكناً مثلاً .

وأما السابع - وهو إثباتُ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها - فله أدلة كثيرة ، ذكرناها في « عقيدتنا الكبرى » و « شرحها » (١) ، وأقربُ الأدلَّةِ أن نقول : إذا كانَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِ الحوادثِ حادثاً في نفسِهِ .. فعدمُ جميعها ثابت في الأزلِ ، ثم لا يخلو : إمَّا أَنْ يقارنَ ذلكَ العدمَ فردٌ مِنَ الأفراد الحادثة أو لا ؛ فإن قارنَهُ لزم اجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ ؛ إِذْ ذلكَ الفردُ مِنْ جملةِ الأفرادِ التي تقرَّرَ عدمُها في الأزلِ ، واجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ محال بضرورة العقل ، وإنْ لم يقارن ذلكَ العدمَ شيءٌ مِنْ تلكَ الأفرادِ الحادثة.. لزمَ أنَّ لها أَوَّلاً ؛ لخلو الأزلِ على هذا الفَرْضِ عن جميعها .
As for the fifth — which is the proof of the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal — its proof is that if it ceased to exist, its existence would be possible, not necessary, and what is possible is only originated (muḥdath), so this eternal would be originated! And that is a contradiction. As for the sixth — which is the proof that bodies are not free from that extra (attribute) — it is necessary; because it is not conceivable for a body to be free from being, for example, moving or at rest. As for the seventh — which is the proof of the impossibility of events without a beginning — it has many proofs, which we mentioned in our 'Great Creed (ʿAqīdatunā al-Kubrā)' and its commentary (1). The closest of the proofs is to say: If every individual of events is in itself originated, then the non-existence of all of them is established in pre-eternity (al-azal). Then it is inevitable: either an individual from the originating events accompanies that non-existence or not. If it accompanies it, then the simultaneous coexistence of existence and non-existence of a thing follows, since that individual is among the individuals whose non-existence was established in pre-eternity. And the simultaneous coexistence of existence and non-existence of a thing is impossible by necessity of reason. And if nothing from those originating events accompanies that non-existence, then it follows that they have a beginning, because pre-eternity on this assumption is empty of all of them.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs for the fifth, sixth, and seventh of the seven principles (al-uṣūl al-sabʿa) used to demonstrate the creation of the world. The argument begins with the fifth principle: the impossibility of the eternal ceasing to exist. The proof is that if the eternal (i.e., God) could cease to exist, then its existence would be mere possibility (jāʾiz) rather than necessity (wājib), and only originated things are possible; thus the eternal would be originated, which is a contradiction. The sixth principle asserts that bodies (al-ajrām) are inseparable from an added attribute (al-zāʾid), such as motion or rest. This is self-evident (ḍarūrī), as one cannot conceive of a body that is neither moving nor at rest. The seventh principle is the impossibility of an infinite series of events without a beginning. Sanusi presents a concise proof: If every individual event is originated in itself, then all events are non-existent in pre-eternity (al-azal). Now either one particular event coexists with that pre-eternal non-existence or not. If it does, then that event would both exist (since it is present) and not exist (since all events are non-existent in pre-eternity), which is impossible. If no event coexists, then there must be a first moment when events begin, i.e., a first event, because pre-eternity is empty of all events. Thus an infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
الأجرام
الزائد
الحركة
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think a body can exist without any properties or attributes.
Correction: Sanusi asserts that it is inconceivable: a body must have some attribute like motion or rest. A body without any attribute is a logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the sixth principle necessary (ḍarūrī)?
A) Because it is proven by revelation.
B) Because it is self-evident and cannot be denied.
C) Because it is a conclusion from other principles.
D) Because it is based on empirical observation.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are always accompanied by an attribute such as motion or rest, a self-evident fact that supports the argument for creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is never without motion or rest—you are constantly in a state of change. How does this constant dependency on attributes remind you that you are not self-sufficient, but perpetually in need of the Creator who sustains you moment by moment? What will you do today to acknowledge that dependence?
?
If it is inconceivable for a body to exist without an attribute like motion or rest, then your very existence is tied to change. Does that unsettle your desire for permanence and control? How will you surrender the illusion of stability and place your trust in the One who never changes?

The Seventh Principle: Impossibility of Events Without a Beginning
Sanusi proves that an infinite series of events is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that an infinite series of events (a chain of originated things with no first) is impossible, establishing that the world had a beginning.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of originated events leads to a logical contradiction.

The seventh principle addresses a crucial question: can there be an infinite series of events? Sanusi provides a concise proof: if every event is originated, then all events are non-existent in pre-eternity. Either one event coexists with that non-existence (leading to simultaneous existence and non-existence) or not (implying a first event). Both options disprove an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully. Notice the two-case structure: either an event accompanies pre-eternal non-existence or not.
The proof uses the concept of al-azal (pre-eternity).
2
Understand the premise
The premise is that every individual event is originated (ḥādith) in itself. Therefore, before they exist, all events are non-existent in pre-eternity.
This is the starting point.
3
Analyze the first case
If an event accompanies that pre-eternal non-existence, then that event would both exist (since it is present) and not exist (since all events are non-existent in pre-eternity) — a contradiction.
This violates the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Analyze the second case
If no event accompanies, then pre-eternity is empty of all events, so there must be a first moment when events begin, i.e., a first event.
Thus events cannot be beginningless.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since both options lead to impossibility (contradiction or beginning), an infinite series of events is impossible.
This establishes that the world had a beginning.

Extracting Five Principles from One Argument
Sanusi extracts five principles from one argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single argument in kalam can simultaneously refute an opponent's position and establish multiple foundational principles.

Core Idea
One argument can yield many principles at once.

In this passage, Sanusi shows the remarkable efficiency of logical reasoning. From a single statement that refutes a specific position about bodies in pre-eternity, he extracts five of the seven principles needed for the proof of the world's creation. This demonstrates how deeply interconnected theological arguments are.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the transition from the earlier established principles to the new extraction.
The passage begins with a summary conclusion before showing the extraction.
2
Identify the five extracted principles
Sanusi explicitly states that from the statement refuting the third division, two principles are established, and then three more are derived from the same statement. List these five principles.
The first two: (1) bodies have an added attribute, (2) impossibility of being free from it. The next three: (3) accident not self-subsisting, (4) no transference, (5) no latency/manifestation.
3
Reflect on the final note
Sanusi mentions that the last three principles may not be essential for the basic proof of the creed. Why would he include them then? Consider their role in a more complete argument.
This shows the depth versus necessity in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا تقرَّرَتْ لكَ هذهِ السبعةُ الأصولِ بأدلَّتِها عرفت حينئذٍ حدوث كلٌّ مِنَ الحركة والسكون ؛ بدليل طروء وجودهما تارةً ، وعدمهما أخرى ، ولها مبداً ؛ لما عرفتَ مِن استحالة حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ، فلزمَ أنَّ الأجرام الملازمة لهما حادثةٌ لها مبدأ مثلهما ، وهو المطلوب

واعلم : أنَّ تقريرنا لبرهان حدوثِ العالم في أصلِ العقيدةِ يُؤخَذُ منه جواب هذه الأصول السبعة .

فيُؤخَذُ كونُ كلَّ جِرْمٍ يلازم معنى زائداً على ذاتِهِ - وهو كونه متحركاً أو ساكناً مثلاً - مِنْ قولِهِ في إبطال القسم الثالث : ( لا يُعقَلُ جِرْمٌ في الأزلِ ولا فيما لا يزالُ .. ليس ثابتاً في الحيّز ولا منتقلاً عنه ) ، ومعنى كونه ثابتاً في الحيّز هو معنى كونه ساكناً هو معنى كونه ساكناً ، وكونه منتقلاً عنه هو معنى كونه متحركاً ، فقد ثبت بهذا الكلام أصلانِ مِنَ السبعة ؛ وهما : إثبات زائد على الجِرْمِ ، وإثباتُ استحالة تعري الجِرْمِ عنه

وكذا أيضاً يُؤخَذُ مِنْ هذا الكلام إبطال قيامِ العَرَضِ بنفسِهِ وإبطال انتقالِهِ ، وإبطالُ كُمونِهِ وظهوره ؛ لأنَّه تضمَّنَ هذا الكلامُ أنَّ معنى السكونِ : ثبوتُ الجِرْمِ في حيّزه ، ومعنى الحركة : انتقاله عنه ، فلو كَمَنا ، أو انتقلا ، أو قاما بأنفسهما . . لانقلبَتْ حقيقتهما هذه ، وبطلتْ صفة نفسهما التي هي إيجابهما للجرْمِ أنْ يكونَ ثابتاً في الحيّز أو منتقلاً عنه ، وذلك محال

فقد تضمَّنَ هذا الكلامُ الواحد إثبات خمسةِ أصولٍ مِنَ السبعةِ . على أنَّ هذه الثلاثةَ الأخيرة قد لا يتوقَّفُ برهانُ العقيدة على
So when these seven principles are established for you with their proofs, you know thereby the createdness (ḥudūth) of each of motion and rest, by the evidence of their occurring sometimes and being absent other times, and they have a beginning, because you have known the impossibility of events without a beginning. So it follows that the bodies (al-ajrām) that are inseparable from them are originated, having a beginning like them, and that is what is sought. And know: that our formulation of the proof of the world's creation in the foundation of the creed (aṣl al-ʿaqīda) is taken from the answer to these seven principles. So it is taken that every body (jirm) accompanies an additional meaning (maʿnā zāʾid) beyond its essence — which is its being, for example, moving or at rest — from his statement in the refutation of the third division: 'A body is not conceivable in pre-eternity nor in what will never cease, that is not fixed in place nor transferred from it.' And the meaning of its being fixed in place is the meaning of its being at rest — is the meaning of its being at rest — and its being transferred from it is the meaning of its being moving. So by this statement two principles from the seven are established: the affirmation of an extra (attribute) upon the body, and the affirmation of the impossibility of the body being free from it. And likewise also from this statement is taken the nullification of an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting by itself, and the nullification of its transference, and the nullification of its latency (kumūn) and manifestation (ẓuhūr). Because this statement implies that the meaning of rest is the body's being fixed in its place, and the meaning of motion is its transference from it. So if they were latent, or transferred, or subsisted by themselves, then their true nature would be inverted, and the description of themselves that is their making the body be fixed in place or transferred from it would become void, and that is impossible. So this single statement includes the affirmation of five principles from the seven. However, the proof of the creed may not depend on these last three.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of the seven principles and shows how they are extracted from a single argument. Sanusi begins by stating that once the seven principles are established with their proofs, one can deduce the createdness (ḥudūth) of motion and rest, because they sometimes exist and sometimes do not, and they have a beginning due to the impossibility of an infinite series of events. Consequently, the bodies (al-ajrām) that are inseparable from motion and rest must also be created — that is the desired conclusion. Sanusi then reveals that the entire proof for the world's creation, as presented in his 'Great Creed', is derived from the answers to these seven principles. Specifically, from his statement in refuting the third division (of those who posit a state in pre-eternity) — namely, 'A body is not conceivable in pre-eternity nor in what will never cease, that is neither fixed in place nor transferred from it' — two principles are taken: (1) that bodies have an added attribute beyond their essence (i.e., motion or rest), and (2) that it is impossible for a body to be devoid of this attribute. Furthermore, from the same statement, three additional negations are derived: (3) the nullification of an accident subsisting by itself (since motion and rest are accidental and cannot subsist independently), (4) the nullification of the transference of an accident from one substance to another, and (5) the nullification of the latency and manifestation of accidents (i.e., that they can be hidden and then appear). These follow because the definition of rest is precisely a body's fixation in its place, and motion is its transference from that place; if accidents were independent, transferable, or latent, those definitions would break down. Thus, Sanusi highlights that a single argument yields five of the seven principles. However, he notes that the latter three (the negations) may not be essential for the proof of the creed in its basic form.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
الأجرام
الوجود
العدم
الأزل
العرض
الكُمون
الظهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the extraction is circular or merely asserted.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is a logical deduction: the refutation statement implies the definitions of motion and rest, which in turn necessitate the negations. The argument is squarely deductive.

Checkpoint
How many principles from the seven are extracted from the statement refuting the third division?
A) One
B) Two
C) Five
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
A single well-constructed argument can simultaneously negate an opponent's position and establish multiple foundational truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi shows that one argument can yield multiple foundational truths. Is there one truth about Allah – not a piece of information, but a deep conviction – that you have allowed to remain isolated from the rest of your life? What would it take for that single truth to reorganize your priorities, fears, and hopes? Name that truth and ask Allah to make it penetrate your heart today.
?
The passage teaches that motion and rest are not self-subsisting; they inhere in something else. Consider your own fleeting emotions – anger, joy, anxiety. Do you sometimes treat them as if they define who you are, as if they have an independent reality? Are you willing to see them as passing accidents that do not touch the essence of your soul? How would your day change if you truly believed that?
?
Sanusi notes that the last three principles may not be essential for the basic proof, yet they are still derived and true. In your pursuit of knowledge, do you sometimes dismiss insights that are 'not essential' for your immediate goal? What might you be missing by ignoring those extra truths? Commit to giving time today to one non-essential but enriching aspect of your faith.

The Three States Are Not Essential for the Proof of Temporality
Sanusi argues that refuting three states is not essential for the proof of temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why refuting the three states (quiescence, transmigration, and self-subsistence) is not strictly necessary for the proof of the temporality of the body, and how an apparent objection is resolved by clarifying the meaning of 'non-acceptance of non-existence'.

Core Idea
The proof of temporality does not require refuting every possible state of accidents.

Sanusi here addresses a potential objection to the standard proof for the temporality of bodies. The objection claims that the proof relies on refuting three specific states of accidents. Sanusi explains that the core proof is self-sufficient and that the refutation of those states, while perhaps helpful, is not logically required. He then clarifies a key phrase that might have been misunderstood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the proof
The proof for temporality is built on the premise: if a body existed in pre-eternity in a certain state (motion or rest), it could never change. Because an eternal state must be necessary or from a necessary cause, change would be impossible.
2
Identify the objection
An objector points to Sanusi's earlier statement that a pre-eternal rest 'does not cease' (does not accept non-existence). This seems to imply that one must refute the possibility of quiescence (an accident being latent and then appearing) to prove change. Find where this objection is raised.
The objection is implicit in the text: 'إلا أنَّ قولَهُ... يقتضي أنَّ...'
3
Examine Sanusi's clarification
Sanusi explains that 'does not accept non-existence' means the non-existence of the state (i.e., the body being in its place) from that body, not the non-existence of the accident itself. This state is necessary if assumed pre-eternal, so change is impossible regardless of what happens to the accident.
The key distinction is between the accident's own existence and the state of the body.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, refuting the three states (subsistence in itself, transmigration, quiescence/manifestation) is not essential for the proof. If someone insists it is, Sanusi notes that the creed already provides guidance on those issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثباتها (۱)؛ لأنَّ برهانها لما انبنى أولاً على كونِ الجِرْمِ لو وُجِدَ في الأزلِ مُتحرِّكاً أو ساكناً لَمَا انتقل عن الحالة التي كانَ عليها في الأزلِ . سواء فرضْتَ انتقالها إلى عدم أو كمون أو قيام بنفسها أو محل آخرَ؛ لأنَّ تلك الحالة إذا كانَتْ قديمة للجِرْمِ لزمَ أنْ تكونَ واجبةٌ لهُ أو مستندةً إلى واجب؛ لئلا يلزم التسلسل، فيلزم ألا يتغيَّر الجزمُ عنها أبداً، إلا أنَّ قولَهُ في إبطالِ كونِ الجِرْم ساكناً في الأزلِ : ( لأنَّ سكونَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ قديم، فلا ينعدم ) . . يقتضي أنَّهُ الدلالة على بنی على أنَّ السكونَ الموجِبَ لكونِ الجِرْم ساكناً لا يصح أن يكون قديماً؛ بدليل قبولِهِ لطريان العدم، وقد قدَّمنا أنَّ الاستدلال لطريانِ العدمِ يحتاج إلى تلك الأصول . وقد يُجابُ بأنَّ معنى قولِهِ : ( فلا يقبلُ العدمَ ) أي : عدم حالِهِ مِنْ ذلكَ الجِرْمِ؛ وهي كونُ الجِرْمِ ثابتاً في حيّزه، لوجوب هذه الحال له لمَّا فُرِضَتْ قديمة، سواءٌ انعدم هو في نفسِهِ أَوِ ادُّعِيَ انتقاله إلى تلك الأمور التي سبق بطلانها وبالجملة : فالتعرُّضُ لبطلانِ تلكَ الأمور الثلاثة لتصحيح برهان العقيدة.. غيرُ ضروري، وعلى تقدير أن يحتاج إليه - وهو الجزم - فقد سبق ما يرشد إلى ذلك مِنَ العقيدة (1) وهی : إبطال قيام العرض بنفسه، وإبطال انتقاله، وإبطال كمونه وظهوره، وعطف الظهور على الكمون من عطف اللازم على الملزوم .
Establishing it (1); because its proof was first built upon that if the body existed in pre-eternity moving or at rest, it would not transition from the state it was in pre-eternity. Whether you suppose its transition to non-existence, quiescence, self-subsistence, or another locus; because if that state were pre-eternal for the body, it would necessarily be obligatory for it or dependent on an obligatory being, to avoid infinite regress. Then it would necessarily not change from it ever (2). Except that his statement in refuting that the body is at rest in pre-eternity: 'Because its rest on this assumption is pre-eternal, so it does not cease'... requires that the indication is built on that the rest necessitating the body being at rest cannot be pre-eternal, due to its accepting the advent of non-existence. And we have previously stated that the inference of the advent of non-existence requires those principles. And it may be answered that the meaning of his statement: 'So it does not accept non-existence' is: the non-existence of its state from that body; which is the body being fixed in its place, due to the necessity of this state for it when it was assumed pre-eternal (3), whether it itself ceases to exist or its transmigration to those matters whose invalidity preceded is claimed. In summary: Engaging in refuting those three matters to correct the proof of the creed is not necessary, and if it were needed—and it is certain—then what guides to that from the creed has already preceded. (1) And they are: refuting the subsistence of an accident in itself, refuting its transmigration, and refuting its quiescence and manifestation, and the conjunction of manifestation upon quiescence is the conjunction of the necessary to the necessitated.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the proof for the temporality of the body, specifically addressing an objection that the proof requires refuting three states: the subsistence of an accident in itself, its transmigration, and its quiescence/manifestation. The author explains that the proof is built on the premise that if the body were pre-eternally in motion or at rest, it could never change from that state, because an eternal state must be obligatory or dependent on an eternal cause, thus change would be impossible. The objection arises from a statement that a pre-eternal rest does not cease, implying the need to refute quiescence (the idea that an accident could be latent and then appear). The author then argues that the statement 'it does not accept non-existence' means the non-existence of that state from the body, not the absolute non-existence of the accident. This is because the state (the body being in its place) is necessary if it is assumed pre-eternal, whether the accident itself ceases or is claimed to transmute. The author concludes that refuting these three matters is not strictly necessary for the proof; if it were, the creed already provides the guidance.

السكون
الحركة
العدم
الكمون
الظهور
قيام العرض بنفسه
انتقال العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof for temporality requires a full refutation of quiescence (الكمون) and transmigration (الانتقال) of accidents, otherwise the proof fails.
Correction: The author shows that the proof only requires the impossibility of change from a pre-eternal state, which holds regardless of what happens to the accident itself. The three states are not essential to refute for the proof to be sound.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the statement 'so it does not accept non-existence' mean?
A) The accident does not cease to exist absolutely.
B) The state of the body (its being in its place) does not cease, not the accident itself.
C) The body itself does not cease to exist.
D) The rest is not subject to non-existence because it is pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
The proof of temporality does not depend on refuting the three states of accidents; it suffices that a pre-eternal state cannot change because it is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems incomplete because it hasn't addressed every possible counterexample, are you quick to dismiss it? This lesson shows that sometimes the core argument stands without addressing all peripheral objections. How does this challenge your intellectual approach to matters of faith? Will you commit to examining the core of an argument before demanding exhaustive refutations?
?
The author states that a pre-eternal state is necessary and thus unchanging. This underscores that only Allah has necessary existence. Consider a time when you desperately wanted something in your life to be permanent, only to see it change. How does the concept of pre-eternal necessity (only in Allah) give you a firm anchor? Can you trust that what is necessary in Allah will never change, even when your circumstances do?

The Impossibility of Beginningless Events
Sanusi proves impossibility of beginningless events via motion/rest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that proves events cannot be beginningless, based on the contingent nature of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are contingent—they cannot be pre-eternal without absurdity.

Sanusi now addresses a key objection: if the world has a beginning, then before that beginning there must have been a state without events. But the kalam tradition argues that such a state would be impossible because the world (bodies) always require either motion or rest. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key premises
The passage argues that motion and rest 'accept change' (i.e., are contingent). This is the first premise. The second premise is that if they were pre-eternal, non-existence would be necessary for each, but then if one existed, existence and non-existence would combine.
Contingent things cannot be eternal by definition.
3
Understand the absurdity
The absurdity is that the same thing would both exist and not exist simultaneously, which is impossible. This shows that motion and rest cannot be pre-eternal.
4
Connect to beginningless events
If motion and rest have a beginning, then all events (which involve motion/rest) must have a beginning. Thus, events without a beginning are impossible.
This is the conclusion of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثباتُ استحالةِ عدم القديم : فقد تعرَّضَ في أصل العقيدة لبرهانه على أكمل وجه (۱) . وأمَّا بطلان حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها : فيُؤخَذُ مِنْ إقامة البرهان على استحالة ثبوتِ كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ السكون والحركة للجرْمِ في الأزلِ؛ لقبولِهما التبدُّلَ فيما لا يزالُ، وإذا وجبَ العدم لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما في الأزلِ، فلو وُجِدَ واحد منهما في الأزلِ لاجتمع وجود الشيء وعدمه ! محال لا يُعقَلُ، وهذا مِنْ براهين استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها، فقد ثبت إذاً أنَّ الحركة والسكونَ لهما أوّل، وذلك يستلزم أنْ يكونَ للجزم الملازم لهما أوّل أيضاً، وهو المطلوب. فقدِ اتَّضحَ لكَ : تضمُّنُ برهان العقيدة للأصول السبعة باختصار، وذلكَ مِنْ محاسنِها، وكم فيها مِنْ محاسنَ ! والله سبحانه الموفّقُ لا أثر لمن سواه في أثر ما على العموم . تنبيه الأول : في بيان بعض جمل متن « العقيدة الوسطى » : ( مِنْ حركةٍ وسكون وغيرهما ) يعني بالغيرِ : الألوان ونحوها، وإنَّما صرَّحَ بالحركة والسكون دون غيرهما؛ لأنَّ انفكاكِ الجِرْمِ عنهما ضروري للعقل، ومعرفة حدوثهما مع وضوح عدم (۱) انظر ( ص ۹۲ ) .
As for establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the pre-eternal: it was addressed in the original creed with its proof in the most complete manner (1). As for the falsity of events without a beginning: it is taken from establishing the proof of the impossibility of the existence of each of rest and motion for the body in pre-eternity; because they accept change in what is without end. If non-existence is necessary for each of them in pre-eternity, then if one of them existed in pre-eternity, the existence of a thing and its non-existence would have combined! An impossibility not conceivable. And this is from the proofs of the impossibility of events without a beginning. So it is established that motion and rest have a beginning, and that necessitates that the body accompanying them also has a beginning, which is what is sought. Thus it has become clear to you: the proof of the creed includes the seven principles concisely, and that is from its excellences, and how many excellences it contains! And Allah, the Exalted, is the Giver of success. No effect of anyone other than Him in any effect in general. First warning: In explaining some sentences of the text of 'Al-Aqida al-Wusta': (From motion and rest and others) meaning by 'others': colors and the like. He specified motion and rest to the exclusion of others because the body's being free of them is rationally necessary, and knowing their temporality with the clarity of non-existence (1) see (p. 92).
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage completes the defense of the proof and draws conclusions. First, the author states that the proof for the impossibility of the non-existence of the pre-eternal (i.e., God) is already covered in the creed. Second, the falsity of events without a beginning (i.e., the world having a beginning) is derived by proving that motion and rest cannot be pre-eternal in a body. They accept change in the future, so each must be contingent. If both were impossible in pre-eternity, then if one existed, that would mean a thing both exists and does not exist simultaneously—an absurdity. Thus motion and rest have a beginning, and the body (which cannot exist without either motion or rest) must also have a beginning. This is the desired conclusion. The author then remarks that this proof of the creed encompasses the seven principles concisely, praising its excellence. The paragraph ends with a note (First warning) explaining a phrase from the text of 'Al-Aqida al-Wusta': 'From motion and rest and others'—'others' means colors etc. Motion and rest are specified because it is rationally impossible for a body to be free of them (a body is always either in motion or at rest), and their temporality is easily known. The reference to page 92 is given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof of the creed is incomplete without a separate proof for the impossibility of beginningless events.
Correction: The proof is derived from the same argument: the impossibility of motion/rest being pre-eternal directly shows events cannot be beginningless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must motion and rest have a beginning?
A) Because they are accidents and accidents are temporally originated.
B) Because if they were pre-eternal, they would combine existence and non-existence in the same entity.
C) Because the body cannot be free of them, so they must be created.
D) Because they accept change, so each must be contingent and have a beginning.

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest cannot be pre-eternal because they are contingent; this proves events have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The proof shows that even the most fundamental changes (motion and rest) could not be eternal. Consider the most permanent thing in your life—your own soul, your wealth, your relationships. Do you treat any of these as if they are self-existent and unending? How does this revelation reshape your perspective on your possessions and your life?
?
If all events require a first cause and you are an event that began, then your accountability to Allah is inescapable. Are you prepared to meet your Creator, having taken this truth seriously? What is one concrete step you can take right now to align your life with this reality?

The Seven Principles and Why Motion and Rest Are Specified
Sanusi concludes the proof, highlights seven principles, and clarifies mention of motion and rest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of the creed concisely contains the seven principles and why motion and rest are specified in the creed's formulation.

Core Idea
The creed's proof wraps up the seven principles, and motion/rest are singled out because a body cannot be without them.

Having established that motion and rest have a beginning, Sanusi draws the final conclusion: the body must also have a beginning. He then praises the creed's proof for encapsulating the seven principles. Finally, he clarifies why the creed specifically mentions motion and rest rather than other accidents like colors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from conclusion to praise to a technical warning.
The passage has three parts: conclusion, praise, and warning.
2
Understand the conclusion
The passage states that motion and rest have a beginning, and therefore the body (which cannot exist without them) also has a beginning.
This is the desired result: the world is temporally created.
3
Appreciate the seven principles
The author notes that the proof concisely contains the seven principles. Reflect on what these might be (the key steps in a kalam argument).
The seven principles are found in other works; they are the logical structure of the proof.
4
Examine the warning
The warning clarifies why motion and rest are specifically mentioned: because a body cannot be free of them (it is always either moving or at rest), and their temporality is clear.
Other accidents like colors are included but not specified.

As for establishing the impossibility of non-existence of the pre-eternal: it was addressed in the original creed with its proof in the most complete manner (1). The Conditional Proof Structure: Evidence Substituted for Brevity
Explains the rhetorical structure: evidence replaces conditional for brevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence to stand in for the conditional statement in the proof for temporality.

Core Idea
In kalam proofs, evidence can replace the conditional for brevity.

In this lesson, we unpack the commentator's explanation of the rhetorical structure of the proof. The original text states 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' This sentence, as the commentator reveals, is not the proof itself but evidence for a conditional that has been omitted for brevity. Understanding this substitution is key to grasping the logic of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source passage carefully. Notice how the commentator identifies the sentence as evidence for a conditional.
The Arabic uses ellipsis (...) to indicate the text is being referenced.
2
Identify the omitted conditional
The conditional is: 'If it were so, it would never accept to move at all.' The evidence supports the opposite: 'But it does accept to move and to cease by its rest.'
3
Understand the substitution
The author omitted the conditional and placed its evidence in its place for brevity. This is a common technique in kalam texts to compress arguments.
Recognizing this helps avoid confusion about what is being argued.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Brevity allows scholars to convey dense arguments concisely, but requires careful reading to reconstruct the full logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودليلُ قَبولِ السكون العدمَ ... ) إلى آخره : هذهِ الجملة دليلٌ على الاستثنائية المشار إليها في قوله : ( لو كان كذلك لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرّك أبداً ) ، وهي قولنا : لكنَّه يقبلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ وينعدم بسكونه ، فاستدل عليها بقولِهِ : ( ودليلُ قَبول السكون العدمَ . . . ) إلى آخره ، فحذف الاستثنائية ، وأقام دليلها مُقامَها اختصاراً .
قوله : ( ولا بدَّ أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ) يعني : لا بدَّ أن يسبقها الوجودُ للجسم في القدْرِ مِنَ الفراغ الذي انتقل عنه الجِرْمُ ، فـ ( الكونُ ) : بمعنى الوجودِ ، مصدر ( كانَ ) التامة، لا الناقصة .
His saying: 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' to the end: this sentence is evidence for the conditional implied in his saying: 'If it were so, it would never accept to move at all', and it is our statement: 'But it does accept to move and to cease by its rest'. So he provided evidence for it by his saying: 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' to the end, thus deleting the conditional and substituting its evidence for it for brevity.
His saying: 'And it is necessary that existence in the space moved from precede its existence' — meaning: it is necessary that the body's existence in the amount of emptiness from which the body moved must precede it. So 'al-kawn' means existence, the infinitive of 'kana' al-tammah (the complete verb), not the incomplete one.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage comprises two distinct explanations. First, the commentator explains the rhetorical structure of the proof: the base text says 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' This sentence serves as evidence (daliil) for a conditional (istithna'iyya) that was implied earlier: 'If it were so, it would never accept to move at all.' The conditional is our statement: 'But it does accept to move and to cease by its rest.' So the author omitted the conditional and replaced it with its evidence for brevity. In essence, the proof is: bodies accept motion and non-existence, therefore they cannot be eternal. Second, the commentator explains another condition for motion: 'It is necessary that existence in the space moved from precede its existence.' This means that for a body to move from one space to another, it must first have existed in the space from which it moves. The term 'kawn' here means existence, derived from the complete verb 'kana' (to be), not the incomplete (which indicates a state). This emphasizes that the body's prior existence is a separate condition for the occurrence of motion.

The Necessity of Prior Existence in the Space of Departure
Explains the necessity of prior existence in the space moved from and clarifies the term 'kawn'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the body must have prior existence in the space it moves from, and what the term 'kawn' means in this context.

Core Idea
For a body to move, it must first exist in the space it leaves.

This lesson focuses on a specific condition for motion: the body must have existed in the space from which it moves before the motion occurs. The commentator clarifies that the term 'kawn' in this context means existence (from the complete verb 'kana'), not a state. This subtle point prevents a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the second part of the source passage. Pay attention to the phrase 'existence in the space moved from'.
2
Understand the condition
The commentator states that it is necessary for the body's existence in the space it leaves to precede its existence elsewhere. This is a logical prerequisite for motion.
This condition is part of the larger proof for temporality.
3
Distinguish the meaning of 'kawn'
The term 'al-kawn' here means existence, derived from 'kana al-tammah' (the complete verb). It does not mean 'being' as a state (which would be from the incomplete verb).
This distinction prevents confusion with other uses of 'kana'.
4
Connect to the proof for temporality
The condition reinforces that bodies are contingent: they need prior existence in a space, implying they are not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودليلُ قَبولِ السكون العدمَ ... ) إلى آخره : هذهِ الجملة دليلٌ على الاستثنائية المشار إليها في قوله : ( لو كان كذلك لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرّك أبداً ) ، وهي قولنا : لكنَّه يقبلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ وينعدم بسكونه ، فاستدل عليها بقولِهِ : ( ودليلُ قَبول السكون العدمَ . . . ) إلى آخره ، فحذف الاستثنائية ، وأقام دليلها مُقامَها اختصاراً .
قوله : ( ولا بدَّ أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ) يعني : لا بدَّ أن يسبقها الوجودُ للجسم في القدْرِ مِنَ الفراغ الذي انتقل عنه الجِرْمُ ، فـ ( الكونُ ) : بمعنى الوجودِ ، مصدر ( كانَ ) التامة، لا الناقصة .
His saying: 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' to the end: this sentence is evidence for the conditional implied in his saying: 'If it were so, it would never accept to move at all', and it is our statement: 'But it does accept to move and to cease by its rest'. So he provided evidence for it by his saying: 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' to the end, thus deleting the conditional and substituting its evidence for it for brevity.
His saying: 'And it is necessary that existence in the space moved from precede its existence' — meaning: it is necessary that the body's existence in the amount of emptiness from which the body moved must precede it. So 'al-kawn' means existence, the infinitive of 'kana' al-tammah (the complete verb), not the incomplete one.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage comprises two distinct explanations. First, the commentator explains the rhetorical structure of the proof: the base text says 'And the evidence of rest's acceptance of non-existence...' This sentence serves as evidence (daliil) for a conditional (istithna'iyya) that was implied earlier: 'If it were so, it would never accept to move at all.' The conditional is our statement: 'But it does accept to move and to cease by its rest.' So the author omitted the conditional and replaced it with its evidence for brevity. In essence, the proof is: bodies accept motion and non-existence, therefore they cannot be eternal. Second, the commentator explains another condition for motion: 'It is necessary that existence in the space moved from precede its existence.' This means that for a body to move from one space to another, it must first have existed in the space from which it moves. The term 'kawn' here means existence, derived from the complete verb 'kana' (to be), not the incomplete (which indicates a state). This emphasizes that the body's prior existence is a separate condition for the occurrence of motion.

الحيّز المنتقل عنه

Correction: The commentator specifies it is the complete verb meaning existence, not the incomplete verb used as a copula.

The Need for a Specifier in the Existence of Rest
Sanusi argues that rest requires a specifier due to its potential for non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the existence of rest (sukūn) at a particular time necessitates a specifying agent (fāʿil), establishing the world's creation.

Core Idea
Rest's existence in time requires a specifier because it could have been non-existent.

Sanusi continues his proof of the world's creation by examining the nature of rest (sukūn). He argues that since rest can accept non-existence, its existence at a specific moment must be due to a specifying agent. This reasoning forms the core of the demonstration for the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rest and specifier
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Sanusi connects the possibility of non-existence to the need for a specifier.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage contains a conditional: 'If rest were eternal, it would never accept motion.' Find the exceptive premise and its proof.
The exceptive premise is 'but it accepts to move'.
3
Understand the logical structure
Sanusi shows that the proof that rest accepts non-existence is evidence for the exceptive premise. This demonstrates that rest cannot be eternal, so it must be created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِنْ غيرِ فاعل .. لكانَ تخصيصاً مِنْ غيرِ مُخصص ، وهو ظاهرُ الاستحالة ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ فاعل يُخصِّصُ وجود السكون بالحدوث بدلاً عن العدمِ الذي يزاحم الوجودَ ؛ لقَبولِ الذاتِ له على حسَبِ قَبولِها الوجود . قوله : ( ودليلُ قَبولِ السكون العدمَ ... ) إلى آخره : هذهِ الجملة دليلٌ على الاستثنائية المشار إليها في قوله : ( لو كان كذلك لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرّك أبداً ) ، وهي قولنا : لكنَّه يقبلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ وينعدم بسكونه ، فاستدل عليها بقولِهِ : ( ودليلُ قَبول السكون العدمَ . . . ) إلى آخره ، فحذف الاستثنائية ، وأقام دليلها مُقامَها اختصاراً . قوله : ( ولا بدَّ أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ( يعني : لا بدَّ أن يسبقها الوجودُ للجزم في القدْرِ مِنَ الفراغ الذي انتقل عنه الجِرْمُ ، فـ ( الكونُ ) : بمعنى الوجودِ ، مصدر ( كانَ ) التامة ، لا الناقصة قوله : ( من عرشِهِ إِلى فَرْشِهِ ) ، أي : مِنْ طرفِهِ الأعلى إلى طرفه الأسفل ، والمراد : تعميم الحدوثِ لجميع أجزائه . قوله : ( لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ شيءٌ منه قديماً ) يعني : لا يُدرَكُ في العقلِ قدمُهُ بعد ما اتَّضحَتْ ملازمته للحركة والسكون اللذين قام البرهان على استحالة وجودِهما في الأزلِ ، وليس المعنى : أنَّهُ لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ قدمُهُ بدءاً قبلَ اتّضاحِ هذا البرهان ، وإلا كانَتْ معرفة حدوثِهِ ضروريةً لا نظريةً ، وهو باطل باتفاق العقلاء فقدِ اتَّضحَ بهذا : أنَّ قدمَ العالمِ مِنَ المستحيل النظري ، لا مِنَ المستحيل الضروري ، وقد سبق لكَ في شرح أقسام الحكم العقلي انقسام المستحيل إليهما
From without an agent … it would be a specification without a specifier, and that is clearly impossible. So necessarily there must be an agent who specifies the existence of rest (sukūn) by coming-to-be (ḥudūth) instead of non-existence (ʿadam) which vies with existence (wujūd); because the essence accepts it according to its acceptance of existence. His statement: (And the proof of rest's acceptance of non-existence … etc.): this sentence is a proof for the exceptive [premise] referred to in his statement: (If it were so, it would never accept to move at all), and it is our statement: but it accepts to move and to cease to be by its rest. So he inferred [that] by his statement: (And the proof of rest's acceptance of non-existence … etc.), thus he omitted the exceptive [premise] and substituted its proof for it for brevity. His statement: (And necessarily preceding its existence is the existence (kawn) in the space from which it moved) means: existence must precede it in order to determine the amount of space from which the body moved. So (kawn) means existence, the masdar of the complete verb kāna, not the incomplete. His statement: (From His throne to His carpet) i.e., from its upper extremity to its lower extremity, and the intent is the generalization of contingency (ḥudūth) to all its parts. His statement: (It is not conceivable in reason that any part of it is eternal) means: its eternity is not perceived in reason after its necessary association with motion and rest, which demonstration has proven impossible to exist in pre-eternity (azal). It does not mean that its eternity is not conceivable in reason initially before the clarification of this demonstration, otherwise knowledge of its contingency would be necessary (ḍarūrī) rather than theoretical (naẓarī), and that is false by consensus of rational people. So it has become clear by this: that the eternity (qidam) of the world is among the theoretical impossibilities (al-mustaḥīl al-naẓarī), not among the necessary impossibilities (al-mustaḥīl al-ḍarūrī), and it has preceded for you in the explanation of the divisions of the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) the division of the impossible into these two.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage continues the proof of the world's creation. It explains that the existence of rest (sukūn) at a particular time requires a specific agent (fāʿil) because if rest were eternal, it could not accept motion. The text clarifies that the proof that rest accepts non-existence is the evidence for the exceptive premise in a conditional argument. It then explains that before motion, there must be existence (kawn) in the space from which the body departs, meaning the body must exist to occupy that space. The phrase 'from His throne to His carpet' means from top to bottom, indicating that all parts of the body are subject to change. The impossibility of any part being eternal is not self-evident but is realized after the demonstration that motion and rest cannot exist in pre-eternity. If it were self-evident, knowledge of contingency would be necessary, not theoretical, which is false. Therefore, the eternity of the world is a theoretical impossibility, not a necessary one. This distinction is important: something can be proven impossible by reason but not be obviously impossible at first glance. The passage concludes by referring to the earlier classification of rational judgments into necessary, possible, and impossible.

Key Terms
فاعل
تخصيص
السكون
العدم
الوجود
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fact that rest is eternal.
B) The fact that rest accepts non-existence.
C) The fact that rest is observed.
D) The fact that rest is a property of bodies.

Key Takeaway
The existence of rest in time requires a specifying agent because rest could have been non-existent, proving that the world is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If rest—a state you experience daily—requires a Specifier, what does that mean for your own existence? You are not self-sufficient; every moment you exist is sustained by Allah. How will you live today differently in light of this constant dependence?
?
Think of an area in your life where you feel 'at rest'—secure or default. This lesson shows that even such neutral states are contingent and need a Cause. Are you taking anything for granted? Commit to acknowledging Allah's preservation in that area today.
?
Sanusi argues that even the possibility of rest's non-existence proves a Specifier. Is there any part of your life where you act as if you are self-caused? What would it look like to truly depend on the Specifier for everything, even your stillness?

Theoretical vs. Necessary Impossibility of the World's Eternity
Sanusi distinguishes theoretical from necessary impossibility regarding the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between theoretical impossibility (al-mustaḥīl al-naẓarī) and necessary impossibility (al-mustaḥīl al-ḍarūrī) and understand why the eternity of the world is a theoretical impossibility.

Core Idea
The world's eternity is a theoretical impossibility, not self-evident, requiring demonstration.

Sanusi concludes his proof by clarifying that the impossibility of the world's eternity is not immediately obvious but is established through rational demonstration. He distinguishes between two types of impossibility: necessary (self-evident) and theoretical (requiring proof). This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of rational judgment (ḥukm ʿaqlī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on motion and the impossibility distinction
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Focus on the logical reasoning that connects motion and rest to the impossibility of eternity.
2
Understand the argument for theoretical impossibility
The passage explains that the impossibility of any part of the world being eternal is not self-evident but is realized after the proof that motion and rest cannot exist in pre-eternity.
3
Distinguish theoretical from necessary impossibility
Sanusi refers to the earlier classification of rational judgments. He notes that if the impossibility were necessary, knowledge of the world's contingency would be self-evident, which is false. Hence it is theoretical.

The Importance of the Four Principles for Proving the World's Creation
A quote on the importance of knowing the world's origination and its four principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the four principles for proving the world's creation are considered essential and how ignorance of them is likened to darknesses in the Quran.

Core Idea
The proof of creation depends on four principles; ignoring them is like stumbling in four darknesses.

In this brief but powerful passage, a group of allusive scholars (ahl al-ishāra) stresses that the entire proof for the world's creation rests on four foundational principles. They even quote the Quran to warn that ignorance of these principles is like being lost in layers of darkness. This lesson unpacks why these principles are so central to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the tone of urgency: 'must pay attention' and 'depends on them'.
2
Identify the key elements
Look for: the four principles, their expansion into seven, and the Quranic reference. The passage does not name the principles themselves but tells you they are crucial.
The Quranic verse reference is Sūrat al-Nūr 24:40.
3
Reflect on the Quranic analogy
Why would the 'four darknesses' be a fitting metaphor for ignorance of these four principles? Consider how darkness prevents clear sight, just as ignorance blocks understanding of creation's contingency.
4
Connect to the broader study
This passage is an introduction to the upcoming lessons that will detail the four principles. Understand that mastering these principles is not optional—it is the foundation for proving that the world is created.
Your future lessons will explain each principle in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ بعضُ أهل الإشارةِ : يجب أن يُعتنى بمعرفةِ حدوثِ العالم ، وتحقق أصولِهِ الأربعة التي تنفصلُ إلى سبعة ؛ لتوقف برهان حدوثِ العالم عليها ، حتى قيل : إنَّ الجهالاتِ بتلك الأصول الأربعة هي التي استعير لها الظلمات الأربع في قوله تعالى : أَوْ كَظُلُمَتِ فِي بَحْرٍ لجي ...) [النور : ٤٠] الآية
Some of the people of allusion said: One must pay attention to knowing the origination of the world and verifying its four principles, which separate into seven, because the proof of the world's origination depends on them, such that it was said: Verily, the ignorances concerning those four principles are those for which the four darknesses were borrowed in His saying: 'Or like darknesses in a vast deep sea...' [24:40] the verse.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an exhortation by a group referred to as 'people of allusion' (ahl al-ishāra), likely Sufi or esoteric commentators. They stress the importance of studying the world's createdness (ḥudūth al-ʻālam) and establishing its four foundational principles, which can be further subdivided into seven. The entire proof of the world's creation depends on these principles. The gravity of ignorance regarding these principles is highlighted by a quotation from the Qur'an (Sūrat al-Nūr 24:40), where the four darknesses are used as a metaphor. Thus, the passage underscores the centrality of these principles in kalam cosmology and epistemology.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the proof of the world's creation depends on these four principles, and ignorance of them is likened to the four darknesses, showing they are foundational.

The Five Opinions on Divine Guidance
Lists five opinions on the meaning of divine guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five scholarly opinions on what 'هدى الله' (guidance of Allah) means in the Quran.

Core Idea
Guidance is multifaceted: faith, knowledge, proof, the Book, or clear exposition.

Scholars have long debated the exact nature of Allah's guidance mentioned in the Quran. This passage lists five distinct views, each highlighting a different aspect of how Allah guides His servants. Recognizing these interpretations helps us appreciate the depth of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of opinions
Read the opening sentence which lists the five opinions: faith, knowledge, proof, the Book, and clear exposition.
Note that these are not necessarily contradictory but complementary.
2
Identify each opinion
Go through each opinion and think about what it emphasizes as the core of guidance.
3
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why there is such a range of interpretations. Does guidance encompass all these elements?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلف الناسُ في هدى الله تعالى الذي يهدي بِهِ مَنْ يَشَاءُ مِنْ عبادِهِ ، فقيل : هو الإيمانُ ، وقيل : هو العلم ، وقيل : هو الدليلُ ، وقيل : هو الكتاب ، وقيل : هو البيانُ . فأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ : هو الإيمانُ . . فقد نظر إلى قوله سبحانه وتعالى : اللهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور : ٣٥] ، قالَ ابنُ عباس : هادي أهلِ السماوات والأرض . قالوا : وإِنَّما كَنَّى بالمشكاة .. عن صدرِ المؤمن ؛ فإنَّ المشكاة الكَوَّةُ غيرُ النافذة ، وكنَّى بالمصباح عن الإيمان الثابت في قلبه ، وكنى بالزجاجةِ عن القلبِ ، والشجرةُ المباركة دعوة الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلامُ وسَنتُهمُ القويم ، وذكر الزيتونة لصفاء دُهنِها ، وأَنَّ الفساد لا يدخلُهُ ، فلا يتعفَّنُ ولا يخامرُ العقل ويفسده ، وهو يعلو على كل ما يُجعَلُ معَهُ مِنَ المائعاتِ ، فكذلك الإيمانُ لا يتضعضعُ أركانه ، ولا يضعفُ سلطانه ، ولا يتحيَّرُ برهانه ، ولا يُخافُ معَ الغفلة نقصانه ، وهو الظاهرُ على كل دين ؛ لِيُظْهِرَهُ عَلَى الدِّينِ كُلِّهِ وَلَوْ كَرِهَ الْمُشْرِكُونَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٣]
Then people differed concerning the guidance of Allah the Exalted by which He guides whom He wills from His servants. It was said: it is faith; and it was said: it is knowledge; and it was said: it is the proof; and it was said: it is the Book; and it was said: it is the clear exposition. As for the one who said: it is faith—he looked to His saying, the Exalted: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' [Quran 24:35]. Ibn Abbas said: He is the guide of the people of the heavens and the earth. They said: He only metaphorically referred by the niche to the chest of the believer, for the niche is a non-penetrating recess; and He metaphorically referred by the lamp to the faith firmly established in his heart; and He metaphorically referred by the glass to the heart; and the blessed tree is the call of the prophets, peace be upon them, and their upright sunna; and He mentioned the olive for the purity of its oil and that corruption does not enter it, so it does not putrefy, nor does it mix with the intellect and corrupt it, and it rises above all liquids mixed with it. Likewise, faith: its pillars do not weaken, its authority does not falter, its proof is not perplexed, and its decrease is not feared with heedlessness; and it is manifest over every religion: 'That He may make it prevail over all religion, even though the polytheists dislike it' [Quran 9:33].
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage begins by enumerating the differing scholarly opinions on what exactly is meant by 'the guidance of Allah' (هدى الله) in the Quran. Five views are listed: that it is faith (الإيمان), knowledge (العلم), the proof (الدليل), the Book (الكتاب), or the clear exposition (البيان). The author then focuses on the first view—that it is faith—and supports it with Quran 24:35 (the Light Verse) and Ibn Abbas's interpretation. The explanation unpacks the figurative language of the Verse: the niche (المشكاة) represents the believer's chest; the lamp (المصباح) represents faith in the heart; the glass (الزجاجة) represents the heart itself; the blessed olive tree represents the prophetic call and sunna; and the pure oil that is not corrupted represents the clarity and incorruptibility of faith. Just as olive oil rises above other liquids, faith is superior to all other things and its proof is never confounded. The passage concludes by citing Quran 9:33, indicating that faith is manifest over all religions. The key theological nuance is that faith is not a mere mental assent but a robust, divinely illuminated reality that withstands neglect and confusion.

الإيمان
العلم
الدليل

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Faith as Guidance in the Light Verse
Explains the Light Verse as evidence that guidance is faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Light Verse (Quran 24:35) is interpreted to show that divine guidance is faith, using metaphors of the niche, lamp, glass, and olive tree.

Core Idea
Faith is a robust, divinely illuminated reality that cannot be corrupted.

After listing opinions, the author focuses on the view that guidance is faith (الإيمان) and supports it with a detailed interpretation of the Light Verse. This exegesis, attributed to Ibn Abbas, unpacks the verse's symbols to reveal the nature of true faith as steadfast and superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the Light Verse interpretation
Read the passage from 'فأما من قال' to the end, noting each metaphor.
2
Identify the four metaphors

The niche is the chest, the lamp is faith, the glass is the heart, the olive tree is the prophetic call.
3
Reflect on the olive oil's properties
Consider why pure olive oil is mentioned: it does not corrupt, mixes but rises above other liquids.
This illustrates faith's purity and superiority.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that faith does not weaken, its proof is not perplexed, and it will prevail over all religions, citing Quran 9:33.

Rational Proofs as the Foundation of Faith
Sanusi asserts the primacy of rational proofs and distinguishes between fitting praise and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of rational proofs in establishing the foundation of faith and the distinction between what reason can know versus what is obligatory only through revelation.

Core Idea
Rational proofs lead to knowledge of God, but obligation comes only from revelation.

Sanusi clarifies a critical point: true faith is not blind imitation but is grounded in rational knowledge and conclusive proofs. He even suggests that a person can properly praise God using reason alone—yet the binding nature of that praise is only known through divine law. This distinction preserves the value of reason while upholding the necessity of revelation for religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the Quranic quote and the phrase 'people of allusion' which refers to Sufi scholars.
2
Identify the main claims
Identify that faith is based on rational proofs, not imitation. Then note the example: rational proofs allow praise of God even without revelation, but obligation is only known through revelation.
3
Understand the Quranic reference
The quote from the Light Verse is used to illustrate how rational insight (like oil) can almost illuminate divine truths without the 'fire' of revelation. Consider how this supports the argument.
The 'people of allusion' interpret the verse as referring to the intellect's ability.
4
Grasp the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between what is rationally fitting (praising God) and what is obligatory (only known through revelation). This is a subtle but important theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ الإيمان أصله المعارفُ العقليَّةُ ، والأدلة البرهانية ، لا يكونُ عن تقليد ، وإِنَّما يكون عن نظر سديد . يَكَادُ زَيْتُهَا يُضِيءُ وَلَوْ لَمْ تَمْسَسْهُ نَارٌ نُورُ عَلَى نُورٍ ﴾ [النور : ٣٥] : قالَ أهل الإشارة : يكادُ العارفُ بأدلَّةِ عقلِهِ يعرفُ أدب الحضرة معَ اللهِ سبحانه وتعالى . وقد قيل : إنَّ الأدلَّةَ العقليَّةَ لمَّا دلت على وحدانيته سبحانه وتنزُّهِهِ . . استقامَ للعارفِ أَنْ يُثني عليهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ولو لم يَرِدْ شرع ولكن لا يُعلَمُ افتراض ذلك إلا بالشرع.
For the foundation of faith is rational knowledges and demonstrative proofs; it is not from imitation, but rather from sound consideration. 'Its oil almost illuminates even if no fire touches it—light upon light' [Quran 24:35]. The people of allusion said: The knower, by the proofs of his intellect, almost knows the etiquette of the court with Allah the glorified and exalted. And it has been said: When the rational proofs indicate His unity, glorified be He, and His transcendence, it is proper for the knower to praise Him, mighty and majestic, even if no revealed law has come—but it is not known that this is obligatory except through revelation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage develops the earlier point that faith is not blind imitation but is rooted in rational cognition and conclusive proofs. It quotes the final part of the Light Verse ('its oil almost illuminates...') and cites the 'people of allusion' (the Sufis) who say that a knower, using his intellect, can discern proper adab in the divine presence. The passage then presents a critical theological principle: rational proofs can establish God's oneness and transcendence, and therefore it is fitting for the knower to praise God even without revelation—but the obligatory nature of that praise is only known through divine law. This distinguishes between what reason can know theoretically (God's existence and attributes) and what it cannot establish as a binding duty (the specific religious obligations). The argument affirms the value of reason while preserving the necessity of revelation for determining legal and ritual obligations.

Key Terms

الأدلة البرهانية
تقليد
نظر سديد
أهل الإشارة
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reason alone is sufficient for all religious duties.
Correction: While reason can know God and offer praise, the specific obligatoriness of acts is only known through revelation.

Key Takeaway
Rational proofs establish the foundation of faith and allow proper praise of God, but the obligatoriness of praise is known only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reason and revelation as light and vision
Explains the Quranic phrase 'light upon light' as combining rational proofs and revealed guidance, with an analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complementary relationship between reason and revelation as illustrated by the Quranic phrase 'light upon light' and the analogy of sight and light.

Core Idea
Reason is like sight; revelation is like light—both are needed to see truth.

This passage offers a beautiful synthesis of the roles of reason and revelation in Islamic theology. Using the Qur'anic metaphor 'light upon light' and an analogy from scholars, it shows that intellect and divine guidance are not rivals but partners. As we read, notice how each 'light' addresses a different domain: one helps us know God, the other helps us know His commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'برهان العقل' (proof of intellect) and 'نهج الشرع' (path of revelation).
2
Identify the two 'lights'
The passage interprets 'light upon light' as two distinct lights: the light of intellectual proofs and the light of revealed proofs. What does each light accomplish?
The first distinguishes the eternal from the temporal; the second clarifies divine rulings.
3
Understand the analogy
The scholars compare the intellect to sight and revelation to light. Without light, sight is useless; without sight, light is unseen. How does this illustrate their interdependence?
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider the two scenarios: someone with intellect but no revelation, and someone with revelation but no intellect. The passage says both are deficient. This guards against two extremes: rationalism and fideism.
The common misunderstanding is that reason and revelation are in conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى : ﴿ نُورٌ عَلَى نُورٍ ﴾ تنبيه على ما أشرنا إليهِ مِنْ برهان العقل ودليله ، ونهج الشرع وبيان سبيله ؛ فنورُ أدلَّةِ العقول يتميز بها القديمُ مِنَ الحوادثِ ، ونورُ أدلَّةِ الشرع يتبين بها أحكامُ اللهِ تعالى في عبادِهِ ، ولذلك ضربَ العلماءُ في ذلكَ مثلاً فقالوا : مثلُ العقل كالبصر ، ومثل الشرع كالضوء ؛ فمَنْ وهبه الله تعالى تأييداً في عقلِهِ أمكنه أن يعرف صدْقَ الرسل ، ومَنْ لم يُؤيَّد بذلك كانَ أعمى في ظلمة الجهالة ، ومَنْ كانَ لهُ بصرُ لم يغنِهِ بصرُهُ قبل الرسالة في معرفة ؛ فكان كرجلٍ صحيح البصر في شدَّةِ الظلمة ، فتمنعه الظلمة عن إدراكِ المبصرات.
And His saying, the Exalted: 'Light upon light' is an alert to what we have indicated of the proof of the intellect and its evidence, and the path of revelation and its explanation. For by the light of intellectual proofs the Eternal is distinguished from originated things, and by the light of revealed proofs the judgments of Allah the Exalted concerning His servants are made clear. Therefore the scholars gave an example in this matter and said: The likeness of the intellect is like sight, and the likeness of revelation is like light. So whoever Allah the Exalted grants support in his intellect, it is possible for him to know the truthfulness of the messengers, and whoever is not supported in that is blind in the darkness of ignorance. And whoever has sight is not benefited by his sight before the message in knowledge; he is like a man of sound sight in intense darkness; darkness prevents him from perceiving visible things.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding synthesis on the relationship between reason and revelation. It interprets the Quranic phrase 'light upon light' (نور على نور) as referring to two kinds of light: the light of intellectual proofs and the light of revealed proofs. The first enables one to distinguish the eternal (God) from temporal, created things; the second clarifies divine rulings for His servants. The author then cites an analogy from scholars: reason is like the faculty of sight, and revelation is like physical light. A person with sound intellect can recognize the truthfulness of prophets, but without that intellectual gift, one is like a blind person in darkness. Conversely, having reason alone without revelation is like having good vision but being in utter darkness—the darkness prevents perception. Therefore, both are necessary: reason needs revelation to illuminate its path, and revelation presupposes reason to be recognized. This elegantly affirms the complementary nature of rational and revealed knowledge in Sunni Islam, guarding against both rationalism that rejects revelation and fideism that dismisses reason.

Key Terms
برهان العقل
نهج الشرع
القديم والمحدث
أحكام الله
البصر
الضوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason and revelation are in conflict or one can replace the other.
Correction: The passage shows they are complementary: reason helps us recognize revelation, and revelation guides reason beyond its limits.

Checkpoint
In the analogy provided, what does 'sight' (البصر) represent?
A) Revelation
B) The intellect
C) Faith
D) The prophets

Key Takeaway
Reason and revelation are complementary: reason is like sight, revelation is like light—both are needed for true understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says that without revelation, your reason is like a person with perfect eyesight in pitch darkness. Think of a moral or theological question you have been trying to answer alone through reasoning. How does this verse challenge you to also turn to revelation for illumination? What is one step you will take this week to bring revelation into that question?

The author says that whoever Allah grants support in his intellect can know the truth of messengers. But the passage also says that even sound sight is useless without light. Reflect: Are you using your intellect as a gift from Allah to humbly seek truth from the messengers, or are you treating it as an independent source? What needs to change in your approach to learning your deen?

The Four Darknesses of Ignorance in the Quranic Parable
Sanusi lists the four layers of ignorance from the Quranic parable of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific layers of ignorance that the Quranic parable of darkness represents, and how they form a logical chain in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
The darknesses in the Quran are four distinct types of metaphysical ignorance.

The Quran uses vivid imagery, like darkness in a deep sea, to convey spiritual realities. Sanusi, following the 'people of allusion,' unpacks this verse into four precise ignorances that form a logical progression. This lesson will help you see the hidden structure in the parable and appreciate how kalam thinkers read the Quran philosophically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the words sink in before you try to analyze them.
Notice the Quranic verse is quoted only partially; the focus is on the interpretation.
2
Identify the four layers of ignorance
The passage lists four things people are ignorant of. Find each one: accidents, their origination, dependence of substances, impossibility of beginningless events.
Each begins with 'ignorance of...'
3
Understand the logical chain
These four points are not random; they form a sequence. First you must know that accidents exist, then that they are originated, then that substances need them, and finally that an infinite chain of originated things is impossible. See how each builds on the previous.
This chain is central to kalam proofs for God's existence.
4
Reflect on the author's humility
Sanusi ends by saying Allah knows best the intended order. This is a reminder that even sound interpretations are tentative. Let this attitude shape your study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ كَظُلُمَةٍ فِي بَحْرٍ لُجِّيٍّ ...) [النور : ٤٠] الآيةَ : قال بعض أهل الإشارة : مثل تلك الظلماتِ مِنَ الجهالة : الجهلُ بثبوتِ الأعراضِ ثم الجهل بحدوثها ، ثم الجهلُ بافتقار الجواهر إليها ، ثم الجهلُ باستحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، والله تعالى أعلم بما أراد في ترتيب هذه الظلمات
'Or like darkness in a deep sea…' (An-Nur 40) the verse: Some of the people of allusion said: The likeness of those darknesses from ignorance is: ignorance of the existence of accidents, then ignorance of their origination, then ignorance of the dependence of substances upon them, then ignorance of the impossibility of events with no beginning. And Allah, may He be exalted, knows best what He intended in the ordering of these darknesses.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the Quranic parable of darkness in verse 40 of Surah an-Nur. The 'people of allusion' (ahl al-ishara) explain that the layers of darkness represent sequential ignorance in metaphysical matters. They list four degrees: (1) ignorance that accidents ('arad) exist, (2) ignorance that they are originated (haditha), (3) ignorance that substances (jawhar) depend on accidents, and (4) ignorance that it is impossible for originated things to have no beginning. These four points form a logical chain in Islamic kalam: one must first know that accidents exist, then that they are contingent and begin to exist, then that substances require accidents, and finally that a series of originated things cannot go back infinitely. Each layer of ignorance is a darkness. The author concludes that Allah knows best the intended order of these darknesses, acknowledging the limitation of human understanding.

Key Terms
الظلمات
الجهالة
الأعراض
حدوث
الجواهر
افتقار
استحالة حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the darkness in the verse simply means ignorance of God in general.
Correction: The passage specifies four precise metaphysical ignorances, each a distinct layer of darkness, not just general ignorance.

Checkpoint
How many layers of ignorance are mentioned in the interpretation of the darkness verse?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The Quranic darkness parable teaches that ignorance has layers, each blocking deeper understanding of God's creative action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

You believe in a Creator, but have you ever truly pondered the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes? How does that realization make you feel about your own dependency on Him at this very moment?
?
The first darkness is ignorance that accidents even exist. Right now, can you name three accidents that are sustaining your own life? If you cannot, what does that reveal about the depth of your awareness of Allah's constant action?
