The First Response to the Objection on Tark
Sanusi presents an objection and its first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the objection that tark cannot be an object of power, which argues that tark is an existential act of restraint.

Core Idea
Tark is not mere non-existence; it is an existential restraint.

The objection claims that if God is powerful, He must be able to refrain, but refraining is pure non-existence and thus impossible as an effect of power. The first answer challenges this by redefining tark as a positive act of holding back, which is existential and therefore a valid object of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the opening objection carefully. Note how it argues that tark cannot be maqdur because it is non-existence and power requires an existential effect.
The objection uses two sub-arguments: eternity of the world and obtaining the obtained.
2
Identify the First Answer
Find the first answer starting with 'الأوّل'. It rejects the premise that tark is pure negation.
The answer defines tark as kaff and imsak, which are existential.
3
Understand the Response to the Consequences
The answer then refutes the objections about eternity and obtaining the obtained by noting that tark only occurs in time.
This shows the objection's flaws without requiring a redefinition of power.
4
Note the Jurist Comment
Notice that the first answer defends the statement 'tark is an act' made by some jurists, warning against hasty accusations of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كانَ المؤثر في العالم قادراً لكان قادراً على الفعل والتركِ؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي إنْ شاءَ فعلَ، وإِنْ شاءَ تركَ، لكنَّ الترك يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ مقدوراً؛ لأنَّهُ نفي وعدمٌ صِرْفٌ، والقدرة لا بدَّ لها مِنْ أثرٍ، والعدمُ ليسَ أثراً، وإلا (۱) لزم قدم العالم، وأيضاً: فالتركُ عدم مستمر، فلو كان أثراً لزم تحصيل الحاصل فالجواب من وجهين: الأوّلُ: لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الترك ليسَ مقدوراً للقادرِ. قولُكم: (التركُ: نفى محض، وعدم مستمر)، قلنا: ممنوع؛ فإن الترك هو الكفُّ والإمساك عن الفعل، وهو أمرٌ وجودي (٢) وقولكم: (يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم، وتحصيل الحاصل) قلنا: ممنوع؛ لأنَّ الفعلَ مِنْ حيث هو فعل ينافي الأزل، وإنَّما تتأتّى حقيقته فيما لا يزالُ، فلا تركَ إذاً في الأزلِ، وإِنَّما يتأتى فيما لا يزالُ، وإذا كانَ التركُ مُتجدّداً بعدَ أنْ لم يكن بطل ما قلتُموهُ: إِنَّهُ تحصيل الحاصل. ومن هنا تعرفُ: أنَّ قولَ مَنْ قالَ مِنَ الفقهاء: (إِنَّ التركَ فعل) لا يلزم عليه محذورٌ كما ظنَّهُ بعضُ القاصرينَ (١)، بل وصرَّحَ بعضُ مَنْ لا يحجزه الحياء ولا الدين منهم بتكفير قائله! الثاني وهو الحق: وهو أن نقول: ما ذكرتُم إنَّما يدلُّ أنَّ المؤثر ليس فاعلاً للتركِ ولا مُوجِداً له، ولا يلزم منه نفي أن يكون قادراً عليه؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي يصحُ أنْ يفعل الشيء وألا يفعله، والمراد بقولنا: (وألا يفعلَهُ) ألا يُخرِجَ الفعلَ إلى الوجودِ، بل يبقيه على العدم، لا أنَّهُ يُوجِدُ العدمَ ويفعلُ التركَ، وإذا كان كذلك فلا استبعاد في إسناده إلى الفاعل المختارِ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِهِ مقدوراً للفاعل أنْ يكون أثراً وجودياً
If it is said: If the effecter in the world were powerful (qādiran), he would be able to do and to refrain (tark), because the powerful (al-qādir) is he who, if he wills, does, and if he wills, refrains. But refraining is impossible to be an object of power (maqdūran), because it is negation and pure non-existence (ʿadam), and power must have an effect (athar), and non-existence is not an effect; otherwise, the eternity of the world would follow. Also, refraining is continuous non-existence, so if it were an effect, obtaining the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) would follow. The answer is from two aspects: The first: We do not concede that refraining is not an object of power for the powerful. Your statement: 'Refraining is pure negation and continuous non-existence'—we say: it is prevented; for refraining is the restraint (kaff) and holding back (imsāk) from the act, and it is an existential matter (amr wujūdī). And your statement: 'The eternity of the world and obtaining the obtained would follow'—we say: it is prevented; because the act as such is incompatible with eternity, and its reality only occurs in what is not yet (mā lā yazālu), so there is no refraining in eternity; it only occurs in what is not yet. And if refraining is renewed after not being, what you said—that it is obtaining the obtained—is invalidated. From this you know that the statement of those among the jurists who said 'refraining is an act' does not entail a problem as some deficient ones thought; rather, some who are not restrained by modesty or religion even declared its speaker an unbeliever! The second, which is the truth, is that we say: What you mentioned only indicates that the effecter is not an agent (fāʿil) of refraining nor a bringer into existence (mūjid) of it, but it does not necessitate denying that he is powerful over it; because the powerful is he who can validly do the thing and not do it. And by our statement 'and not do it' is meant not to bring the act into existence, but to leave it in non-existence, not that he brings non-existence into existence and does refraining. If this is so, there is no absurdity in attributing it to the free agent, and his being powerful over it does not require that it be an existential effect.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection (qīla) against the claim that the agent (muʾaththir) of the world is qādir (powerful). The objection argues: if he is qādir, he must be able to do an act and to refrain from it (tark). But tark is pure negation and non-existence (ʿadam), and power (qudrah) must have an effect (athar). Non-existence cannot be an effect; otherwise, the world would be eternal (since eternal non-existence would be an effect) and it would be obtaining the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). So tark cannot be maqdūr (an object of power). The response (jawāb) is given in two aspects. The first answer denies the premise that tark is not maqdūr: tark is not mere negation; it is kaff (restraint) and imsāk (holding back), which are existential (wujūdī) as experienced by the agent. It also rejects the further problems: the act (fiʿl) as such is incompatible with eternity (azal), so tark only occurs in time (mā lā yazālu) after non-existence, so there is no eternal tark or obtaining the obtained. The first answer also defends the statement of some jurists that 'tark is an act' (fiʿl), arguing it does not lead to a problem. The second answer (which is the truth) redefines the meaning of qādir: it is one who can validly do the thing and not do it. 'Not doing' means not bringing the act into existence, not creating non-existence. Thus the objection only proves that the agent does not create tark, not that he is not qādir over it. This preserves God's power without requiring tark to be an existential effect.

Key Terms
قادر
ترك
قدرة
أثر
نفي
عدم
وجودي
كف
إمساك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tark is purely negative and cannot be an object of power because it is non-existence.
Correction: The first answer shows that tark is not pure non-existence; it is an existential act of restraint (kaff and imsāk), and thus can be an effect of power.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what is tark?
A) Pure non-existence
B) An existential act of restraint
C) The creation of non-existence
D) The absence of power

Key Takeaway
The first answer refutes the objection by redefining tark as an existential restraint, not pure non-existence, thus preserving its status as a valid object of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose to refrain from a sin, do you treat that restraint as a positive act of obedience (an existential 'kaff') or as merely the absence of sin? How does the lesson's redefinition of tark challenge you to see your own refraining as a real, worshipful act?
?
The objection argued that tark cannot be an effect of God's power because it is non-existence. Yet you often treat your own procrastination or neglect as 'nothing'. How would your daily accountability change if you recognized that your omissions are also real actions with weight before Allah?
?
The first answer defends the jurists' statement that 'tark is an act'. If you truly believed that every moment of refraining from evil is a positive act, which neglected areas of your life would you suddenly feel compelled to attend to?

The Second Response: Redefining Power Over Tark
Sanusi presents the second and correct answer to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and correct answer to the objection, which clarifies that being powerful over tark does not require tark to be an existential effect.

Core Idea
Being powerful over an act includes the ability to not do it, without creating non-existence.

The first answer focused on redefining tark. The second answer—presented as the truth—takes a different approach: it redefines what it means for God to be qādir over tark. It argues that being 'powerful over tark' does not mean creating tark, but simply having the ability to leave the act undone.

Guided Path
1
Read the Second Answer
Read from 'الثاني وهو الحق' to the end. Note how it distinguishes between being an agent (fāʿil) of tark and being powerful (qādir) over it.
2
Understand the Definition of Qādir
The answer redefines qādir: one who can validly do a thing and not do it. 'Not doing' means not bringing the act into existence, not creating non-existence.
This avoids the problem of non-existence as an effect.
3
See the Conclusion
The answer concludes that there is no absurdity in attributing power over tark to God, and that being maqdūr does not require being an existential effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو كانَ المؤثر في العالم قادراً لكان قادراً على الفعل والتركِ؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي إنْ شاءَ فعلَ، وإِنْ شاءَ تركَ، لكنَّ الترك يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ مقدوراً؛ لأنَّهُ نفي وعدمٌ صِرْفٌ، والقدرة لا بدَّ لها مِنْ أثرٍ، والعدمُ ليسَ أثراً، وإلا (۱) لزم قدم العالم، وأيضاً: فالتركُ عدم مستمر، فلو كان أثراً لزم تحصيل الحاصل فالجواب من وجهين: الأوّلُ: لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الترك ليسَ مقدوراً للقادرِ. قولُكم: (التركُ: نفى محض، وعدم مستمر)، قلنا: ممنوع؛ فإن الترك هو الكفُّ والإمساك عن الفعل، وهو أمرٌ وجودي (٢) وقولكم: (يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم، وتحصيل الحاصل) قلنا: ممنوع؛ لأنَّ الفعلَ مِنْ حيث هو فعل ينافي الأزل، وإنَّما تتأتّى حقيقته فيما لا يزالُ، فلا تركَ إذاً في الأزلِ، وإِنَّما يتأتى فيما لا يزالُ، وإذا كانَ التركُ مُتجدّداً بعدَ أنْ لم يكن بطل ما قلتُموهُ: إِنَّهُ تحصيل الحاصل. ومن هنا تعرفُ: أنَّ قولَ مَنْ قالَ مِنَ الفقهاء: (إِنَّ التركَ فعل) لا يلزم عليه محذورٌ كما ظنَّهُ بعضُ القاصرينَ (١)، بل وصرَّحَ بعضُ مَنْ لا يحجزه الحياء ولا الدين منهم بتكفير قائله! الثاني وهو الحق: وهو أن نقول: ما ذكرتُم إنَّما يدلُّ أنَّ المؤثر ليس فاعلاً للتركِ ولا مُوجِداً له، ولا يلزم منه نفي أن يكون قادراً عليه؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي يصحُ أنْ يفعل الشيء وألا يفعله، والمراد بقولنا: (وألا يفعلَهُ) ألا يُخرِجَ الفعلَ إلى الوجودِ، بل يبقيه على العدم، لا أنَّهُ يُوجِدُ العدمَ ويفعلُ التركَ، وإذا كان كذلك فلا استبعاد في إسناده إلى الفاعل المختارِ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِهِ مقدوراً للفاعل أنْ يكون أثراً وجودياً
If it is said: If the effecter in the world were powerful (qādiran), he would be able to do and to refrain (tark), because the powerful (al-qādir) is he who, if he wills, does, and if he wills, refrains. But refraining is impossible to be an object of power (maqdūran), because it is negation and pure non-existence (ʿadam), and power must have an effect (athar), and non-existence is not an effect; otherwise, the eternity of the world would follow. Also, refraining is continuous non-existence, so if it were an effect, obtaining the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) would follow. The answer is from two aspects: The first: We do not concede that refraining is not an object of power for the powerful. Your statement: 'Refraining is pure negation and continuous non-existence'—we say: it is prevented; for refraining is the restraint (kaff) and holding back (imsāk) from the act, and it is an existential matter (amr wujūdī). And your statement: 'The eternity of the world and obtaining the obtained would follow'—we say: it is prevented; because the act as such is incompatible with eternity, and its reality only occurs in what is not yet (mā lā yazālu), so there is no refraining in eternity; it only occurs in what is not yet. And if refraining is renewed after not being, what you said—that it is obtaining the obtained—is invalidated. From this you know that the statement of those among the jurists who said 'refraining is an act' does not entail a problem as some deficient ones thought; rather, some who are not restrained by modesty or religion even declared its speaker an unbeliever! The second, which is the truth, is that we say: What you mentioned only indicates that the effecter is not an agent (fāʿil) of refraining nor a bringer into existence (mūjid) of it, but it does not necessitate denying that he is powerful over it; because the powerful is he who can validly do the thing and not do it. And by our statement 'and not do it' is meant not to bring the act into existence, but to leave it in non-existence, not that he brings non-existence into existence and does refraining. If this is so, there is no absurdity in attributing it to the free agent, and his being powerful over it does not require that it be an existential effect.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection (qīla) against the claim that the agent (muʾaththir) of the world is qādir (powerful). The objection argues: if he is qādir, he must be able to do an act and to refrain from it (tark). But tark is pure negation and non-existence (ʿadam), and power (qudrah) must have an effect (athar). Non-existence cannot be an effect; otherwise, the world would be eternal (since eternal non-existence would be an effect) and it would be obtaining the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). So tark cannot be maqdūr (an object of power). The response (jawāb) is given in two aspects. The first answer denies the premise that tark is not maqdūr: tark is not mere negation; it is kaff (restraint) and imsāk (holding back), which are existential (wujūdī) as experienced by the agent. It also rejects the further problems: the act (fiʿl) as such is incompatible with eternity (azal), so tark only occurs in time (mā lā yazālu) after non-existence, so there is no eternal tark or obtaining the obtained. The first answer also defends the statement of some jurists that 'tark is an act' (fiʿl), arguing it does not lead to a problem. The second answer (which is the truth) redefines the meaning of qādir: it is one who can validly do the thing and not do it. 'Not doing' means not bringing the act into existence, not creating non-existence. Thus the objection only proves that the agent does not create tark, not that he is not qādir over it. This preserves God's power without requiring tark to be an existential effect.

ترك
مقدور
فاعل
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is powerful over tark, then tark must be an existential creation.
Correction: The second answer clarifies that being powerful over tark only means having the ability not to bring the act into existence, not creating a separate entity called tark.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer, what does it mean for God to be powerful over tark?
A) He creates an act of refraining.
B) He has the ability to not bring the act into existence.
C) He creates non-existence.
D) He lacks the ability to do the act.

Key Takeaway
The second answer shows that being powerful over tark means having the ability to leave an act undone, without requiring tark to be an existential effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is Qādir over everything. But reflect: does your heart truly accept that His non-action—when He delays an answer or withholds a blessing—is also an expression of His perfect power? How can you train yourself to see His tark not as weakness but as deliberate, wise choice?
?
If God's power over refraining does not require creating 'non-existence', then His non-intervention is not a void but a purposeful leaving. When you face silence from God, will you interpret it as absence or as a planned restraint meant to draw you closer? What will you do differently this week to embrace His tark as an act of love?

Refutation of the Objection on Ability to Leave Undone
Sanusi refutes the objection against the second position that God is able to leave an act undone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's ability to leave an act undone does not entail creating non-existence, and why this position is not subject to the objection raised against it.

Core Idea
Being able to leave an act undone means not bringing it into existence, not creating non-existence.

Sanusi defends the truth of the second position, which holds that God is capable of leaving an act undone. Some object that this would require God to create non-existence, which is absurd. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the meaning of 'leaving undone' and shows that the objection rests on a misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Note how the passage begins by acknowledging an objection, then states the true position, and then explains why the objection fails.
The key is the definition of 'capable'.
3
Understand the definition of 'capable'
Focus on the definition of القادر: one who can do the thing or not do it. The clarification about 'not doing' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the capacity to leave undone does not require creating non-existence. This clears the objection.
Connect to the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يلزم عليه محذورٌ كما ظنَّهُ بعضُ القاصرينَ، بل وصرَّحَ بعضُ مَنْ لا يحجزه الحياء ولا الدين منهم بتكفير قائله ! الثاني وهو الحق : وهو أن نقول : ما ذكرتُم إنَّما يدلُّ أنَّ المؤثر ليس فاعلاً للتركِ ولا مُوجِداً له، ولا يلزم منه نفي أن يكون قادراً عليهِ؛ لأنَّ القادر هو الذي يصحُ أنْ يفعل الشيء وألا يفعله، والمراد بقولنا : ( وألا يفعلَهُ ( ألا يُخرِجَ الفعلَ إلى الوجودِ، بل يبقيه على العدم، لا أنَّهُ يُوجِدُ العدمَ ويفعلُ التركَ، وإذا كان كذلك فلا استبعاد في إسناده إلى الفاعل المختارِ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِهِ مقدوراً للفاعل أنْ يكون أثراً وجودياً
No impermissible consequence follows, as some of the deficient ones imagined. Rather, some of those whom modesty and religion do not restrain among them even declared the one who says it an unbeliever! The second [position], which is the truth: it is that we say: What you have mentioned only indicates that the effective agent is not a doer of abandonment nor a creator of it, and it does not necessitate the denial that He is capable of it; because the capable is one whom it is valid to do the thing and not to do it. And what we mean by our saying 'and not to do it' is that He does not bring the act into existence, but rather leaves it upon non-existence, not that He creates non-existence and does abandonment. And when it is such, then there is no improbability in attributing it to the free agent, and it does not necessarily follow from its being possible for the agent that it be an existent effect.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer defending the truth of the second position regarding God's ability. It begins by noting that some deficient people think the second position leads to an impermissible consequence, and some even go so far as to declare its proponent an unbeliever. The truth (الثاني وهو الحق) is then stated: the argument that the effective agent is not a doer of abandonment does not negate that He is capable of it. The definition of 'capable' (القادر) is given: one who can do a thing and can also leave it undone. The key clarification is that 'leaving it undone' means not bringing the act into existence, i.e., leaving it in non-existence, not creating non-existence itself. Thus, there is no absurdity in attributing the capacity to leave undone to the free agent (الفاعل المختار). The passage concludes that being possible (مقدور) for the agent does not require it to be an existent effect.

Misconception: Some think that 'being able to not act' means God creates non-existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies it simply means not bringing the act into existence, i.e., leaving it in its original non-existence, not creating non-existence as an act.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'capable' (al-qādir) given in this passage?
A) One who can do something and also create its opposite.
B) One who can do an act and also leave it undone.
C) One who brings all things into existence.
D) One who is not affected by anything.

Key Takeaway
God's ability to leave an act undone simply means He does not bring it into existence, not that He creates non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that God's ability to leave undone does not mean He creates non-existence. When you think about your own moments of spiritual laziness—prayers missed, charity not given—do you treat your inaction as if you have created a void that can only be filled by punishment? How can you instead trust that God's mercy fills every void, and that your next act, not regret for the 'non-act', is what He holds you accountable for?

Refutation of the Objection on Specification of Divine Power
Sanusi refutes the objection on specification of divine power through a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that divine power is specified to some possibles by showing the absurd consequences of both having a specifier and not having one.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be specified arbitrarily; any specification leads to contradiction.

Sanusi now addresses an objection: why does divine power attach to some possible things but not others? The objector suggests this implies a limitation. Sanusi dismantles this by presenting a dilemma: either there is a specifier or not. Both options lead to impossible consequences, thereby reaffirming that God's power is absolute and eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector questions why divine power is specified to some possibles. Recognize that this objection targets the attribute of power (qudra).
2
First horn of the dilemma
If there is a specifier, then power would be temporal. Read the explanation: the specifier would need to create both power and incapability, contradicting God's eternal power.
3
Second horn of the dilemma
If no specifier, the unsuited possible becomes impossible. Follow the logic: only necessary and impossible are unsuited; since it's possible, it can't be necessary, so it becomes impossible.
4
Consequences of the second horn
If that possible becomes impossible, then by similarity all possibles become impossible, meaning God cannot create anything—contradicting reason and observation.
5
Conclusion
The objection is refuted. Divine power remains eternal and unrestricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لتعلُّقِ القدرة ، فاختصاص بعضها بصلاحيته لتعلق القدرة دونَ الآخر : إنْ كانَ لمُخصِّص : لزمَ حدوث القدرةِ ؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يُحتاجُ حينئذ إلى أنْ يكونَ الفاعل المخصّص خلق قدرةً تتعلق ببعض الممكنات ، وعجزاً يتعلَّق بالبعض الآخرِ ، وقد عرفت برهان وجوبِ القدم لقدرته تعالى وإنْ كانَ تخصيص تعلُّقها بالبعض لا لمُخصّص : لزمَ أَنَّ البعض الآخر لا يصلح لذاتِهِ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلَّقاً لها ؛ لما فُرِضَ مِنْ عدمِ المخصص ، وما لا يصلحُ لذاتِهِ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلقاً للقدرة ينحصر في الواجب والمستحيل ، وكونُ هذا البعض الممكن واجباً لا يصح ؛ لأنَّه معدوم لم يُوجَدْ ، ولا شيء مِنَ الواجب بمعدوم ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مستحيلاً ، والفَرْضُ أنَّه ممكن مماثل لسائر الممكنات التي تعلقت بها القدرة ، فقد لزم انقلابُ الممكن مستحيلاً ، وإذا انقلب هذا الممكنُ مستحيلاً لزم انقلاب سائرِ الممكناتِ مستحيلاً ؛ للتماثل ، فلا يَقدِرُ الإله على شيء منها ، ويلزمُ ألا يُوجَدَ شيء منها ، والعقلُ والعِيانُ يُكذِّبُ ذلك ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
Concerning the attachment of power, the specification of some [possibles] by their suitability for the attachment of power without the other: if it is due to a specifier, then the temporality of power follows, because then the specifying agent would need to create a power that attaches to some possibles and an inability that attaches to the others, and you have already known the proof of the necessity of eternity for His power, exalted is He. And if the specification of its attachment to some [possibles] is without a specifier, then it follows that the other [possible] is not in itself suitable to be an object of it, because of the assumed absence of a specifier. And that which is not in itself suitable to be an object of power is restricted to the necessary and the impossible. And that this particular possible be necessary is not valid, because it is non-existent, not brought into existence, and nothing necessary is non-existent. So it is determined that it is impossible, while the assumption is that it is a possible similar to the other possibles to which power attaches. Thus the conversion of the possible into impossible follows, and if this possible becomes impossible, then the conversion of all possibles into impossible follows, due to similarity. So the deity has no power over anything from them, and it follows that nothing from them exists. And reason and observation refute that. And with God Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the divine attribute of power (qudra). The objector questions why power attaches to some possible things and not others. Sanusi presents a dilemma: either there is a specifier (mukhassis) for this specification, or not. If there is a specifier, then power would be temporal (hadith) because the specifier would need to create power for some possibles and incapability ('ajz) for others, contradicting the proof that God's power is eternal (qadim). If there is no specifier, then the possible that is not an object of power must be inherently unsuitable for power. But only the necessary (wajib) and the impossible (mustahil) are inherently unsuitable. The possible cannot be necessary because it is non-existent, so it must be impossible. However, the assumption is that it is possible and similar to other possibles. This leads to all possibles becoming impossible, meaning God cannot create anything, which contradicts reason and observed existence. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
الممكن (al-mumkin)
المخصص (al-mukhassis)
الواجب (al-wajib)
المستحيل (al-mustahil)
التماثل (al-tamathul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the specification of power to some possibles requires a specifier distinct from God, but Sanusi shows that any such specifier would entail temporal power in God.
Correction: Actually, any specification either undermines God's eternity or leads to all possibles being impossible, so the objection is invalid.

Checkpoint
What follows if the specification of divine power to some possibles is without a specifier?
A) The other possible becomes necessary
B) The other possible becomes impossible
C) Power becomes temporal
D) All possibles remain possible

Key Takeaway
Divine power cannot be specified to some possibles without contradiction; thus God's power is absolute and eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power is unlimited, or do you sometimes act as if some things are impossible for Him? In what area of your life have you limited God's power by your own reasoning?
?
This argument shows that any limitation on God's power leads to contradiction. What contradiction exists in your own heart when you doubt God's ability to change your situation?
?
If God's power were limited to some possibles, He would be dependent on a specifier. Are you living as if God is dependent on your efforts? Repent of relying on your own power and trust in His absolute power today.

Refutation of Rational Good and Evil (al-Tahsin wa al-Taqbih)
Sanusi defines good and evil as purely legal terms and rejects Mu'tazilite rationalism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of good and evil as purely legal terms and why the Mu'tazilite claim of rational morality is rejected.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by divine command, not by reason.

In this passage, Sanusi lays out the Ash'ari position on moral values, known as al-tahsin wa al-taqbih. He contrasts it directly with the Mu'tazilite view that reason alone can determine good and evil. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the broader Islamic theological debate on the role of reason versus revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi uses a very precise legal language.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find the sentences where Sanusi defines 'good' and 'evil'. Notice that he ties them exclusively to what the Law commands.
The defining phrase is 'ليس الحَسَنُ شرعاً عندَ أهلِ الحقِّ إلا ما قالَ الشرع فيه : افعلوه'.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi dismisses the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can know God's rulings. He asserts that his earlier proofs have already demolished their foundations.
He does not engage in detailed refutation here, saying it is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا تعرف فساد ما تزعمُهُ المعتزلةُ مِنْ أنَّ العقل وحدَهُ قد يتوصَّلُ إلى معرفة أحكامِ اللهِ تعالى بغيرِ واسطةِ الرُّسُلِ عليهم السلام ، وهذهِ المسألةُ هي المعبَّرُ عنها بالتحسينِ والتقبيح أو الحَسَنِ والقبيحِ ، فليس الحَسَنُ شرعاً عندَ أهلِ الحقِّ إلا ما قالَ الشرع فيه : افعلوه ، وليس القبيحُ شرعاً إلا ما قالَ فيهِ : لا تفعلوه ، وتخصيص كل واحد منهما بما اختص بهِ مِنَ الأفعال لا علَّةَ لَهُ ومسألة الحسنِ والقبيح يطولُ الكلام فيها ، باعتبار نقل مذاهبِ المعتزلة فيها ، والرد على كل مذهب بما يخصه تحقيقاً وجدلاً ، وقد هدمنا بما أشرنا إليه هنا وفي أصلِ العقيدةِ مِنَ البرهان القطعي .. أصول مذاهبهم ، ولا حاجة في هذا المختصر إلى تتبع فروعها التي اجتُبَّتْ مِنْ أصلها ، ومَنْ أرادَ بعض التطويل في ذلك فعليه بـ « شرح العقيدة الكبرى
And by this you know the invalidity of what the Mu'tazila claim, that the intellect alone may arrive at knowledge of God's rulings without the intermediary of the messengers, peace be upon them. This issue is what is referred to as al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ (declaring things good or evil) or al-ḥasan wa al-qabīḥ (the good and the evil). For, according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq, the good in the Law is nothing other than what the Law said: 'Do it', and the evil in the Law is nothing other than what it said: 'Do not do it'. And the specification of each one of them with the actions it is specified with has no cause. And the issue of al-ḥasan wa al-qabīḥ would be lengthy to discuss, considering the transmission of the Mu'tazilite schools on it and the refutation of each school with what is specific to it, both in verification and dialectic. And we have demolished, by what we indicated here and in the original creed (Aṣl al-ʻAqīda) of the decisive proof, the foundations of their schools. And there is no need in this abridgement to follow their branches which have been uprooted from their root. And whoever wants some lengthiness on that should refer to 'Sharḥ al-ʻAqīda al-Kubrā'.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on moral values (al-ḥasan wa al-qabīḥ) and refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of rational good and evil (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-ʻaqliyyān). Sanusi begins by stating that from the preceding refutation of causality, one can recognize the falsity of the Mu'tazilite claim that reason alone can know God's rulings without prophets. He then identifies that issue as the one called 'declaring things good or evil' or 'the good and the evil'. The Ash'ari definition follows: Good (al-ḥasan) in the Revealed Law (sharʻan) is what the Law says 'Do it'; Evil (al-qabīḥ) is what it says 'Do not do it'. There is no cause (lā ʻillata lahu) for why certain acts are assigned to one category or the other – it is purely divine command. Sanusi then notes that the topic is lengthy, involving many Mu'tazilite subschools and their refutations. He asserts that his earlier arguments in this text and in his 'Aṣl al-ʻAqīda' (a longer work) have already demolished the foundations of their doctrines. In this abridgement, he does not need to pursue the branches (subsidiary issues) because they are uprooted from their origin. He directs the reader who wants a fuller treatment to his 'Sharḥ al-ʻAqīda al-Kubrā'.

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح
الحسن والقبيح
أهل الحق
العقل
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris deny any rational basis for morality; they affirm that reason can know the existence of God and some duties indirectly, but not specific moral values without revelation.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that moral value is determined solely by divine command, but reason can still be used to understand revelation and infer some rulings.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq in this passage, what is the good in the Law?
A) Whatever reason deems good.
B) Whatever brings benefit.
C) Whatever the Law says 'Do it'.
D) Whatever is intrinsically good.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that moral value is determined solely by divine command, with no rational basis independent of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your strongest moral intuition conflicts with a clear command from Allah, which do you actually follow? Have you ever justified disobedience by saying 'this makes sense to me'? What does this lesson demand of you in that moment?
?
This teaching says that good and evil have no cause other than God's command. Does that unsettle you? Do you feel a pull to find a reason behind every rule? Where would that pull lead you if left unchecked?
?
Think of an action your society calls 'good' but Allah has forbidden. Are you ready to call it evil solely because He said 'do not do it'? Is there any area where you still rely on your own judgment more than His command? What will you do about it starting today?

Refutation of Rational Tahseen and Taqbeeh
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite rational ethics and defines good and evil by Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that good and evil are determined solely by divine command, not by reason.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by Sharia, not by human reason.

The Mu'tazilites held that reason alone can discern moral values, even without revelation. Sanusi refutes this, asserting that only divine command defines good and evil. This passage establishes the Ash'ari position on moral epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong claim of corruption at the beginning.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find where Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite claim and where he states the Sunni definition. Contrast them.
The key definitions are: 'ليس الحسن شرعاً عند أهل الحق إلا ما قال الشرع فيه: افعلوه'.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: If good and evil are not known by reason, what does that imply about the role of revelation and prophets?
This connects to the necessity of prophethood and divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فساد قول المعتزلة بالتحسين والتقبيح العقليين. وبهذا تعرف فساد ما تزعمه المعتزلة من أن العقل وحده قد يتوصل إلى معرفة أحكام الله تعالى بغير واسطة الرسل عليهم السلام، وهذه المسألة هي المعبر عنها بالتحسين والتقبيح أو الحسن والقبيح، فليس الحسن شرعاً عند أهل الحق إلا ما قال الشرع فيه: افعلوه، وليس القبيح شرعاً إلا ما قال فيه: لا تفعلوه، وتخصيص كل واحد منهما بما اختص به من الأفعال لا علة له. ومسألة الحسن والقبيح يطول الكلام فيها، باعتبار نقل مذاهب المعتزلة فيها، والرد على كل مذهب بما يخصه تحقيقاً وجدلاً، وقد هدمنا بما أشرنا إليه هنا وفي أصل العقيدة من البرهان القطعي .. أصول مذاهبهم، ولا حاجة في هذا المختصر إلى تتبع فروعها التي اجتثت من أصلها، ومن أراد بعض التطويل في ذلك فعليه بـ « شرح العقيدة الكبرى
The corruption of the Mu'tazilite claim of rational tahseen and taqbeeh. And by this you know the corruption of what the Mu'tazila claim that reason alone can arrive at knowledge of Allah's rulings without the intermediary of the messengers. This issue is what is called tahseen and taqbeeh or hasan and qabeeh. According to the people of truth, what is good (hasan) in the Sharia is only that which the Sharia said: do it; and what is bad (qabeeh) in the Sharia is only that which it said: do not do it. And the specification of each one of them to the acts to which it is specific has no cause. The issue of hasan and qabeeh becomes lengthy, considering the transmission of the schools of the Mu'tazila and the refutation of each school according to its specifics in verification and dialectic. And we have demolished by what we have indicated here and in the original creed of definitive proof the foundations of their schools. And there is no need in this summary to follow their branches which have been uprooted from their root. And whoever wants some length in that, let him refer to 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra'.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that reason alone can determine moral value (tahseen wa taqbeeh aqliyyan). The author defines the Sunni position: good and evil are purely legislative—whatever the Sharia commands is good, whatever it forbids is evil. There is no inherent rational cause for moral value. The author notes that a full treatment is lengthy and refers to his larger work for details.

Misconception: Some think that reason alone can judge an act as good or bad independently of revelation.
Correction: This passage insists that good and bad are purely legislative: an act is good only because Sharia commands it, and bad only because Sharia forbids it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what defines an act as 'good' (hasan) in Sharia?
A) Reason judges it as good.
B) It brings benefit.
C) The Sharia commands it.
D) It is inherently virtuous.

Key Takeaway
For the people of truth, good and evil are purely legislative concepts defined by Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If good and evil are defined solely by Sharia, then your personal culture, upbringing, or society do not define morality. How does that challenge your comfort zone? What change in your daily choices does this reality demand from you starting now?

Refuting the Quranic Proof for Rational Ethics
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite use of Quran 16:90 for rational ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite use of Quran 16:90 to support rational good and evil by showing that the verbs 'commands' and 'forbids' establish the ruling rather than presuppose inherent moral values.

Core Idea
The verse does not prove inherent good and evil; it proves the opposite.

The Mu'tazila point to Quran 16:90, where Allah commands justice and forbids evil, as evidence that actions have inherent moral value known by reason. Sanusi argues that the wording of the verse actually undermines their claim. By focusing on the verbs 'commands' and 'forbids', he shows that the ruling is established by the command itself, not by any pre-existing rational judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilite argument is presented and then refuted.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazila argue that the verse 'commands justice and forbids evil' shows that justice and evil are known rationally before the command. Find this in the passage.
They think the verse confirms pre-existing rational values.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi points out that the words 'commands' and 'forbids' establish the ruling for the first time, not merely confirm something already known. See how he uses the logic of the verse itself.
The verse actually demonstrates that moral value comes from the command.
4
Distinguish from customary judgments
Sanusi adds that this is different from customary moral judgments (tahseen wa taqbeeh 'urfiyyain). Understand this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهة المعتزلة النصية في الحسن والقبح العقليين: نحو قوله سبحانه وتعالى عن سوء فهمهم: { إن الله يأمر بالعدل والإحسان } فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل الأمر، { وينهى عن الفحشاء والمنكر والبغي } فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل النهي. ونحو حديث سيدنا معاذ رضي الله عنه الذي رواه مسلم: أيأتي أحدنا شهوته ويكون له فيها أجر؟ قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أرأيتم لو وضعها في حرام أكان عليه فيها وزر؟ فكذلك إذا وضعها في الحلال كان له أجر». وإنما الآية حجة عليهم؛ إذ كلمتا (يأمر) و(ينهى) دلتا على تأسيس الحكم، وكأن العدل والإحسان والفحشاء والمنكر والبغي في رتبة واحدة قبل الأمر والنهي، ثم تفرقتا بهما، وهذا غير التحسين والتقبيح العرفيين. وأما الحديث فهو من باب قياس العكس.
And the textual doubt of the Mu'tazila concerning rational hasan and qabeeh: like His statement - glorified and exalted - about their misunderstanding: { Indeed Allah commands justice and excellence } so they claim He affirmed them before the command, and { Forbids indecency, evil, and transgression } so they claim He affirmed them before the prohibition. And like the hadith of our master Mu'adh (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated by Muslim: 'Does one of us pursue his desire and have reward in it?' He said: 'Do you see if he placed it in the unlawful, would he have sin? So similarly if he places it in the lawful, he has reward.' And the verse is a proof against them, because the two terms 'commands' and 'forbids' indicate the establishment of the ruling, and it is as if justice, excellence, indecency, evil, and transgression were in one rank before the command and prohibition, then they became distinct by them. And this is other than customary tahseen and taqbeeh. As for the hadith, it is from the chapter of analogia a contrario (qiyas al-'aks).
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage refutes two scriptural proofs used by the Mu'tazila to support rational good and evil. First, the verse (Q16:90) that says Allah commands justice and forbids evil: the Mu'tazila claim these actions are inherently good/evil, but the author argues that the words 'commands' and 'forbids' actually establish the ruling; the actions are neutral before revelation. Second, the hadith about pursuing desire: the same act (sexual desire) can be either rewardable (in lawful marriage) or sinful (in adultery), showing that moral value depends on divine ruling, not inherent nature. The hadith is classified as qiyas al-'aks (analogy of the opposite). The author concludes this is different from customary moral judgments (al-tahseen wa al-taqbeeh al-'urfiyyain).

Key Terms
شبهة نصية
تحسين وتقبيح عقلي
تحسين وتقبيح عرفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah commands justice and forbids evil' proves that justice and evil are inherently good and evil, known by reason before revelation.
Correction: The verbs 'commands' and 'forbids' show that the ruling is established by the command itself; the actions were neutral before revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what do the words 'commands' and 'forbids' in Quran 16:90 indicate?
A) That actions have inherent rational goodness or evil.
B) That the ruling is established for the first time by the command.
C) That justice and evil are known by social custom.
D) That the verse confirms the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah commands justice and forbids evil' actually proves that moral value is established by the divine command, not by rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel certain that an action is good or evil without consulting divine revelation, where does that certainty come from? This lesson challenges the assumption that such moral knowledge is independent of Allah's command. Are you willing to submit your moral intuitions to the authority of revelation, even when they conflict?
?
Consider a time you relied on your own reasoning to decide what was right. How does the idea that moral values are neutral before divine command affect your trust in your own judgment? Can you recall an instance where you had to set aside your rational preference to follow a religious ruling?
?
The verse commands justice and forbids evil. Reflect deeply: do you obey these commands because you rationally perceive their goodness, or because Allah has commanded them? Which motive yields greater sincerity and submission? This lesson demands you examine the foundation of your obedience.

Refuting the Hadith Proof for Rational Ethics
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite use of a hadith from Mu'adh for rational ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite use of the hadith about sexual desire by showing that the same act can be rewardable or punishable based on legal permission, and how this is an example of qiyas al-aks.

Core Idea
The same act can be good or evil depending on divine ruling, not inherent nature.

The Mu'tazila also cite a hadith where the Prophet says that placing one's desire in a lawful context brings reward, while placing it in an unlawful context brings sin. They claim this shows the act has inherent moral value. Sanusi explains that the hadith actually works against them: the act itself is neutral; its moral status depends entirely on whether it is done lawfully or unlawfully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the hadith in Arabic and its English translation. Note that the same act (sexual desire) is treated differently based on context.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite reasoning
The Mu'tazila argue that the hadith shows the act itself deserves reward or punishment, implying inherent moral value. See how they interpret it.
They think the act has an inherent quality that revelation confirms.
3
See Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi classifies this as qiyas al-aks (analogy of the opposite): the same act in two different rulings shows that the moral value is not intrinsic but depends on the divine ruling.
The act is neutral; only the legal context gives it moral value.
4
Reflect on the principle
Think about how this refutes the Mu'tazilite claim: if an act can be both good and evil, it cannot have a fixed rational judgment.

Source Text

The Mu'tazilite Proof from Quran 16:90 and Its Refutation
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite scriptural proof from Quran 16:90.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris refute the Mu'tazilite claim that Quran 16:90 proves actions are intrinsically good or evil before divine command.

Core Idea
The words 'commands' and 'prohibits' establish the ruling, not prior intrinsic values.

The Mu'tazilites often cite Quran 16:90 to argue that justice and goodness are known rationally before revelation. Sanusi here dissects their proof and shows that the verse actually supports the Ash'ari position: moral qualities are determined by the command, not prior to it. Let's examine this crucial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite proof
Read the passage describing how the Mu'tazilites interpret Quran 16:90 to claim that justice and goodness are established before the command.
Notice they think the verse implies an prior moral order.
2
Identify the Ash'ari counter-argument
Sanusi explains that the verbs 'commands' and 'prohibits' indicate that the ruling itself is established by the command—the qualities are not intrinsic but become good or evil by the command.
He also distinguishes this from customary good/evil.
3
Understand the distinction from customary good/evil
Sanusi clarifies that the verse is not about al-tahsin wa'l-taqbih al-'urfiyyan (customary good/evil), which is based on social norms, not rational necessity.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: If good and evil are not intrinsic, what does that imply about God's commands? This is a key point in Ash'ari theology.
It preserves God's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهة المعتزلة النصية في الحُسْن والقبح العقليين : نحو قوله سبحانه وتعالى عن سوء فهمهم : هو إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَنِ فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل الأمر ، وَيَنْهَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنكَرِ وَالْبَغْيِ ﴾ [النحل : ٩٠] فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل النهي ونحو حديث سيدنا معاذ رضي الله عنه الذي رواه مسلم ( ١٠٠٦ ) : أيأتي أحدنا شهوته ويكون له فيها أجر ؟! قال صلى الله عليه وسلم : « أرأيتُم لو وضعَها في حرام أكانَ عليهِ فيها وزرٌ ؟ فكذلك إذا وضعها في الحلالِ كَانَ لهُ أجر . وإنما الآية حُجَّةٌ عليهم ؛ إذ كلمتا ( يأمر ) و( ينهى ) دلتا على تأسيس الحكم ، وكأنَّ العدل والإحسان والفحشاء والمنكر والبغي في رتبة واحدة قبل الأمر والنهي ، ثم تفرّقتا بهما ، وهذا غير التحسين والتقبيح العرفيين ، وأما الحديث فهو من باب قياس العكس (۱) قال الإمام الرازي في الأربعين ) ( ١ / ٣٥٠ ) : ( اتفقت المعتزلة على أنَّ أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة برعاية مصالح العباد ، وهو اختيار أكثر المتأخرين من الفقهاء ، وهذا عندنا باطل )
And the scriptural proof (shubha) of the Mu'tazilites concerning rational good and evil (al-husn wa'l-qubh al-'aqliyyayn): such as His saying, Exalted and Majestic, in misinterpretation of their understanding: He [said?] 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and goodness' — so they claim that they [justice and goodness] are established before the command; 'and prohibits immorality, evil, and transgression' [Quran 16:90] — so they claim that they [immorality, evil, and transgression] are established before the prohibition. And similar to it is the hadith of our master Mu'adh, may Allah be pleased with him, which Muslim narrated (1006): 'Does any one of us come to his desire and have reward in it?' The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do you see that if he placed it in the unlawful, would he have sin in it? So likewise, if he places it in the lawful, he has reward.' But indeed the verse is a proof against them, since the two words 'commands' and 'prohibits' indicate the establishment of the ruling, and as if justice, goodness, immorality, evil, and transgression are in one rank before the command and prohibition, and then they become differentiated by them. And this is other than customary good and evil (al-tahsin wa'l-taqbih al-'urfiyyan). As for the hadith, it is from the chapter of qiyas al-'aks (inverse analogy). Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in (1/350): 'The Mu'tazilites have agreed that the actions of Allah and His rulings are caused by considerations of the interests of the servants, and this is the choice of most of the later jurists, and this is false in our view.'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite scriptural proof for rational good and evil (al-husn wa'l-qubh al-'aqliyyayn) and then refutes it from an Ash'ari perspective. The author describes the Mu'tazilite argument: they cite Quran 16:90, where Allah commands justice and goodness and prohibits immorality, evil, and transgression. The Mu'tazilites claim that these qualities (justice, goodness, etc.) must be intrinsically good or evil before the command or prohibition, because the command presumes the goodness of the commanded, and the prohibition presumes the evil of the prohibited. They also cite the hadith of Mu'adh where the Prophet indicates that lawful sexual relations can have reward, implying that the act itself has a moral quality before the ruling. The Ash'ari refutation is twofold. First, regarding the verse: the words 'commands' and 'prohibits' actually show that the ruling is established by the command/prohibition; the qualities are on the same level before the command, and differentiation occurs only by the command. This means that the good and evil are not intrinsic but determined by the Sharia. The author notes that this is different from customary good and evil (al-'urfiyyan), which are based on social norms, not rational necessity. Second, the hadith is explained as an example of qiyas al-'aks (inverse analogy): if an act in the unlawful has sin, then the same act in the lawful has reward — but this does not prove intrinsic moral value; it is a legal analogy, not a rational proof. The passage ends with a quote from Imam al-Razi confirming that the Mu'tazilite position that God's actions and rulings are motivated by considerations of the interests of creatures is false according to the Ash'aris.

Key Terms
شبهة المعتزلة
الحُسْن والقبح العقليين
الأمر والنهي
التحسين والتقبيح العرفيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quran 16:90 proves that actions are intrinsically good or evil before divine command.
Correction: The verse actually shows that the command itself establishes the moral quality; the actions are not intrinsically good or evil prior to the command.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari refutation in this passage, what do the words 'commands' and 'prohibits' in Quran 16:90 indicate?
A) That justice and goodness exist intrinsically before the command.
B) That the ruling is established by the command and prohibition, not that the qualities are intrinsic.
C) That God is obliged to command good and forbid evil.
D) That the Mu'tazilites are correct.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris argue that Quran 16:90 does not prove intrinsic moral values; rather, it shows that commands themselves establish moral rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quran 16:90, do you instinctively assume that justice is good because you feel it is, or because Allah declares it so? What does this lesson reveal about the source of your moral convictions? Commit to consciously affirming Allah's authority over every moral judgment today.
?
Is there any command of Allah that you find difficult to accept as good because it conflicts with your own sense of right and wrong? How does this lesson challenge you to submit your inner moral compass to divine command? Will you pray for humility to accept His rulings without reservation?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that actions have value independent of Allah's command. In what area of your life do you still act as if your own standards define good and evil? What would it take for you to let go of that attitude and fully embrace divine sovereignty over moral values?

The Hadith of Mu'adh and the Ash'ari Rebuttal
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite proof from the hadith of Mu'adh and quotes al-Razi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris refute the Mu'tazilite proof from the hadith of Mu'adh and the broader principle that God's actions are not motivated by created interests.

Core Idea
The hadith is an inverse analogy, not a proof of intrinsic moral value.

The Mu'tazilites also cite a hadith where the Prophet states that lawful sexual relations can have reward, implying an intrinsic moral quality. Sanusi explains that this is an example of qiyas al-'aks (inverse analogy) and therefore does not prove rational good/evil. He then seals the refutation with a quote from al-Razi rejecting the Mu'tazilite position that God's actions are caused by created interests.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the Mu'tazilite use
Read the hadith of Mu'adh and understand why the Mu'tazilites think it supports rational good/evil.
They argue that reward for a lawful act implies it is intrinsically good.
2
Understand qiyas al-'aks
Sanusi calls this 'inverse analogy'. Learn what that means and why it does not prove intrinsic value.
It is a legal reasoning tool, not a metaphysical proof.
3
Consider the Razi quote
Al-Razi affirms that the Mu'tazilite view that God's actions are motivated by created interests is false according to the Ash'aris.
This reinforces the Ash'ari position that morality is command-based.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Combine both proofs: the verse and the hadith. How does the Ash'ari refutation uphold the sovereignty of God's command?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهة المعتزلة النصية في الحُسْن والقبح العقليين : نحو قوله سبحانه وتعالى عن سوء فهمهم : هو إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَنِ فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل الأمر ، وَيَنْهَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنكَرِ وَالْبَغْيِ ﴾ [النحل : ٩٠] فأثبتهما بزعمهم قبل النهي ونحو حديث سيدنا معاذ رضي الله عنه الذي رواه مسلم ( ١٠٠٦ ) : أيأتي أحدنا شهوته ويكون له فيها أجر ؟! قال صلى الله عليه وسلم : « أرأيتُم لو وضعَها في حرام أكانَ عليهِ فيها وزرٌ ؟ فكذلك إذا وضعها في الحلالِ كَانَ لهُ أجر . وإنما الآية حُجَّةٌ عليهم ؛ إذ كلمتا ( يأمر ) و( ينهى ) دلتا على تأسيس الحكم ، وكأنَّ العدل والإحسان والفحشاء والمنكر والبغي في رتبة واحدة قبل الأمر والنهي ، ثم تفرّقتا بهما ، وهذا غير التحسين والتقبيح العرفيين ، وأما الحديث فهو من باب قياس العكس (۱) قال الإمام الرازي في الأربعين ) ( ١ / ٣٥٠ ) : ( اتفقت المعتزلة على أنَّ أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة برعاية مصالح العباد ، وهو اختيار أكثر المتأخرين من الفقهاء ، وهذا عندنا باطل )
And the scriptural proof (shubha) of the Mu'tazilites concerning rational good and evil (al-husn wa'l-qubh al-'aqliyyayn): such as His saying, Exalted and Majestic, in misinterpretation of their understanding: He [said?] 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and goodness' — so they claim that they [justice and goodness] are established before the command; 'and prohibits immorality, evil, and transgression' [Quran 16:90] — so they claim that they [immorality, evil, and transgression] are established before the prohibition. And similar to it is the hadith of our master Mu'adh, may Allah be pleased with him, which Muslim narrated (1006): 'Does any one of us come to his desire and have reward in it?' The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do you see that if he placed it in the unlawful, would he have sin in it? So likewise, if he places it in the lawful, he has reward.' But indeed the verse is a proof against them, since the two words 'commands' and 'prohibits' indicate the establishment of the ruling, and as if justice, goodness, immorality, evil, and transgression are in one rank before the command and prohibition, and then they become differentiated by them. And this is other than customary good and evil (al-tahsin wa'l-taqbih al-'urfiyyan). As for the hadith, it is from the chapter of qiyas al-'aks (inverse analogy). Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in (1/350): 'The Mu'tazilites have agreed that the actions of Allah and His rulings are caused by considerations of the interests of the servants, and this is the choice of most of the later jurists, and this is false in our view.'

They also cite the hadith of Mu'adh where the Prophet indicates that lawful sexual relations can have reward, implying that the act itself has a moral quality before the ruling. The Ash'ari refutation is twofold. ?

Rational Proof for Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi provides a rational proof that Allah must possess hearing, sight, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that proves Allah must possess hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
A living being cannot be devoid of perfections without having their opposites, so Allah being perfect cannot have defects.

Sanusi presents a powerful rational proof for three of Allah's attributes: hearing, sight, and speech. The argument is based on the nature of living beings and the impossibility of defects in the divine. By reasoning from the necessity of either an attribute or its opposite for any living being, Sanusi concludes that Allah, the perfect Living, must possess these perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, deduction, conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with a general principle about living beings. Identify that principle and how it applies to hearing, sight, and speech.
The premise is: every living being receptive to an attribute is not devoid of it or its like or its opposite.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the reasoning from the premise to the conclusion. Why must Allah have these attributes? Because lacking them would require their opposites, which are impossible for Him.
The conclusion is that Allah must possess hearing, sight, and speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويدلُّ على اتِّصافِهِ تعالى بهذهِ الثلاثة : العقل . . . ) إلى آخره ؛ يعني : أَنَّهُ يُستدَلُّ على ثبوتِ هذه الصفات الثلاث ـ وهي السمع والبصر والكلام - بالعقل ، والسمع وهو أولى
أما وجه الاستدلال عليها بالعقل : فهو أَنَّ كلَّ حيّ قابل لصفةٍ فَإِنَّهُ لا يخلو عنها أو عن مثلها أو عن ضدّها ، ولما وجدنا الحيَّ هو الذي يقبلُ الاتصاف بهذه الصفاتِ الثلاث ؛ بدليل امتناع اتصاف الميتِ بها .. وجبَ أنْ يكونَ المُصرِّحُ لقبولِها : إمَّا الحياةَ وإما أمراً يلازم الحياة ، وأياً ما كانَ يلزمُ عليهِ قبولُ اتِّصافِ كل حي بها ، فإذا لم يتّصف بها الحيُّ لزمَ أن يتّصفَ بأضدادِها ؛ وهي الصَّمَمُ والعمى والبَكَمُ ، لكنَّ هذهِ الأضداد في حقّهِ تعالى مستحيلة ؛ لكونها آفاتِ ونقائص ، وهو جلَّ وعزَّ مُنزَّه عنها بإجماع العقلاء ، فوجب أن يتّصفَ تعالى بتلك الكمالات
His saying: (And what indicates the description of the Most High with these three is reason...) to the end; means: that the establishment of these three attributes—hearing (al-samʿ), sight (al-baṣar), and speech (al-kalām)—is inferred by reason (al-ʿaql), and [inferred] by scripture (al-samʿ), the latter being superior. As for the way of inference by reason: it is that every living being that is receptive to an attribute is not devoid of it or of its like or of its opposite. And since we found that the living being is the one that accepts being described with these three attributes—as evidenced by the impossibility of a dead being being described with them—it becomes necessary that that which grants such acceptance is either life (al-ḥayāt) or something concomitant with life. Whichever it is, it necessarily follows that every living being can be described with them. So if the living being is not described with them, it must be described with their opposites, which are deafness (al-ṣamām), blindness (al-ʿamā), and muteness (al-bakam). But these opposites are impossible with respect to the Most High, because they are defects and deficiencies, and He, exalted and majestic, is transcendent above them by consensus of the rational people. Therefore it is necessary that the Most High be described with those perfections.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān aqlī) reasoning from the nature of living beings and the impossibility of defects for Allah. The argument proceeds as follows: every living being is such that for any attribute it can have, it either has that attribute, its like, or its opposite. Specifically, hearing, sight, and speech are attributes that only a living being can have—a dead being cannot have them. Therefore, the ground for their acceptance is either life itself or something inseparable from life. So every living being must be capable of having them. If a living being lacks them, it must have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness). Since Allah is the Living (al-Ḥayy), He cannot have these defective opposites, which are agreed to be imperfections. Therefore, He must possess the perfections of hearing, sight, and speech. Note that the text also mentions that scripture (al-samʿ) is a superior proof, but the rational proof is given first.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الكلام
الحياة
الصمم
العمى
البكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reason alone is insufficient to prove that Allah possesses hearing, sight, and speech.
Correction: This passage shows that a valid rational argument can be constructed based on the nature of living beings and the impossibility of defects in Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, if a living being lacks hearing, sight, and speech, what must it have?
A) Nothing; it simply lacks them.
B) Their opposites: deafness, blindness, muteness.
C) Some other unrelated attributes.
D) It must not be alive.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof shows that Allah must possess hearing, sight, and speech because a perfect living being cannot have their opposites, which are defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim Allah hears every word you utter—including whispers in darkness. But do you live as if He does? When you say something in private, what would you change right now if you truly believed He was listening? Commit today to speak in a way that honors His hearing.
?
If seeing Allah's perfection requires seeing your own defects, what defects are you avoiding? The proof says Allah cannot be blind, but you might be blind to your own faults. Name one area where you have been refusing to see the truth, and ask Allah to open your eyes today.
?
You affirm Allah speaks—through revelation. But when was the last time you let His speech change your actions? This is not just belief; it is accountability. Will you choose one verse from your next reading to implement immediately, or are you content to merely acknowledge His speech without letting it speak into your life?

The Kalima: Negation and Affirmation
Sanusi explains the composition of the kalima and the general meaning of negation and affirmation of divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'La ilaha illa Allah' is composed of negation and affirmation, and that it negates divinity and its properties from all besides God while affirming them exclusively for Him.

Core Idea
The kalima is a declaration of divine uniqueness encompassing all attributes.

Sanusi begins by explaining that the word of tawhid is built on two pillars: negation and affirmation. He then unpacks what is being negated and affirmed, showing that it is not just a simple statement but a comprehensive theological claim. This lesson will guide you through that foundational understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the repetition of 'except God' emphasising exclusivity.
2
Identify negation and affirmation
Identify the specific things that are negated from other than God and affirmed for Him. Make a list of each.
The negation covers eternity, necessity, and various transcendence attributes.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider what these negations and affirmations tell us about God's nature. How does this shape our understanding of tawhid?
These are not mere phrases but indicate a radical uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فكلمة التوحيد - وهي : ( لا إله إلا الله ) لمَّا كَانَتْ مُركَّبَةً مِنْ نفي وإثباتِ ، ولا شك أنَّ الذي نُفِيَ عن غيرِ مولانا جلَّ وعز ، وأُثبِتَ لهُ تعالى على سبيل الحصر هو الألوهية وخواصها ، حتى يتضمَّنُ قولنا : ( لا إله إلا الله ) معنى : لا قديم في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ إلا الله تعالى ، ولا واجبَ البقاء فيهما إلا الله جل وعلا ، ولا مخالف للحوادثِ كلّها إلا الله تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجِرْمٍ ولا قائم بالجِرْمِ ، ولا في جهةٍ مِنَ الجهات ولا له جهةٌ ، ولا يُكيَّفُ ولا يُتوهم ، ولا قائم بنفسِهِ - بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لا غني عن المحل والمخصّص - إلا الله جل وعزَّ ، ولا قادر على كل الممكناتِ بقدرةٍ قديمة إلا الله جل وعلا ، ولا عالم بما لا يتناهى مِنَ المعلوماتِ بعلمٍ واحدٍ قديم إلا الله جلَّ وعزّ ، وقِسٌ على هذا جميع ما عرفت وجوبَهُ لمولانا جل وعزَّ ، ولهذا قالوا : حقيقة التوحيد : اعتقاد عدم الشريكِ في الألوهية وخواصها .. كانَ اللائق في الترتيب أنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَوَّلاً ما يُعرَفُ به وجود إله العالَمِ ، ثم ما يليقُ أنْ يتّصفَ بِهِ ، ثم بعد ذلكَ يُبَيِّنُ أَنَّ الذي عُرفَ وجوبُ اتِّصافِهِ بتلك الصفاتِ يستحيل أن يكونَ مُتعدّداً ، بل لا يمكن أن يكون إلا واحداً ، فلهذا قدَّمنا نحنُ إثباتَ أُلوهِيَّةِ مولانا جل وعزَّ وإثبات خواصها ، ثم شرعنا في هذا الباب في بيانِ انفرادِهِ تعالى بذلكَ
And also: the word of tawhid – which is 'La ilaha illa Allah' – since it is composed of negation and affirmation, and there is no doubt that what is negated from other than our Master, Exalted and Majestic, and affirmed for Him exclusively, is al-uluhiyya (divinity) and its properties, such that our saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' includes the meaning: there is no eternal in essence and attributes except God, and no necessary of existence in them except God, and no one contrary to all accidents except God – meaning that He is not a body nor a substratum, nor in any direction, nor has a direction, nor can be qualified or imagined, nor is He self-subsistent in the sense of not needing a place and a specifier – except God, and no one powerful over all possibles with an eternal power except God, and no one knowing infinite things with a single eternal knowledge except God. And compare to this all that you have known to be obligatory for our Master. Therefore they said: the reality of tawhid is the belief in the absence of a partner in divinity and its properties. It was appropriate in the order that one first explain that by which the existence of the God of the world is known, then what is befitting for Him to be described with, then after that explain that He who is known to be necessarily described with those attributes cannot be multiple, but must be one. For this reason, we first presented the proof of the divinity of our Master and the proof of its properties, then we began in this chapter to explain His exclusivity in that.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah'. The author notes that it is composed of negation and affirmation. The negation removes al-uluhiyya (divinity) and its properties from anything other than God, while the affirmation restricts them exclusively to Him. The author then unpacks the implications: the kalima negates any other eternal being, any other necessary existent in essence and attributes, and any being that is not subject to accidents. It then explains God's transcendence: He is not a body, not a substratum, not in or having a direction, not qualifiable or imaginable, and not self-subsistent in the sense of needing a place and a specifier. The kalima also affirms that only God has eternal power over all possibles and eternal knowledge of all infinite things. The author concludes by stating that the proper order is to first prove God's existence, then His attributes, then His oneness, which is why they arranged the book accordingly.

Key Terms
توحيد
إله
ألوهية
نفي
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kalima only negates the existence of other gods.
Correction: The kalima negates all divine attributes from others and affirms them exclusively for God, including eternity, transcendence, power, and knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the kalima negate from other than God?
A) Only existence
B) Only power
C) Divinity and its properties
D) Only creation

Key Takeaway
The kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah' declares that all divine attributes are exclusive to God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly internalize that there is no eternal being besides Allah? How does this affect your reliance on things that will pass away?
?
If Allah is not a body, not in a direction, and cannot be imagined, how does this challenge the mental image you may have harbored when supplicating? Are you willing to let go of that image to worship Him as He truly is?
?
Only Allah has power over all possibles and knows all infinite things. In what area of your life are you still acting as if someone else has ultimate control? What step can you take today to transfer that trust exclusively to Allah?

The Reality of Tawhid and the Order of Proof
Sanusi defines the reality of tawhid and outlines the logical order of proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper order for establishing tawhid and the definition of its reality.

Core Idea
True tawhid begins with proving God's existence, then His attributes, then His oneness.

After explaining the content of the kalima, Sanusi turns to a meta-reflection: what is the proper sequence for teaching tawhid? He states that one should first prove the existence of God, then His attributes, then His oneness. This lesson covers that methodological insight and the definition of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the definition of tawhid and the three-step order.
The order is: existence → attributes → oneness.
2
Understand the three parts
Identify each of the three steps: what to establish first, second, third. Why is this order logical?
You cannot establish oneness without first knowing what attributes are being affirmed.
3
Apply to the broader text
Relate this methodological note to the overall structure of the book: the author first proved God and His attributes, and now in this chapter proves His oneness.
This shows the author follows his own recommended order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فكلمة التوحيد - وهي : ( لا إله إلا الله ) لمَّا كَانَتْ مُركَّبَةً مِنْ نفي وإثباتِ ، ولا شك أنَّ الذي نُفِيَ عن غيرِ مولانا جلَّ وعز ، وأُثبِتَ لهُ تعالى على سبيل الحصر هو الألوهية وخواصها ، حتى يتضمَّنُ قولنا : ( لا إله إلا الله ) معنى : لا قديم في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ إلا الله تعالى ، ولا واجبَ البقاء فيهما إلا الله جل وعلا ، ولا مخالف للحوادثِ كلّها إلا الله تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجِرْمٍ ولا قائم بالجِرْمِ ، ولا في جهةٍ مِنَ الجهات ولا له جهةٌ ، ولا يُكيَّفُ ولا يُتوهم ، ولا قائم بنفسِهِ - بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لا غني عن المحل والمخصّص - إلا الله جل وعزَّ ، ولا قادر على كل الممكناتِ بقدرةٍ قديمة إلا الله جل وعلا ، ولا عالم بما لا يتناهى مِنَ المعلوماتِ بعلمٍ واحدٍ قديم إلا الله جلَّ وعزّ ، وقِسٌ على هذا جميع ما عرفت وجوبَهُ لمولانا جل وعزَّ ، ولهذا قالوا : حقيقة التوحيد : اعتقاد عدم الشريكِ في الألوهية وخواصها .. كانَ اللائق في الترتيب أنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَوَّلاً ما يُعرَفُ به وجود إله العالَمِ ، ثم ما يليقُ أنْ يتّصفَ بِهِ ، ثم بعد ذلكَ يُبَيِّنُ أَنَّ الذي عُرفَ وجوبُ اتِّصافِهِ بتلك الصفاتِ يستحيل أن يكونَ مُتعدّداً ، بل لا يمكن أن يكون إلا واحداً ، فلهذا قدَّمنا نحنُ إثباتَ أُلوهِيَّةِ مولانا جل وعزَّ وإثبات خواصها ، ثم شرعنا في هذا الباب في بيانِ انفرادِهِ تعالى بذلكَ
And also: the word of tawhid – which is 'La ilaha illa Allah' – since it is composed of negation and affirmation, and there is no doubt that what is negated from other than our Master, Exalted and Majestic, and affirmed for Him exclusively, is al-uluhiyya (divinity) and its properties, such that our saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' includes the meaning: there is no eternal in essence and attributes except God, and no necessary of existence in them except God, and no one contrary to all accidents except God – meaning that He is not a body nor a substratum, nor in any direction, nor has a direction, nor can be qualified or imagined, nor is He self-subsistent in the sense of not needing a place and a specifier – except God, and no one powerful over all possibles with an eternal power except God, and no one knowing infinite things with a single eternal knowledge except God. And compare to this all that you have known to be obligatory for our Master. Therefore they said: the reality of tawhid is the belief in the absence of a partner in divinity and its properties. It was appropriate in the order that one first explain that by which the existence of the God of the world is known, then what is befitting for Him to be described with, then after that explain that He who is known to be necessarily described with those attributes cannot be multiple, but must be one. For this reason, we first presented the proof of the divinity of our Master and the proof of its properties, then we began in this chapter to explain His exclusivity in that.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of the kalima 'La ilaha illa Allah'. The author notes that it is composed of negation and affirmation. The negation removes al-uluhiyya (divinity) and its properties from anything other than God, while the affirmation restricts them exclusively to Him. The author then unpacks the implications: the kalima negates any other eternal being, any other necessary existent in essence and attributes, and any being that is not subject to accidents. It then explains God's transcendence: He is not a body, not a substratum, not in or having a direction, not qualifiable or imaginable, and not self-subsistent in the sense of needing a place and a specifier. The kalima also affirms that only God has eternal power over all possibles and eternal knowledge of all infinite things. The author concludes by stating that the proper order is to first prove God's existence, then His attributes, then His oneness, which is why they arranged the book accordingly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid is simply saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' without any rational proof.
Correction: The reality of tawhid is a belief based on a logical sequence of proofs: existence, attributes, oneness.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what should be proven first in establishing tawhid?
A) God's oneness
B) God's existence
C) God's attributes
D) God's power

Key Takeaway
Tawhid is not merely a statement; it is a rationally grounded belief built on proving God's existence, attributes, and oneness in that order.

?
Sanusi emphasizes a logical order in proving tawhid. In your own journey of faith, are you following that order? If not, what step have you neglected? Commit to studying one aspect of God's existence or attributes this week to strengthen your tawhid.
